[L s=30.0 r=3.00 x=55 y=85 b=0 i=0 f=f7] Preface
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=134 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Basic epidemiology was originally written with a view to strengthening education,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=146 b=0 i=0 f=f5] training and research in the field of public health. Since the book was published in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=158 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1993, more than 50 000 copies have been printed, and it has been translated into
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=170 b=0 i=0 f=f5] more than 25 languages. A list of these languages and contact addresses of local
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=182 b=0 i=0 f=f5] publishers is available on request from WHO Press, World Health Organization, 1211
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=194 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Geneva 27, Switzerland.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=72 y=206 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Basic epidemiology starts with a definition of epidemiology, introduces the his-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=218 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tory of modern epidemiology, and provides examples of the uses and applications of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=230 b=0 i=0 f=f5] epidemiology. Measurement of exposure and disease are covered in Chapter 2 and a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=242 b=0 i=0 f=f5] summary of the different types of study designs and their strengths and limitations
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=254 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is provided in Chapter 3. An introduction to statistical methods in Chapter 4 sets the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=266 b=0 i=0 f=f5] scene for understanding basic concepts and available tools for analysing data and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=278 b=0 i=0 f=f5] evaluating the impact of interventions. A fundamental task of epidemiologists is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=290 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to understand the process of making causal judgements, and this is covered in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=302 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Chapter 5. The applications of epidemiology to broad areas of public health are cov-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=314 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ered in the following chapters: chronic noncommunicable disease (Chapter 6),
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=326 b=0 i=0 f=f5] communicable disease (Chapter 7), clinical epidemiology (Chapter 8) and environ-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=338 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mental, occupational and injury epidemiology (Chapter 9); the process of health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=350 b=0 i=0 f=f5] planning is outlined in Chapter 10. The final chapter, Chapter 11, introduces the steps
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=362 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that new epidemiologists can take to further their education and provides links to a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=374 b=0 i=0 f=f5] number of current courses in epidemiology and public health.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=386 b=0 i=0 f=f5] As with the first edition of Basic epidemiology, examples are drawn from different
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=398 b=0 i=0 f=f5] countries to illustrate various epidemiological concepts. These are by no means ex-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=410 b=0 i=0 f=f5] haustive or comprehensive and we encourage students and teachers to seek locally
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=422 b=0 i=0 f=f5] relevant examples. Each chapter starts with a few key messages and ends with a series
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=434 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of short questions (answers are provided) to stimulate discussion and review progress.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=446 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The authors gratefully acknowledge contributions to the first edition from
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=458 b=0 i=0 f=f5] John Last and Anthony McMichael. Martha Anker wrote Chapter 4 for the first
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=470 b=0 i=0 f=f5] edition. In the second edition, Chapter 4 was written by Professor O. Dale Williams.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=482 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A version of the course material upon which this chapter is based is available at
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=494 b=0 i=0 f=f5] http://statcourse.dopm.uab.edu. A number of corrections to the equations in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=506 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Chapter 4 have been included in the second printing of this edition.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=518 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In addition, the authors would like to thank the following people for their
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=56 y=530 b=0 i=0 f=f5] contributions to the second edition: Michael Baker, Diarmid Campbell-Lendrum,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=56 y=542 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Carlos Corvalen, Bob Cummings, Tevfik Dorak, Olivier Dupperex, Fiona Gore, Alec
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=56 y=554 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Irwin, Rodney Jackson, Mary Kay Kindhauser, Doris Ma Fat, Colin Mathers, Hoomen
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=56 y=566 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Momen, Neal Pearce, Rudolpho Saracci, Abha Saxena, Kate Strong, Kwok-Cho Tang,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=56 y=578 b=0 i=0 f=f5] José Tapia and Hanna Tolonen. Laragh Gollogly was managing editor, and graphic
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=56 y=590 b=0 i=0 f=f5] design was done by Sophie Guetanah-Aguettants and Christophe Grangier.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=602 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The International Programme on Chemical Safety (a joint programme of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=614 b=0 i=0 f=f5] United Nations Environment Programme, the International Labour Organization, and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=626 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the World Health Organization), the Swedish International Development Authority

[L s=9.0 r=0.95 x=407 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Introduction xi
[L s=30.0 r=3.16 x=55 y=85 b=0 i=0 f=f7] Introduction
[L s=10.0 r=1.05 x=55 y=134 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The essential role of epidemiology is to improve the health of populations. This text-
[L s=10.0 r=1.05 x=55 y=146 b=0 i=0 f=f5] book provides an introduction to the basic principles and methods of epidemiology.
[L s=10.0 r=1.05 x=55 y=158 b=0 i=0 f=f5] It is intended for a wide audience, and to be used as training material for professionals
[L s=10.0 r=1.05 x=55 y=170 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in the health and environment fields.
[L s=10.0 r=1.05 x=73 y=194 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The purpose of this book is to:
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=73 y=216 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) explain the principles of disease causation with particular emphasis on modifiable
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=81 y=228 b=0 i=0 f=f5] environmental factors, including environmentally-determined behaviours,
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=73 y=239 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) encourage the application of epidemiology to the prevention of disease and the
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=81 y=251 b=0 i=0 f=f5] promotion of health,
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=73 y=262 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) prepare members of the health-related professions for the need for health services
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=81 y=274 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to address all aspects of the health of populations, and to ensure that health re-
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=81 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sources are used to the best possible effect, and
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=73 y=297 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) encourage good clinical practice by introducing the concepts of clinical
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=81 y=308 b=0 i=0 f=f5] epidemiology.
[L s=10.0 r=1.05 x=55 y=330 b=0 i=0 f=f5] At the end of the course the student should be able to demonstrate knowledge of:
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=73 y=352 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) the nature and uses of epidemiology
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=73 y=364 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) the epidemiological approach to defining and measuring the occurrence of health-
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=81 y=375 b=0 i=0 f=f5] related states in populations
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=73 y=387 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) the strengths and limitations of epidemiological study designs
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=73 y=399 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) the epidemiological approach to causation
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=73 y=411 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) the contribution of epidemiology to the prevention of disease, the promotion of
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=81 y=422 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health and the development of health policy
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=73 y=434 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) the contribution of epidemiology to good clinical practice and
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=73 y=446 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) the role of epidemiology in evaluating the effectiveness and efficiency of health
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=81 y=457 b=0 i=0 f=f5] care.
[L s=10.0 r=1.05 x=55 y=479 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In addition, the student will be expected to have gained a variety of skills, including
[L s=10.0 r=1.05 x=55 y=491 b=0 i=0 f=f5] an ability to:
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=73 y=513 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) describe the common causes of death, disease and disability in her or his
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=81 y=524 b=0 i=0 f=f5] community
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=73 y=536 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) outline appropriate study designs to answer specific questions concerning disease
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=81 y=547 b=0 i=0 f=f5] causation, natural history, prognosis, prevention, and the evaluation of therapy
[L s=9.5 r=1.00 x=81 y=559 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and other interventions to prevent and control disease.

[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] xii Introduction

[PAGE 14]
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=470 y=53 b=1 i=1 f=f8] 1
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Chapter 1
[L s=32.0 r=3.20 x=55 y=101 b=0 i=0 f=f7] What is epidemiology?
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=168 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Key messages
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=180 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Epidemiology is a fundamental science of public health.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=191 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Epidemiology has made major contributions to improving population health.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=203 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Epidemiology is essential to the process of identifying and mapping emerging
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=217 b=0 i=0 f=f5] diseases.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=226 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • There is often a frustrating delay between acquiring epidemiological evidence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=240 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and applying this evidence to health policy.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=268 b=0 i=0 f=f9] The historical context
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=300 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Origins
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=327 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemiology originates from Hippocrates’ observation more than 2000 years ago that
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=340 b=0 i=0 f=f5] environmental factors influence the occurrence of disease. However, it was not until
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=353 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the nineteenth century that the distribution of disease in specific human population
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=366 b=0 i=0 f=f5] groups was measured to any large extent. This work marked not only the formal
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=379 b=0 i=0 f=f5] beginnings of epidemiology but also some of its most spectacular achievements.1 The
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=392 b=0 i=0 f=f5] finding by John Snow (Box 1.1) that the risk of cholera in London was related to the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=405 b=0 i=0 f=f5] drinking of water supplied by a particular company provides a well-known example;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=418 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the map (see Figure 4.1) highlights the clustering of cases. Snow’s epidemiological
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=431 b=0 i=0 f=f5] studies were one aspect of a wide-ranging series of investigations that examined
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=444 b=0 i=0 f=f5] related physical, chemical, biological, sociological and political processes.2
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=457 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Comparing rates of disease in subgroups of the human population became com-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=470 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mon practice in the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries. This approach was
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=483 b=0 i=0 f=f5] initially applied to the control of communicable diseases (see Chapter 7), but proved
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=496 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to be a useful way of linking environmental conditions or agents to specific diseases.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=509 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In the second half of the twentieth century, these methods were applied to chronic
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=522 b=0 i=0 f=f5] noncommunicable diseases such as heart disease and cancer, especially in middle-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=535 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and high-income countries.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=559 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Recent developments in epidemiology
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=587 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemiology in its modern form is a relatively new discipline1 and uses quantitative
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=600 b=0 i=0 f=f5] methods to study diseases in human populations to inform prevention and control
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=613 b=0 i=0 f=f5] efforts. For example, Richard Doll and Andrew Hill studied the relationship between
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=626 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tobacco use and lung cancer, beginning in the 1950s.4 Their work was preceded by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=639 b=0 i=0 f=f5] experimental studies on the carcinogenicity of tobacco tars and by clinical observa-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=652 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tions linking tobacco use and other possible factors to lung cancer. By using long-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=665 b=0 i=0 f=f5] term cohort studies, they were able to establish the association between smoking
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=678 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and lung cancer (Figure 1.1).

[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 2 Chapter 1
[L s=8.0 r=0.89 x=147 y=92 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Box 1.1. Early epidemiological observation
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=107 b=0 i=0 f=f5] John Snow located the home of each person who died from cholera in London during
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=118 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1848–49 and 1853–54, and noted an apparent association between the source of drinking-
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=129 b=0 i=0 f=f5] water and the deaths.3 He compared cholera deaths in districts with different water supplies
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=140 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (Table 1.1) and showed that both the number of deaths and the rate of deaths were higher
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=151 b=0 i=0 f=f5] among people supplied water by the Southwark company. On the basis of his meticulous
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] research, Snow constructed a theory about the communication of infectious diseases and
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=173 b=0 i=0 f=f5] suggested that cholera was spread by contaminated water. He was able to encourage
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=184 b=0 i=0 f=f5] improvements in the water supply long before the discovery of the organism responsible
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=195 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for cholera; his research had a direct and far-reaching impact on public policy.
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=160 y=206 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Snow’s work reminds us that public health measures, such as the improvement of
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=217 b=0 i=0 f=f5] water supplies and sanitation, have made enormous contributions to the health of popu-
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=228 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lations, and that in many cases since 1850, epidemiological studies have identified the
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=239 b=0 i=0 f=f5] appropriate measures to take. It is noteworthy, however, that outbreaks of cholera are still
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=250 b=0 i=0 f=f5] frequent among poor populations, especially in developing countries. In 2006, Angola
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=261 b=0 i=0 f=f5] reported 40 000 cholera cases and 1600 deaths; Sudan reported 13 852 cases resulting in
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=272 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 516 deaths in the first few months of the year.
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=158 y=305 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The British doctors’ cohort has also shown a progressive decrease in death rates
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=140 y=318 b=0 i=0 f=f5] among non-smokers over subsequent decades. Male doctors born between 1900–
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=140 y=331 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1930 who smoked cigarettes died, on average, about 10 years younger than lifelong
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=140 y=344 b=0 i=0 f=f5] non-smokers5 (Figure 1.2).
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=257 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] S moking is a particularly clear-cut case, but for most
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=240 y=370 b=0 i=0 f=f5] diseases, several factors contribute to causation. Some

[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=27 y=383 b=0 i=0 f=f5] supplied by two water companies,3 8 July to 26 factors are essential for the development of a disease and
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=27 y=396 b=0 i=0 f=f5] August 1854 some increase the risk of developing disease. New epi-

[L s=20.0 r=2.23 x=140 y=575 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Definition, scope, and uses of epidemiology
[L s=14.0 r=1.56 x=140 y=607 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Definition
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=140 y=634 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemiology as defined by Last9 is “the study of the distribution and determinants
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=140 y=648 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of health-related states or events in specified populations, and the application of this
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=140 y=661 b=0 i=0 f=f5] study to the prevention and control of health problems” (see Box 1.2). Epidemiolo-
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=140 y=674 b=0 i=0 f=f5] gists are concerned not only with death, illness and disability, but also with more

[PAGE 16]
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=55 y=335 b=0 i=0 f=f5] positive health states and, most importantly, with the means to improve health. The
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=55 y=348 b=0 i=0 f=f5] term “disease” encompasses all unfavourable health changes, including injuries and
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=55 y=361 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mental health.
[L s=14.0 r=1.75 x=55 y=385 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Scope
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=55 y=412 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A focus of an epidemiological study is the population defined in geographical or other
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=55 y=425 b=0 i=0 f=f5] terms; for example, a specific group of hospital patients or factory workers could be
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=55 y=438 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the unit of study. A common population used in epidemiology is one selected from
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=55 y=451 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a specific area or country at a specific time. This forms the base for defining subgroups
[L s=8.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=483 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Figure 1.2. Survival from age 35 for continuing cigarette smokers and lifelong non-

[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=34 y=145 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Box 1.2. Definition of epidemiology9
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=161 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The word “epidemiology” is derived from the Greek words: epi “upon”, demos “people” and logos “study”.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=46 y=172 b=0 i=0 f=f5] This broad definition of epidemiology can be further elaborated as follows:
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=34 y=196 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Term Explanation
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=34 y=212 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Study includes: surveillance, observation, hypothesis testing, analytic research
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=173 y=221 b=0 i=0 f=f14] and experiments.
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=34 y=233 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Distribution refers to analysis of: times, persons, places and classes of people affected.
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=34 y=245 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Determinants include factors that influence health: biological, chemical, physical, social,
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=173 y=254 b=0 i=0 f=f14] cultural, economic, genetic and behavioural.
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=34 y=266 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Health-related states and events refer to: diseases, causes of death, behaviours such as use of tobacco,
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=173 y=275 b=0 i=0 f=f14] positive health states, reactions to preventive regimes and provision and
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=173 y=284 b=0 i=0 f=f14] use of health services.
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=34 y=296 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Specified populations include those with identifiable characteristics, such as occupational groups.
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=34 y=308 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Application to prevention and control the aims of public health—to promote, protect, and restore health.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=140 y=341 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Epidemiology and public health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=374 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Public health, broadly speaking, refers to collective actions to improve population
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=387 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health.1 Epidemiology, one of the tools for improving public health, is used in several
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=400 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ways (Figures 1.3–1.6). Early studies in epidemiology were concerned with the causes
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=413 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (etiology) of communicable diseases, and such work continues to be essential since
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=426 b=0 i=0 f=f5] it can lead to the identification of preventive methods. In this sense, epidemiology is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=439 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a basic medical science with the goal of improving the health of populations, and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=452 b=0 i=0 f=f5] especially the health of the disadvantaged.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=476 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Causation of disease
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=504 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Although some diseases are caused solely by genetic factors, most result from an
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=517 b=0 i=0 f=f5] interaction between genetic and environmental factors. Diabetes, for example, has
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=530 b=0 i=0 f=f5] both genetic and environmental components. We define environment broadly to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=543 b=0 i=0 f=f5] include any biological, chemical, physical, psychological, economic or cultural factors
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=240 y=556 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that can affect health (see Chapter 9). Personal behaviours
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=240 y=569 b=0 i=0 f=f5] affect this interplay, and epidemiology is used to study
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=27 y=573 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Figure 1.3. Causation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=240 y=582 b=0 i=0 f=f5] their influence and the effects of preventive interventions
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=41 y=586 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Genetic factors
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=240 y=595 b=0 i=0 f=f5] through health promotion (Figure 1.3).

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Applying epidemiological principles and methods to problems encountered in

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] macoepidemiology, molecular epidemiology, and genetic epidemiology (Box 1.3).11
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=147 y=158 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Box 1.3. Molecular and genetic epidemiology
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=174 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Molecular epidemiology measures exposure to specific substances and early biological
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=185 b=0 i=0 f=f5] response, by:
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=197 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •evaluating host characteristics mediating response to external agents, and
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=209 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •using biochemical markers of a specific effect to refine disease categories.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=225 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Genetic epidemiology deals with the etiology, distribution, and control of disease in groups
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=236 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of relatives, and with inherited causes of disease in populations.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=247 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Genetic epidemiological research in family or population studies aims to establish:
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=259 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •a genetic component to the disorder,
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=271 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •the relative size of that genetic effect in relation to other sources of variation in disease
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=166 y=284 b=0 i=0 f=f5] risk, and
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=294 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •the responsible gene(s).
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=310 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Public health genetics include:
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=322 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •population screening programs,
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=334 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •organizing and evaluating services for patients with genetic disorders, and
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=345 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •the impact of genetics on medical practice.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=140 y=382 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Achievements in epidemiology
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=414 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Smallpox
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=442 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The elimination of smallpox contributed greatly to the health and well-being of mil-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=455 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lions of people, particularly in many of the poorest populations. Smallpox illustrates
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=468 b=0 i=0 f=f5] both the achievements and frustrations of modern public health. In the 1790s it was
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=481 b=0 i=0 f=f5] shown that cowpox infection conferred protection against the smallpox virus, yet it
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=494 b=0 i=0 f=f5] took almost 200 years for the benefits of this discovery to be accepted and applied
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=507 b=0 i=0 f=f5] throughout the world.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=520 b=0 i=0 f=f5] An intensive campaign to eliminate smallpox was coordinated over many years
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=533 b=0 i=0 f=f5] by the World Health Organization (WHO). An understanding of the epidemiology
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=546 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of smallpox was central to its eradication, in particular, by:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=568 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • providing information about the distribution of cases and the model, mecha-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=580 b=0 i=0 f=f5] nisms and levels of transmission;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=591 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • mapping outbreaks of the disease;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=603 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • evaluating control measures (Box 1.4).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=626 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The fact that there was no animal host was of critical importance together with the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=639 b=0 i=0 f=f5] low average number of secondary cases infected by a primary case.

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=576 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Rheumatic fever and rheumatic heart disease
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=604 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Rheumatic fever and rheumatic heart disease are associated with poverty, and in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=617 b=0 i=0 f=f5] particular, with poor housing and overcrowding, both of which favour the spread of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=630 b=0 i=0 f=f5] streptococcal upper respiratory tract infections. In many affluent countries, the decline
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=643 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in rheumatic fever started at the beginning of the twentieth century, long before the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=656 b=0 i=0 f=f5] introduction of effective drugs such as sulfonamides and penicillin (Figure 1.7). Today
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=669 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the disease has almost disappeared from most high-income countries although

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=447 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Iodine deficiency diseases
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=475 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Iodine deficiency, which occurs commonly in certain mountainous regions, causes
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=488 b=0 i=0 f=f5] loss of physical and mental energy associated with inadequate production of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=501 b=0 i=0 f=f5] iodine-containing thyroid hormone.18 Goitre and cretinism were first described in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=514 b=0 i=0 f=f5] detail some 400 years ago, but it was not until the twentieth century that sufficient
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=527 b=0 i=0 f=f5] knowledge was acquired to permit effective prevention and control. In 1915, endemic
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=540 b=0 i=0 f=f5] goitre was named as the easiest known disease to prevent, and use of iodized salt for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=553 b=0 i=0 f=f5] goitre control was proposed the same year in Switzerland.18 The first large-scale trials
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=566 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with iodine were done shortly afterwards in Ohio, USA, on 5000 girls aged between
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=579 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 11 and 18 years. The prophylactic and therapeutic effects were impressive and iodized
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=592 b=0 i=0 f=f5] salt was introduced on a community scale in many countries in 1924.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=605 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The use of iodized salt is effective because salt is used by all classes of society
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=618 b=0 i=0 f=f5] at roughly the same level throughout the year. Success depends on the effective
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=631 b=0 i=0 f=f5] production and distribution of the salt and requires legislative enforcement, quality
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=644 b=0 i=0 f=f5] control and public awareness (Box 1.6).

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=473 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Hip fractures
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=501 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemiological research on injuries often involves collaboration between scientists
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=514 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in epidemiology and in the social and environmental health fields. Injuries related to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=527 b=0 i=0 f=f5] falls – particularly fractures of the neck of the femur (hip fractures) in older people –
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=540 b=0 i=0 f=f5] have attracted a great deal of attention in recent years because of the implications for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=553 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the health service needs of an ageing population. Hip fractures increase exponentially
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=566 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with age as the result of age-related decreased bone mass at the proximal femur and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=579 b=0 i=0 f=f5] an age-related increase in falls. With the rising number of elderly individuals in most
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=592 b=0 i=0 f=f5] populations, the incidence of hip fracture can be expected to increase proportionately
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=605 b=0 i=0 f=f5] if efforts are not directed towards prevention.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=618 b=0 i=0 f=f5] As hip fractures account for a large number of days spent in hospital, the eco-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=631 b=0 i=0 f=f5] nomic costs associated with hip fracture are considerable.23, 24 In a study of cost of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=644 b=0 i=0 f=f5] injuries in the Netherlands, hip fracture – which ranked only fourteenth of 25 listed
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=657 b=0 i=0 f=f5] injuries in terms of incidence – was the leading injury diagnosis in terms of costs,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=670 b=0 i=0 f=f5] accounting for 20% of all costs associated with injury.

[PAGE 23]
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Most hip fractures are the result of a fall, and most deaths associated with falls
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in elderly people result from the complications of hip fractures.25 The optimal strate-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] gies to prevent hip fractures are unclear. Epidemiologists have a vital role in examining
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] both modifiable and non-modifiable factors in an effort to reduce the burden of hip
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] fractures.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=160 b=0 i=0 f=f10] HIV/AIDS
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=188 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The acquired immunodeficiency syndrome (AIDS) was first identified as a distinct
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease entity in 1981 in the USA.26 By 1990, there were an estimated 10 million
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f5] people infected with the human immunodeficiency virus (HIV). Since then, 25 million
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=227 b=0 i=0 f=f5] people have died of AIDS and a further 40 million have been infected with HIV27
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=240 b=0 i=0 f=f5] making it one of the most destructive infectious disease epidemics in recorded history
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=253 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (Figure 1.8).28

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 10 Chapter 1
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=452 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Of the 3.1 million deaths from AIDS in 2005, approximately 95% occurred in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=465 b=0 i=0 f=f5] low- and middle-income countries, with 70% occurring in sub-Saharan Africa and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=478 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 20% in Asia.27 Most of the 4.3–6.6 million people newly infected with HIV in 2005
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=491 b=0 i=0 f=f5] live in these regions. However, within regions or countries themselves, levels of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=504 b=0 i=0 f=f5] infection and routes of transmission vary considerably (Box 1.7).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=517 b=0 i=0 f=f5] AIDS has a long incubation period and, without treatment, about half of those
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=530 b=0 i=0 f=f5] infected with the causative human immunodeficiency virus (HIV) develop AIDS
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=543 b=0 i=0 f=f5] within nine years of infection (see Chapter 7). The virus is found in blood, semen
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=556 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and cervical or vaginal secretions. Transmission occurs mainly through sexual inter-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=569 b=0 i=0 f=f5] course or sharing of contaminated needles, but the virus can also be transmitted
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=582 b=0 i=0 f=f5] through transfusion of contaminated blood or blood products, and from an infected
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=595 b=0 i=0 f=f5] woman to her baby during pregnancy, at birth or through breastfeeding.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=619 b=0 i=0 f=f10] SARS
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=647 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Although minor from the perspectives of mortality or burden of disease, the outbreak
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=141 y=660 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of severe acute respiratory syndrome (SARS) reminded the world of the shared vul-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=673 b=0 i=0 f=f5] nerability to new infections.30, 31 It also highlighted the weakened state of essential

[PAGE 24]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=370 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] What is epidemiology? 11
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] public health services, not only in Asia but also in high-income countries such as
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Canada. SARS first appeared in November 2002 in southern China with two patients
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with atypical pneumonia of unknown cause. The spread – facilitated by air travel of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] highly infectious people – was rapid over the following months, causing more than
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 8000 cases and approximately 900 deaths in 12 countries.31 Death rates were lower
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in places where SARS was acquired in the community and higher in hospitals, where
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health workers had close or repeated contact with infected people.30
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=62 y=197 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Box 1.7. HIV, epidemiology, and prevention
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemiological and sociological studies have played a vital role in identifying the epidemic,
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=224 b=0 i=0 f=f5] determining the pattern of its spread, identifying risk factors and social determinants, and
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=235 b=0 i=0 f=f5] evaluating interventions for prevention, treatment and control. The screening of donated
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=246 b=0 i=0 f=f5] blood, the promotion of safe sexual practices, the treatment of other sexually transmitted
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=257 b=0 i=0 f=f5] infections, the avoidance of needle-sharing and the prevention of mother-to-child trans-
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=268 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mission with antiretrovirals are the main ways of controlling the spread of HIV/AIDS. With
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=279 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the development of new antiretroviral drugs given in combination, the lives of people with
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=290 b=0 i=0 f=f5] HIV living in high-income countries have been prolonged and improved. The cost of these
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=301 b=0 i=0 f=f5] drugs, however, severely limits their use, and they are currently unavailable to most infected
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=312 b=0 i=0 f=f5] people. A major international effort to scale up treatment of HIV/AIDS – the “3 × 5 cam-
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=323 b=0 i=0 f=f5] paign” (3 million people on treatment by the end of 2005),29 – managed to get 1 million
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=334 b=0 i=0 f=f5] people on treatment, averting between 250 000 and 350 000 deaths. The next global goal
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=345 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is for universal access to treatment by 2010. Epidemiology has made a major contribution
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=356 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to understanding the AIDS pandemic; however knowledge alone is no guarantee that the
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=367 b=0 i=0 f=f5] appropriate preventive actions will be taken.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=399 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Important lessons have been learnt from the experience of responding to the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=412 b=0 i=0 f=f5] SARS epidemic. For example, SARS has demonstrated that such epidemics can have
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=425 b=0 i=0 f=f5] significant economic and social consequences that go well beyond the impact on
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=439 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health.32 Such effects show the importance that a severe new disease could assume
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=452 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in a closely interdependent and highly mobile world.30

[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=85 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Chapter 2
[L s=31.9 r=3.20 x=55 y=102 b=0 i=0 f=f7] Measuring health and
[L s=31.9 r=3.20 x=55 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f7] disease
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=168 y=184 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Key messages
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • The measure of health and disease is fundamental to the practice of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=228 b=0 i=0 f=f5] epidemiology.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=237 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • A variety of measures are used to characterize the overall health of populations.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=249 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Population health status is not fully measured in many parts of the world,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=262 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and this lack of information poses a major challengefor epidemiologists.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=290 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Defining health and disease
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=322 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Definitions
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=350 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The most ambitious definition of health is that proposed by WHO in 1948: “health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=363 b=0 i=0 f=f5] isa state of complete physical, mental, and social well-being and not merely the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=376 b=0 i=0 f=f5] absence of disease or infirmityy.”1 This definition – criticized because of the difficulty
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=389 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in defining and measuring well-being – remains an ideal.The World Health Assembly
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=402 b=0 i=0 f=f5] resolved in 1977 that all people should attain a level of health permitting
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=415 b=0 i=0 f=f5] them to lead socially and economically productive lives bythe year 2000. This commit-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=428 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ment to the “health-for-all”strategy was renewed in 1998 and again in 2003.2
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=441 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Practical definitions of health and disease are needed in epidemiology, which
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=454 b=0 i=0 f=f5] concentrates on aspects of health that are easily measurable and amenable to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=467 b=0 i=0 f=f5] improvement.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=480 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Definitions of health states used byepidemiologists tend to be simple, for ex-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=493 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ample, “disease present” or “disease absent” (see Box 2.1). The development of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=506 b=0 i=0 f=f5] criteria to establish the presence of a disease requires a definition of “normality” and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=518 b=0 i=0 f=f5] “abnormality.” However, it may be difficult to define what is normal, and there is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=531 b=0 i=0 f=f5] often no clear distinction between normal and abnormal, especially with regard to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=544 b=0 i=0 f=f5] normally distributed continuous variables that may be associated with several dis-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=557 b=0 i=0 f=f5] eases (see Chapter 8).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=570 b=0 i=0 f=f5] For example, guidelines about cut-off points for treating high blood pressure are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=583 b=0 i=0 f=f5] arbitrary, as there is a continuous increase in risk of cardiovascular disease at every
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=596 b=0 i=0 f=f5] level (see Chapter 6). A specific cut-off point for an abnormal value is based on an
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=609 b=0 i=0 f=f5] operational definitionn and not on any absolute threshold. Similar considerations apply
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=622 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to criteria for exposure to health hazards: for example, the guideline for a safee blood
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=635 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lead level would be based on judgment of the available evidence, which is likely to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=648 b=0 i=0 f=f5] change over time (see Chapter 9).

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Diagnostic criteria
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=112 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Diagnostic criteria are usually based on symptoms, signs, history and test results. For
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=125 b=0 i=0 f=f5] example, hepatitis can be identified by the presence of antibodies in the blood;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=138 b=0 i=0 f=f5] asbestosis can be identified by symptoms and signs of specific changes in lung func-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=151 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tion, radiographic demonstration of fibrosis of the lung tissue or pleural thickening,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=164 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and a history of exposure to asbestos fibres. Table 2.1 shows that the diagnosis of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=177 b=0 i=0 f=f5] rheumatic fever diagnosis can be made based on several manifestations of the disease,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=190 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with some signs being more important than others.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=256 y=203 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In some situations very simple criteria are justified.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=239 y=216 b=0 i=0 f=f5] For example, the reduction of mortality due to bacterial

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=85 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Diagnostic criteria may change quite rapidly as knowledge increases or diagnostic
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=98 b=0 i=0 f=f5] techniques improve; they also often change according to the context in which they
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=111 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are being used. For example, the original WHO diagnostic criteria for myocardial
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=124 b=0 i=0 f=f5] infarction for use in epidemiological studies, were modified when an objective method
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for assessing electrocardiograms (the Minnesota Code) was introduced in the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1980s.8, 9 The criteria were further modified in the 1990s, when it became possible to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=163 b=0 i=0 f=f5] measure cardiac enzymes.10
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=185 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Measuring disease frequency
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=217 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Several measures of disease frequency are based on the concepts of prevalence and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=230 b=0 i=0 f=f5] incidence. Unfortunately, epidemiologists havenot yet reached complete agreement
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=243 b=0 i=0 f=f5] on the definitions of terms used in this field. In this text we generally use the terms
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=256 b=0 i=0 f=f5] as defined in Last’sDictionary of Epidemiology.11
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=280 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Population at risk
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=308 b=0 i=0 f=f5] An important factor in calculating measures of disease frequency is the correct esti-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=321 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mate of the numbers of people under study. Ideally these numbers should only include
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=334 b=0 i=0 f=f5] people who are potentially susceptible to the diseases being studied. For instance,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=347 b=0 i=0 f=f5] men should not be included when calculating the frequency of cervical cancer
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=360 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (Figure 2.1).
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=55 y=389 b=1 i=0 f=f4] Figure 2.1. Population at risk in a study of carcinoma of the cervix
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=100 y=402 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Total population All women Population at risk
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=207 y=412 b=0 i=0 f=f13] (age groups)
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=219 y=444 b=0 i=0 f=f13] 0—24
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=219 y=454 b=0 i=0 f=f13] years
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=86 y=483 b=0 i=0 f=f13] All men All women 25—69 years 25—69 years
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=219 y=512 b=0 i=0 f=f13] 70+
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=219 y=522 b=0 i=0 f=f13] years
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=566 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The people who are susceptible to a given disease are called the population at
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=579 b=0 i=0 f=f5] risk, and can be defined by demographic,geographic or environmental factors. For
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=592 b=0 i=0 f=f5] instance, occupational injuries occur only among working people, so the population
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=605 b=0 i=0 f=f5] at risk is the workforce; in some countries brucellosis occurs only among people
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=618 b=0 i=0 f=f5] handling infected animals, so the population at risk consists of those working on
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=631 b=0 i=0 f=f5] farms and in slaughterhouses.

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Incidence and prevalence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=112 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The incidence of disease represents the rate of occurrence of new cases arising in a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=125 b=0 i=0 f=f5] given period in a specified population, while prevalence is the frequency of existing
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=138 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cases in a defined population at a given point in time. These are fundamentally dif-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=151 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ferent ways of measuring occurrence (see Table 2.2) and the relation between
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=164 b=0 i=0 f=f5] incidence and prevalence varies among diseases. There may be low incidence and a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=177 b=0 i=0 f=f5] high prevalence – as for diabetes – or a highincidence and a low prevalence – as for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=190 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the common cold. Colds occur more frequently than diabetes but last only a short
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=203 b=0 i=0 f=f5] time, whereas diabetes isessentially lifelong.
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=232 b=1 i=0 f=f4] Table 2.2. Differences between incidence and prevalence
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=192 y=254 b=1 i=0 f=f4] Incidence Prevalence

[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=192 y=280 b=0 i=0 f=f14] during a specified period of time at a given point of time
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=292 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Denominator Population at risk Population at risk
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=304 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Focus Whether the event is a new case Presence or absence of a disease

[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=320 y=322 b=0 i=0 f=f14] “snapshot” in time
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=334 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Uses Expresses the risk of becoming ill

[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=320 y=389 b=0 i=0 f=f14] health services
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=404 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Note: If incident cases are not resolved, but continue over time, then they become existing
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=413 b=0 i=0 f=f14] (prevalent) cases. In this sense, prevalence = incidence × duration.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=440 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Measuring prevalence and incidence involves the counting of cases in defined
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=453 b=0 i=0 f=f5] populations at risk. Reporting the number of cases without reference to the population
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=466 b=0 i=0 f=f5] at risk can be used to give an impression of theoverall magnitude of a health problem,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=478 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or of short-term trends in a population, for instance, during an epidemic. WHO’s
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=491 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Weekly Epidemiological Recordd contains incidence data in the form of case numbers,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=504 b=0 i=0 f=f5] which in spite of their crude nature, can give usefulinformation about the develop-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=517 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ment of epidemics of communicable diseases.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=530 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The term “attack rate” is often used instead of incidence during a disease out-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=543 b=0 i=0 f=f5] break in a narrowly-defined population over ashort period of time. The attack rate
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=556 b=0 i=0 f=f5] can be calculated as the number of peopleaffected divided by the number exposed.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=569 b=0 i=0 f=f5] For example, in the case of a foodborne disease outbreak, the attack rate can be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=582 b=0 i=0 f=f5] calculated for each type of foodeaten, and then these ratescompared to identify the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=595 b=0 i=0 f=f5] source of the infection.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=608 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Data on prevalence and incidence becomemuch more useful if converted into
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=621 b=0 i=0 f=f5] rates (see Table 1.1). A rate is calculated by dividing the number of cases by the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=634 b=0 i=0 f=f5] corresponding number of people in the population at risk and is expressed as cases
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=647 b=0 i=0 f=f5] per 10nnpeople. Some epidemiologists use the term “rate” only for measurements of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=660 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease occurrence per time unit (week, year, etc.). In this book, we use the term

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=345 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Measuring health and disease 19
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=85 b=0 i=0 f=f5] “disease” in its broad sense, including clinical disease, adverse biochemical and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=98 b=0 i=0 f=f5] physiological changes, injuries and mental illness.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=121 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Prevalence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=131 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Prevalence(P))of a disease is calculated as follows:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=117 y=154 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Numberofpeoplewiththediseaseorcondition
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=117 y=169 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ataspecifiedtime
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=93 y=175 b=0 i=0 f=f5] P= (×10n)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=114 y=187 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Numberofpeopleinthepopulationatriskatthe
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=114 y=202 b=0 i=0 f=f5] specifiedtime
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=225 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Data on the population at risk are not always available and in many studies the total
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=237 b=0 i=0 f=f5] population in the study areais used as an approximation.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=250 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Prevalence is often expressed as cases per 100 (percentage), or per 1000 popu-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=263 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lation. In this case, PPhas to be multipliedby the appropriate factor: 10n. If the data
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=276 b=0 i=0 f=f5] have been collected for one point in time, PPis the “point prevalence rate.” It is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=289 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sometimes more convenient to use the “period prevalence rate,” calculated as the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=302 b=0 i=0 f=f5] total number of cases at any time during a specified period, divided by the population
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=315 b=0 i=0 f=f5] at risk midway through the period. Similarly, a “lifetime prevalence” is the total num-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=328 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ber of persons known to have had the disease for at least some part of their lives.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=341 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Apart from age, several factors determine prevalence (Figure 2.2). In particular:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=363 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • the severity of illness (if many people who develop a diseasedie within a short
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=375 b=0 i=0 f=f5] time, its prevalence is decreased);
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=386 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • the duration of illness (if a disease lasts a short time its prevalence is lower
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=398 b=0 i=0 f=f5] than if it lasts a long time);
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=409 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • the number of new cases (if many people develop a disease, its prevalence is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=421 b=0 i=0 f=f5] higher than if few people do so).

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=412 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Incidence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=423 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Incidence refers to the rate at which new events occur in a population. Incidence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=436 b=0 i=0 f=f5] takes into account thevariable time periods during which individuals are disease-free
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=449 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and thus “at risk” of developingthe disease.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=462 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In the calculation of incidence, the numerator is the number of new events that
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=475 b=0 i=0 f=f5] occur in a defined time period, and the denominator is the population at risk of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=488 b=0 i=0 f=f5] experiencing the event duringthis period. The most accurate way of calculating in-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=501 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cidence is to calculate what Last calls the “person-time incidence rate.”11 Each person
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=514 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in the study population contributes one person-year to the denominator for each year
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=527 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (or day, week, month) of observation before disease develops, or the person is lost
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=540 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to follow-up.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=553 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Incidence (I)) is calculated as follows:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=200 y=573 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Number of new events in a specified period
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=164 y=577 b=0 i=0 f=f5] I= (×10n)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=181 y=585 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Number of persons exposed to risk during this period
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=606 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The numerator strictly refers only to first events offdisease. The unitsof incidence rate
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=619 b=0 i=0 f=f5] must always include a unit of time (cases per 10n and per day, week, month, year, etc.).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=632 b=0 i=0 f=f5] For each individual in the population, the time of observation is the period that the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=645 b=0 i=0 f=f5] person remains disease-free. The denominator used for the calculation of incidence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=658 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is therefore the sum of all the disease-free person-time periods during the period of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=671 b=0 i=0 f=f5] observation of thepopulation at risk.

[PAGE 34]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=345 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Measuring health and disease 21
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=85 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Since it may not be possible to measure disease-free periods precisely, the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=98 b=0 i=0 f=f5] denominator is often calculated approximately by multiplying the average size of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=111 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the study population by the length of the study period. This is reasonably accurate
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=124 b=0 i=0 f=f5] if the size of the populationis large and stable and incidence is low, for example, for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] stroke.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In a study in the United States of America, the incidence rate of stroke was
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] measured in 118 539 women who were 30–55 years of age and free from coronary
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=175 b=0 i=0 f=f5] heart disease, stroke and cancer in 1976 (see Table 2.4).A total of 274 stroke cases
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=188 b=0 i=0 f=f5] were identified in eight years of follow-up (908 447 person-years). The overall stroke
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] incidence rate was 30.2 per 100 000 person-years of observation and the rate was
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f5] higher for smokers than non-smokers; the rate for ex-smokers was intermediate.
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=55 y=244 b=1 i=0 f=f4] Table 2.4. Relationship between cigarette smoking and incidence rate of stroke in
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=55 y=255 b=1 i=0 f=f4] a cohort of 118 539 women13

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=374 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Cumulative incidence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=385 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Cumulative incidence is a simpler measure of the occurrence of a disease or health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=398 b=0 i=0 f=f5] status. Unlike incidence, it measures the denominator only at the beginning of a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=411 b=0 i=0 f=f5] study.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=424 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The cumulative incidence canbe calculated as follows:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=162 y=447 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Numberofpeoplewhogetadiseaseduringa
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=162 y=462 b=0 i=0 f=f5] specifiedperiod
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=54 y=471 b=0 i=0 f=f5] CumulativeIncidence=
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=341 y=466 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (×10n)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=162 y=479 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Numberofpeoplefreeofthediseaseinthe
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=162 y=494 b=0 i=0 f=f5] populationatriskatthebeginningoftheperiod
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=517 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Cumulative incidence is often presented as cases per 1000 population. Table 2.4
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=530 b=0 i=0 f=f5] shows that the cumulative incidence for stroke over the eight-year follow-up was 2.3
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=543 b=0 i=0 f=f5] per 1000 (274 cases of stroke dividedby the 118 539 women who entered the study).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=556 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In a statistical sense, the cumulative incidence is the probability that individuals in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=569 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the population get the disease during the specified period.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=582 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The period can be of any length but is usually several years, or even the whole
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=595 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lifetime. The cumulative incidence rate therefore is similar to the “risk of death” con-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=608 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cept used in actuarial and life-table calculations. The simplicity of cumulative
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=621 b=0 i=0 f=f5] incidence rates makes them useful when communicating health information to the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=634 b=0 i=0 f=f5] general public.

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Case fatality
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=112 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Case fatality is a measure of disease severity and is defined as the proportion of cases
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=125 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with a specified disease or condition who die within a specified time. It is usually
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=138 b=0 i=0 f=f5] expressed as a percentage.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=237 y=161 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Numberofdeathsfromdiagnosed casesina
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=237 y=176 b=0 i=0 f=f5] givenperiod
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=183 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Casefatality(%%%)= ×100
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=237 y=193 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Numberofdiagnosedcasesofthediseaseinthe
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=238 y=208 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sameperiod
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=234 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Interrelationships of the different measures
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=261 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Prevalence is dependent on both incidence and disease duration. Provided that the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=274 b=0 i=0 f=f5] prevalence (P)) is low and does not vary significantly with time, it can be calculated
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=287 b=0 i=0 f=f5] approximately as:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=212 y=308 b=0 i=0 f=f5] P = incidence × average duration of disease
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=329 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The cumulative incidence rate of a disease depends on both the incidence and the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=342 b=0 i=0 f=f5] length of the period of measurement. Since incidenceusually changes with age, age-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=355 b=0 i=0 f=f5] specific incidence rates needto be calculated. The cumulative incidence rate is a useful
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=368 b=0 i=0 f=f5] approximation of incidence when the rate is low or when the study period is short.

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=505 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Limitations of death certificates
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=532 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Data derived from death statistics are prone to various sources of error but, from an
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=545 b=0 i=0 f=f5] epidemiological perspective, often provide invaluable information on trends in a pop-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=558 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ulation’s health status. The usefulness of thedata depends on many factors, including
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=571 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the completeness of records and the accuracy in assigning the underlying causes of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=584 b=0 i=0 f=f5] death—especially in elderly people for whom autopsy rates are often low.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=597 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemiologists relyheavily on death statistics for assessing the burden of dis-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=610 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ease, as well asfor tracking changes in diseases over time. However, in many countries
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=623 b=0 i=0 f=f5] basic mortality statistics are not available, usually because of alack of resources to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=636 b=0 i=0 f=f5] establish routine vital registration systems. The provision of accurate cause-of-death
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=649 b=0 i=0 f=f5] information is a priority for health services.15

[PAGE 37]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 24 Chapter 2
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Limitations of vital registration systems
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=112 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The WHO Mortality Database includes only one third of adult deaths in the world,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=125 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and these are mainly in high-income and middle-income countries.16, 17Not all coun-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=138 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tries are able to submit mortality data to WHO, and for some there are concerns about
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=151 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the accuracy of the data. In some countries, the vital registration system covers only
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=164 b=0 i=0 f=f5] part of the country (urban areas, or only some provinces).In other countries, although
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=177 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the vital registration system covers the whole country, notall deaths are registered.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=190 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Some countries rely on validation of deaths from representative samples of the pop-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=203 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ulation (as in China and India); in other countries, demographic surveillance sites
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=216 b=0 i=0 f=f5] provide mortality rates for selected populations.18
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=239 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Verbal autopsy
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=250 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A verbal autopsy is an indirect method of ascertaining biomedical causes of death
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=263 b=0 i=0 f=f5] from information on symptoms, signs and circumstances precedingdeath, obtained
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=276 b=0 i=0 f=f5] from the deceased person’s family.19 In many middle- and low-income countries,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=289 b=0 i=0 f=f5] verbal autopsy is the only method used to obtain estimates of the distribution of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=302 b=0 i=0 f=f5] causes of death.20 Verbal autopsies areused mainly in the context of demographic
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=315 b=0 i=0 f=f5] surveillance and sample registration systems. The diversity of tools and methods used
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=328 b=0 i=0 f=f5] makes it difficult to compare cause-of-death data between places over time.21
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=352 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Towards comparable estimates
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=379 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Even in countries where underlying causes of death are assigned by qualified staff,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=392 b=0 i=0 f=f5] miscoding can occur. The main reasons for this are:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=414 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • systematic biases in diagnosis
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=426 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • incorrect or incomplete death certificates
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=438 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • misinterpretation of ICD rules for selection of the underlying cause
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=450 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • variations in the use of coding categories for unknown and ill-defined causes.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=473 b=0 i=0 f=f5] For these reasons, data comparisons between countries can be misleading. WHO
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=486 b=0 i=0 f=f5] works with countries to produce country-level estimates, which are then adjusted to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=499 b=0 i=0 f=f5] account for these differences (see Box 2.3).
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=147 y=533 b=1 i=0 f=f4] Box 2.3. Comparable estimates derived from official statistics
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=550 b=0 i=0 f=f5] An assessment of the global status of cause of death data suggests that of the 192 Member
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=560 b=0 i=0 f=f5] States of WHO, only 23 countries have high-quality data defined as:
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=573 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •data are more than 90% complete
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=585 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •ill-defined causes of death account for less than 10% of the total causes of death
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=596 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •ICD-9 or ICD-10 codes are used.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=612 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The country-level estimates that WHO produces adjust for differences in completeness
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=623 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and accuracy of data supplied by countries. Estimates are basedon data from 112 national
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=634 b=0 i=0 f=f5] vital registration systems that capture about 18.6 million deaths annually, representing
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=645 b=0 i=0 f=f5] one third of all deaths occurring in the world. Information from sample registration sys-
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=656 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tems, population laboratories and epidemiological studies are also used to improve these
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=667 b=0 i=0 f=f5] estimates.

[PAGE 38]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=345 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Measuring health and disease 25
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=85 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Where national vital registration systems do existand are included in the WHO
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=98 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Mortality Database:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=119 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • death certificates may not be complete
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=131 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • poorer segments of populations may not be covered
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=143 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • deaths may notbe reported for cultural or religious reasons
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=155 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • the age at death may not be given accurately.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=178 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Other factors contributing to unreliable registration systems include: late registration,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=191 b=0 i=0 f=f5] missing data and errors in reporting or classifying thecause of death.19
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=204 b=0 i=0 f=f5] As it takes a long time for countries to build good quality vitalregistration systems,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=217 b=0 i=0 f=f5] alternative methods are often used to assigncause-of-death and toestimate mortality.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=240 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Death rates
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=272 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The death rate (or crude mortality rate) for all deaths or a specific cause of death is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] calculatedas follows:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=165 y=305 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Numberofdeathsduringaspecifiedperiod
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=63 y=309 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Crudemortalityrate= (×10n)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=165 y=320 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Numberofpersonsatriskofdyingduring
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=165 y=335 b=0 i=0 f=f5] thesameperiod
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=358 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The main disadvantage of the crude mortality rate is that it does not take into
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=371 b=0 i=0 f=f5] account the fact thatthe chance of dying varies according to age, sex, race, socio-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=383 b=0 i=0 f=f5] economic class and other factors. It is not usually appropriate touse it for comparing
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=396 b=0 i=0 f=f5] different time periods or geographical areas.For example, patterns of death in newly
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=409 b=0 i=0 f=f5] occupied urban developments with many youngfamilies are likely to be very different
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=421 b=0 i=0 f=f5] from those in seaside resorts, where retired people may chooseto live. Comparisons
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=434 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of mortality rates between groups of diverse age structureare usually based on age-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=447 b=0 i=0 f=f5] standardized rates.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=469 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Age-specific death rates
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=480 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Death rates can be expressed for specific groups in a population which are defined
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=493 b=0 i=0 f=f5] by age, race, sex, occupation or geographical location, or for specific causes of death.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=506 b=0 i=0 f=f5] For example, an age- and sex-specific deathrate is defined as:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=529 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Totalnumberofdeathsoccurringinaspecificageandsexgroup
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=544 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ofthepopulationinadefinedareaduringaspecifiedperiod
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=329 y=550 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (×10n)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=72 y=561 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Estimatedtotalpopulationofthesameageandsexgroupof the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=72 y=576 b=0 i=0 f=f5] populationinthesameareaduringthesameperiod
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=601 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Proportionate mortality
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=612 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Occasionally the mortality in a population is described by using proportionate
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=625 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mortality, which is actually aratio: the number of deaths from a given cause per 100
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=638 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or 1000 total deaths in the same period. Proportionate mortality does not express the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=651 b=0 i=0 f=f5] risk of members of a population contracting orrdying from a disease.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=664 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Comparisons of proportionate rates between groups may show interesting
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=677 b=0 i=0 f=f5] differences. However, unless the crude or age-group-specific mortality rates are

[PAGE 39]
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=85 b=0 i=0 f=f5] known, it may not be clear whether a difference between groups relates to variations
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=98 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in the numerators or the denominators. For example, proportionate mortality rates
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=111 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for cancer would be much greater in high-income countries with many old people
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=124 b=0 i=0 f=f5] than in low- and middle-income countries with few old people, even if the actual
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lifetime risk of cancer is the same.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=160 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Infant mortality
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=188 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The infant mortality rateis commonly used as an indicator of the level of health in a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] community. It measures the rate of death in children during the first year of life, the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f5] denominator being the number of live births in the same year.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=227 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The infant mortality rate is calculated as follows:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=253 y=250 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Numberofdeathsinayearofchildren
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=253 y=265 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lessthan1yearofage
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=159 y=274 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Infantmortalityrate= ×1000
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=254 y=280 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Numberoflivebirthsinthesameyear
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=300 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The use of infant mortality rates as a measure of overall health status for a given
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=313 b=0 i=0 f=f5] population is based on the assumption that it is particularly sensitive to socioeco-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=326 b=0 i=0 f=f5] nomic changes and to healthcare interventions. Infant mortality has declined in all
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=339 b=0 i=0 f=f5] regions of the world, but widedifferences persist between and within countries (see
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=352 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Figure 2.4).
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=381 b=1 i=0 f=f4] Figure 2.4. Worldwide trends in infant mortality, 1950–200022

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=531 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Child mortality rate
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=559 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The child mortality rate (under-5 mortality rate) is based on deaths of children aged
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=572 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1–4 years, and is frequently used as a basic health indicator. Injuries, malnutrition
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=585 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and infectious diseases are common causes of death in this age group. The under-5
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=598 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mortality rate describes the probability (expressed per 1000 live births) of a child dying
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=611 b=0 i=0 f=f5] before reaching 5 years of age. Table 2.5 shows the mortality rates for countries
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=624 b=0 i=0 f=f5] representing a range of income categories. The areas of uncertainty around the esti-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=637 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mates for middle-income and low-income countries are shown in parentheses.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=650 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Data in Table 2.5 have been calculatedso that the information can be compared
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=663 b=0 i=0 f=f5] between countries. Mortality rates per 1000 live births vary from as low as 4 for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=675 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Japan (based on precise data) to 297 for males in Sierra Leone (with a wide range of

[L s=8.0 r=0.89 x=62 y=386 b=1 i=0 f=f4] Box 2.4. Alternative approaches to obtaining information on deaths in children
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=402 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Where accurate death registers do not exist, infant and child mortality can be estimated from information collected in
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=413 b=0 i=0 f=f5] household surveys in which the following question is initially asked: “During the last two years, have any children in this
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=424 b=0 i=0 f=f5] household died who were aged five years or less?”
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=75 y=435 b=0 i=0 f=f5] If the answer is “yes,” three questions are asked:
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=75 y=447 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •“How many months ago did the death occur?”
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=75 y=459 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •“How many months of age was the child at death?”
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=75 y=470 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •“Was the child a boy or a girl?”
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=486 b=0 i=0 f=f5] If information on the number and ages of surviving children is collected during a survey, infant and child mortality rates can
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=497 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be estimated with reasonable accuracy.Adult mortality can also beapproximated from household surveys if accurate infor-
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=508 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mation is not available.
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=519 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Problems with using household surveys to obtain information on deaths include:
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=75 y=532 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •respondents may not understand the time span of the question,
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=75 y=543 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •children who die shortly after birth may be left out,
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=75 y=555 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •for cultural reasons, more male than female deaths may be reported.
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=570 b=0 i=0 f=f5] However, this is the only method that is applicable in some communities. Measurement off infant mortality in low-income
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=581 b=0 i=0 f=f5] communities is particularly important in helping planners to address the need for equity in health care. Additionally, reducing
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=592 b=0 i=0 f=f5] child mortality rates is one of the Millennium Development Goals (see Chapter 10).
[L s=14.0 r=1.56 x=55 y=624 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Maternal mortality rate
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=55 y=652 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The maternal mortality rate refers to the risk of mothers dying from causes associated
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=55 y=665 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with delivering babies, complications of pregnancy or childbirth. This important

[PAGE 41]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 28 Chapter 2
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=85 b=0 i=0 f=f5] statistic is often neglected because it isdifficult to calculate accurately. The maternal
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=98 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mortality rate is given by:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=256 y=127 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Number of maternal deaths in a given
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=256 y=142 b=0 i=0 f=f5] geographic area in a given year
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=151 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Maternalmortalityrateee=
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=418 y=146 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ( ×10n)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=259 y=159 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Number of live births that occurred
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=259 y=174 b=0 i=0 f=f5] among the population of the given
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=259 y=189 b=0 i=0 f=f5] geographic area during the same year
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=212 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The maternal mortality rate ranges from about 3 per 100 000 live births in high-income

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=404 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Age-standardized rates
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=431 b=0 i=0 f=f5] An age-standardized death rate (also referred to as an age-adjusted rate) is a summary
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=444 b=0 i=0 f=f5] measure of the death rate that a population would have if it had a standard age
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=457 b=0 i=0 f=f5] structure. The standardization of rates can be done either
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=470 b=0 i=0 f=f5] directly or indirectly (see Box 2.5).
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=288 y=479 b=1 i=0 f=f4] Box 2.5. Direct and indirect standardization of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=483 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Age-standardized rates enable comparisons to be
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=288 y=490 b=1 i=0 f=f4] disease rates
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=496 b=0 i=0 f=f5] made between populations that have different age struc-
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=288 y=506 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The direct method of standardization is more frequently
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=509 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tures. Standardization can alsobe done for variables other
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=288 y=517 b=0 i=0 f=f5] used, and is done by applying the disease rates of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=522 b=0 i=0 f=f5] than age. This is necessary when comparing two or more
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=288 y=528 b=0 i=0 f=f5] populations being compared toa standard population.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=535 b=0 i=0 f=f5] populations that have different basic characteristics that
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=288 y=538 b=0 i=0 f=f5] This method yields the number of cases that would be
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=548 b=0 i=0 f=f5] independently influence the risk of death (such as age, expected if the age-specific rates in the standard popu-
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=560 b=0 i=0 f=f5] race, socioeconomic status, etc.). lation were true for the study population.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=73 y=571 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Frequently used standard populations include: Standardized rates are used,whenever relevant, for
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=288 y=582 b=0 i=0 f=f5] morbidity as well asmortality. The choice of a standard
