[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=140 y=486 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Morbidity
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=519 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Death rates are particularly useful for investigating diseases with a high case-fatality.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=532 b=0 i=0 f=f5] However, many diseases have low case-fatality, for example, most mental illnesses,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=545 b=0 i=0 f=f5] musculoskeletal diseases, rheumatoid arthritis, chickenpox and mumps. In this situ-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=558 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ation, data on morbidity (illness) are more useful than mortality rates.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=571 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Morbidity data areoften helpful in clarifying the reasons for particular trends in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=583 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mortality. Changes in death rates could be due to changes in morbidity rates or in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=596 b=0 i=0 f=f5] case-fatality. For example, the recent declinein cardiovascular disease mortality rates
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=609 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in many developed countries could be due to a fall in either incidence (suggesting
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=622 b=0 i=0 f=f5] improvements in primary prevention) or in case-fatality (suggesting improvements in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=635 b=0 i=0 f=f5] treatment). Because population age structures change with time, time-trend analyses
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=648 b=0 i=0 f=f5] should be based on age-standardized morbidity and mortality rates.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=661 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Other sources of morbidity data include:

[PAGE 44]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=345 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Measuring health and disease 31
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • hospital admissions and discharges
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=95 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • outpatient and primaryhealth care consultations
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=107 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • specialist services (such as injury treatment)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=119 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • registers of disease events (such as cancer and congenital malformations).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=142 b=0 i=0 f=f5] To be useful for epidemiological studies, the data must be relevant and easily acces-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=155 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sible. In some countries, the confidential nature of patient medical records may make
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=167 b=0 i=0 f=f5] hospital data inaccessible for epidemiological studies. A recording system focusing
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=180 b=0 i=0 f=f5] on administrative or financial data, rather than on diagnostic and individual charac-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=192 b=0 i=0 f=f5] teristics may diminish the epidemiological value of routine health service data.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=205 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Hospital admission rates are influenced by factors other than the morbidity of the pop-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=217 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ulation, such as the availability of beds, hospital admission policies and social factors.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=229 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Because of the numerous limitations of routinely recorded morbidity data, many
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=242 b=0 i=0 f=f5] epidemiological studies of morbidity rely on the collection of new data using specially
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=254 b=0 i=0 f=f5] designed questionnaires and screening methods. This enables investigators to have
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=267 b=0 i=0 f=f5] more confidence in the data and the rates calculated from them.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=291 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Disability
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=318 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemiologists are concerned not only with the occurrence of disease, but also with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=330 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the consequences of disease: impairments, disabilities and handicaps. These have
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=343 b=0 i=0 f=f5] been defined by the WHO International Classification of Functioning, Disability and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=355 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Health (ICF).32
[L s=9.9 r=1.00 x=71 y=369 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ICF describes how people live with their health condition. Since an individual’s
[L s=9.9 r=1.00 x=54 y=382 b=0 i=0 f=f5] functioning and disability occurs within a context, ICF also includes a list of envi-
[L s=9.9 r=1.00 x=54 y=394 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ronmental factors. ICF is a useful tool for understanding and measuring health
[L s=9.9 r=1.00 x=54 y=406 b=0 i=0 f=f5] outcomes. It can be used in clinical settings, health services or surveys, at the indi-
[L s=9.9 r=1.00 x=54 y=419 b=0 i=0 f=f5] vidual or population level.
[L s=9.9 r=1.00 x=71 y=432 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The key parameters of ICF are as follows:
[L s=9.9 r=1.00 x=71 y=454 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) impairment: any loss or abnormality of psychological, physiological or
[L s=9.9 r=1.00 x=79 y=465 b=0 i=0 f=f5] anatomical structure or function;
[L s=9.9 r=1.00 x=71 y=476 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) disability: any restriction or lack (resulting from an impairment) of ability to
[L s=9.9 r=1.00 x=79 y=488 b=0 i=0 f=f5] perform an activity in the manner or within the range considered normal for a
[L s=9.9 r=1.00 x=79 y=499 b=0 i=0 f=f5] human being;
[L s=9.9 r=1.00 x=71 y=511 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) handicap: a disadvantage for a given individual, resulting from an impairment
[L s=9.9 r=1.00 x=79 y=522 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or a disability, that limits or prevents the fulfilment of a role that is normal
[L s=9.9 r=1.00 x=79 y=534 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (depending on age, sex, and social and cultural factors) for that individual.
[L s=9.9 r=1.00 x=54 y=556 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The relationship between the different non-fatal outcomes is shown in Box 2.6.
[L s=7.9 r=0.80 x=61 y=591 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Box 2.6. Schema for assessing non-fatal health outcomes
[L s=7.9 r=0.80 x=61 y=609 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Disease (cid:314) Impairment (cid:314) Disability (cid:314) Handicap

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=85 b=0 i=0 f=f5] It is difficult to measurethe prevalence of disability, but it is becoming increas-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=98 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ingly important in societies where acute morbidity and fatal illness are decreasing,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=111 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and where there is an increasingnumber of aged people living with disabilities.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=135 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Health determinants, indicators, and risk factors
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=162 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Health determinants
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=173 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Health determinants are generally defined as the underlying social, economic, cultural
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=186 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and environmental factorsthat are responsible for health and disease, most of which
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=199 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are outside the health sector.33–35
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=222 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Health indicators
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=233 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A health indicator is a variable – that canbe measured directly toreflect the state of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=246 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health of people within a community. WHO presents the most recent data for 50
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=259 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health indicators each year.25 Health indicators can also be used as components in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=272 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the calculation of a broader social development index. The best example is the Human
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Development Index, which ranks countries each year according to a combination of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=298 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the level of economic development, literacy, education, and life expectancy (http://
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=311 b=0 i=0 f=f5] hdr.undp.org/).
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=334 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Risk factors
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=345 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A risk factor refers to anaspect of personal habits or an environmental exposure, that
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=358 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is associated with an increased probability of occurrence of a disease. Since risk factors
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=371 b=0 i=0 f=f5] can usually be modified, intervening to alter them in a favourable direction can reduce
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=384 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the probability of occurrence of disease. The impact of these interventions can be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=397 b=0 i=0 f=f5] determined by repeated measures using the same methods and definitions (see
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=410 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Box 2.7).
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=34 y=444 b=1 i=0 f=f4] Box 2.7. Measuring risk factors
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=460 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Risk factors can include tobacco and alcohol use, diet, physical inactivity, blood pressure and obesity. Since risk factors can
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=471 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be used to predict future disease, their measurement at a population level is important, but also challenging.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=47 y=482 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Tobacco use can be measured byself-reported exposure (yes/no), quantity of cigarettes smoked, or by biological markers
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=493 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (serum cotinine). However, different surveys use different methods, often with different measurement techniques and criteria
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=504 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for detecting a risk factor or clinical outcome (for example, diabetes or hypertension). Additionally, surveys may only be
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=515 b=0 i=0 f=f5] representative of small population groups within a country, district or city. These methodologicaldifferences mean that it is
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=526 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be difficult to compare results from different surveys and countries.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=52 y=537 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Efforts have been made to standardize methods of measurement of risk factors at the global level, including the WHO
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=548 b=0 i=0 f=f5] MONICA Project in the 1980s and 1990s.36, 37More recently, the WHO STEPS approach to the measurement of population
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=559 b=0 i=0 f=f5] levels of risk factors provides methods and materials to encourage countries to collect data in a standardized manner.38, 39
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=52 y=570 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Data from individual countries can be adjusted to account for known biases to make them internationally comparable.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=581 b=0 i=0 f=f5] This step is also necessary because countries conduct standard surveys at different times. If risk factor rates are changing
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=592 b=0 i=0 f=f5] over time, information on trends will be needed to adjust data to a standard reporting year.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=634 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Other summary measures of population health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=662 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Policy-makers face the challenge of responding to current disease prevention and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=675 b=0 i=0 f=f5] control priorities, while being responsible for predicting future priorities. Such decisions

[PAGE 46]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=345 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Measuring health and disease 33
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=85 b=0 i=0 f=f5] should be based on summary measures that quantify the amount of disease at the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=98 b=0 i=0 f=f5] population level. These measures need to combinedeaths and time spent in ill-health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=111 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in an internally consistent way, using a common unitof measurement.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=124 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Such summary measures serve as a common currency for reporting the burden
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of disease in populations. They provide a way of monitoring and evaluating popula-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tion health, so that prevention and control actions can be taken rapidly when
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] necessary.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=175 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Mortality alone does not provide a full picture of how different causes affect
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=188 b=0 i=0 f=f5] population health. Duration of life combined with some notion of its quality are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] reflected in the following population measures:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=223 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • years of potential life lost (PLL) basedon the years of life lost through premature
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=235 b=0 i=0 f=f5] death (before an arbitrarily determined age);
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=246 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • healthy life expectancy (HALE);
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=258 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • disability-free life expectancy (DFLE);
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=270 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • quality-adjustedlife years (QALYs);
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=282 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • disability-adjusted life years (DALYs).
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=305 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Disability-adjusted life years
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=316 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The Global Burden offDisease project40combines the impact off premature mortality
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=329 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with that of disability. It captures the population impact of important fatal and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=342 b=0 i=0 f=f5] non-fatal disabling conditions through a single measure. The major measure used is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=355 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disability-adjustedlife years (DALYs) which combines:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=376 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • years of lost life (YLL) – calculated from the number of deaths at each age
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=389 b=0 i=0 f=f5] multiplied by the expected remaining years of life according to a global stan-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=400 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dard life expectancy
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=411 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • years lost to disability (YLD), where the number offincident casesdue to injury
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=423 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and illness is multiplied by the average duration of the disease and a weighting
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=434 b=0 i=0 f=f5] factor reflecting the severity of the disease on a scale from 0 (perfect health)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=446 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to 1 (dead).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=468 b=0 i=0 f=f5] One DALY is one lost year of “healthy” life and the measured disease burden is the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=481 b=0 i=0 f=f5] gap between a population’s current health status and that of an ideal situation where
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=494 b=0 i=0 f=f5] everyone lives into old age, free of disability. The normative reference population has
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=507 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a life expectancy at birth of 82.5 years for females and 80.0 years for males.40
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=520 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Time-discounting andnon-uniform age weights, which give less weight to years
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=533 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lived at young and older ages, are used in calculating standard DALYs as reported in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=546 b=0 i=0 f=f5] recent WHO World Health Reports. With age weights and time discounting, a death
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=559 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in infancy corresponds to 33 DALYs, and deaths from ages 5 to 20 to around 36
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=572 b=0 i=0 f=f5] DALYs. Thus a disease burden of 3300 DALYs in a population would be the equivalent
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=585 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of 100 infant deaths or to approximately 5500 persons aged 50 years living one year
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=598 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with blindness (disability weighting = 0.6).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=611 b=0 i=0 f=f5] DALYs were designed to guide World Bank investment policies for health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=624 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and to inform global priority setting for health research and international health pro-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=637 b=0 i=0 f=f5] grams.41Analysis of DALYs due to a variety of causes and risk factors has given new
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=650 b=0 i=0 f=f5] perspectives on the relative importance of different areas of disease prevention.42

[PAGE 47]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 34 Chapter 2
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Comparing disease occurrence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=116 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Measuring the occurrence of disease or other health states is the first step of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=129 b=0 i=0 f=f5] epidemiological process. The next step is comparing occurrence in two or more
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=142 b=0 i=0 f=f5] groups of people whoseexposures have differed. An individual can be either exposed
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=155 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or unexposed to a factor under study. An unexposed group is oftenused as a reference
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=168 b=0 i=0 f=f5] group. Exposed people can have different levels and durations of exposure (see
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=181 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Chapter 9). The total amount of a factor that reaches an individual is called the “dose.”
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=194 b=0 i=0 f=f5] We can then compare occurrences to calculate the riskthat a health effect will
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=207 b=0 i=0 f=f5] result from an exposure. We can make bothabsolute and relative comparisons; the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=220 b=0 i=0 f=f5] measures describe the strength of anassociation betweenexposure andoutcome.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=244 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Absolute comparisons
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=272 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Risk difference
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=283 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The risk difference, also called excess risk, is the difference in rates of occurrence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=296 b=0 i=0 f=f5] between exposed and unexposed groups inthe population. It isa useful measure of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=309 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the extent of the public health problem caused by the exposure. For example, from
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=322 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the data in Table 2.4 the risk difference between the incidence rate of stroke in women
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=335 b=0 i=0 f=f5] who smoke, and the rate of stroke in women who have never smoked, is 31.9 per
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=347 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 100 000 person-years.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=360 b=0 i=0 f=f5] When comparing two or more groups, it is important that they are as similar as
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=373 b=0 i=0 f=f5] possible, with the exception of the factor being analysed. If the groups differ in relation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=386 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to age, sex, etc. the rates must be standardized before a comparison can be made.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=409 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Attributable fraction (exposed)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=420 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The attributable fraction (exposed), also known as the etiological fraction (exposed),
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=433 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is the proportion of all cases that can be attributed to a particular exposure. We
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=446 b=0 i=0 f=f5] can determine the attributable fraction (AF) by dividing the risk (or attributable)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=459 b=0 i=0 f=f5] difference by the incidence among the exposed population. For the data in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=472 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Table 2.4 the attributable fraction of smoking for stroke in the smokers is:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=485 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ((49.6 – 17.7)/49.6) ×100 = 64%.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=498 b=0 i=0 f=f5] When a particular exposure is believed to be a cause of a given disease, the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=511 b=0 i=0 f=f5] attributable fraction is the proportion of the disease in the specific population that
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=524 b=0 i=0 f=f5] would be eliminated if the exposure were eliminated. In the above example, one would
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=537 b=0 i=0 f=f5] expect to achieve a 64%reduction in the risk of stroke among the women smokers
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=550 b=0 i=0 f=f5] if smoking were stopped, based on the assumption that smoking is both causal and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=563 b=0 i=0 f=f5] preventable.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=576 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Attributable fractions are useful for assessing priorities for public health action.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=589 b=0 i=0 f=f5] For example, both smoking and air pollution are causes of lung cancer, but the at-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=602 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tributable fraction due to smoking is usually much greater than that due to air
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=615 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pollution. Only in communities with very low smoking prevalence and severe air
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=628 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pollution is the latter likely to be the majorrcause of lung cancer. In most countries,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=641 b=0 i=0 f=f5] smoking control should take priority in lung cancer prevention programmes.

[PAGE 48]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=345 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Measuring health and disease 35
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=85 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Population attributable risk
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=96 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The population attributable risk (PARRR) is the incidence of a disease in a population
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=109 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that is associated with (or attributed to) an exposure to a risk factor.11 This measure
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=122 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is useful for determining the relative importance of exposures for the entire population.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=135 b=0 i=0 f=f5] It is the proportion by which the incidence rate of the outcome in the entire population
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=148 b=0 i=0 f=f5] would be reducedif exposure were eliminated.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=72 y=161 b=0 i=0 f=f5] PARRcan be estimated by the formula:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=213 y=181 b=0 i=0 f=f5] I −I
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=216 y=186 b=0 i=0 f=f5] p u
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=183 y=190 b=0 i=0 f=f5] PAR=
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=221 y=196 b=0 i=0 f=f5] I
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=224 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] p
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=212 b=0 i=0 f=f5] where
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=72 y=225 b=0 i=0 f=f5] I is the incidence of the disease in the total population and
[L s=7.0 r=0.70 x=75 y=229 b=0 i=0 f=f5] p
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=72 y=239 b=0 i=0 f=f5] I is the incidence of the disease among the unexposed group.
[L s=7.0 r=0.70 x=75 y=243 b=0 i=0 f=f5] u
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=264 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Relative comparisons
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=292 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Relative risk
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=303 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The relative risk (also called the risk ratio) is the ratio of the risk of occurrence of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=316 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a disease among exposed people to that among the unexposed. As shown in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=329 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Table 2.4, the risk ratio of stroke in women who smoke, compared with those who
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=342 b=0 i=0 f=f5] have never smoked, is 2.8 (49.6 /17.7).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=355 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The risk ratio is a better indicator of the strength of an association than the risk
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=368 b=0 i=0 f=f5] difference, because it is expressed relative to a baseline level of occurrence. Unlike
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=381 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the risk difference, it is related to the magnitude of the baseline incidence rate; pop-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=394 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ulations with similar risk differences can have greatly differing risk ratios, depending
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=407 b=0 i=0 f=f5] on the magnitude of the baseline rates.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=420 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The risk ratio is used in assessing the likelihood that an association represents a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=433 b=0 i=0 f=f5] causal relationship. For example, the risk ratio off lung cancer in long-term heavy
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=445 b=0 i=0 f=f5] smokers compared with non-smokers is approximately 20. This is very high and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=458 b=0 i=0 f=f5] indicates that this relationship is not likely to be a chance finding. Of course, smaller
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=471 b=0 i=0 f=f5] risk ratios can also indicate a causal relationship,but care must be taken to eliminate
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=484 b=0 i=0 f=f5] other possible explanations (see Chapter 5).
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=507 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Attributable risk
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=518 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Attributable risk is the rate (proportion) of a disease or other outcome in exposed
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=531 b=0 i=0 f=f5] individuals that can be attributed to the exposure. This is a more useful term for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=544 b=0 i=0 f=f5] public health purposes as it reflects the amount, usually expressed as a percentage,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=557 b=0 i=0 f=f5] by which the risk of a disease is reduced by elimination or control of a particular
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=570 b=0 i=0 f=f5] exposure. Using attributable risk, it is possible to estimate the number of people
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=583 b=0 i=0 f=f5] spared the consequences of exposure, by subtracting the rate of the outcome (usually
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=596 b=0 i=0 f=f5] incidence or mortality) among the unexposed from the rate among the exposed in-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=609 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dividuals. For example, if there were 6 deaths per 100among smokers, and 1 death
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=622 b=0 i=0 f=f5] per 100 in non-smokers, the attributable risk would be 5per 100. Thisassumes that
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=635 b=0 i=0 f=f5] causes other than the oneunder investigation have had equal effects on the exposed
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=648 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and unexposed groups.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=661 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In summary, there are various measures for studying populations. Chapter 3
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=674 b=0 i=0 f=f5] refers to many of these measures in the context of study design.

[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Chapter 3
[L s=32.0 r=3.20 x=55 y=101 b=0 i=0 f=f7] Types of studies
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=168 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Key messages
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=182 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Choosing the appropriate study design is a crucial step in an epidemiological
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=194 b=0 i=0 f=f5] investigation.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=205 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Each study design has strengths and weaknesses.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=217 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Epidemiologists must consider all sources of bias and confounding, and strive
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=228 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to reduce them.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=240 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Ethical issues are important in epidemiology, as in other sciences.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=268 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Observations and experiments
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=300 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemiological studies can be classified as either observational or experimental.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=313 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The most common types of study are listed with their alternative names and units of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=326 b=0 i=0 f=f5] study in Table 3.1. The terms in the left-hand column are used throughout this book.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=350 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Observational studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=378 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Observational studies allow nature to take its course: the investigator measures but
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=391 b=0 i=0 f=f5] does not intervene. They include studies that can be called descriptive or analytical:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=413 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) A ddescriptive study is limited to a description of the occurrence of a disease
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=424 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in a population and is often the first step in an epidemiological investigation.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=436 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) An aanalytical study goes further by analysing relationships between health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=447 b=0 i=0 f=f5] status and other variables.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=470 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Apart from the simplest descriptive studies, almost all epidemiological studies are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=483 b=0 i=0 f=f5] analytical in character. Pure descriptive studies are rare, but descriptive data in reports
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=496 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of health statistics are a useful source of ideas for epidemiological studies.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=509 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Limited descriptive information (such as that provided in a case series) in which
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=522 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the characteristics of several patients with a specific disease are described but are not
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=535 b=0 i=0 f=f5] compared with those of a reference population, often stimulates the initiation of a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=548 b=0 i=0 f=f5] more detailed epidemiological study. For example, the description in 1981 of four
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=561 b=0 i=0 f=f5] young men with a previously rare form of pneumonia was the first in a wide range of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=574 b=0 i=0 f=f5] epidemiological studies on the condition that became known as the acquired
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=587 b=0 i=0 f=f5] immunodeficiency syndrome (AIDS).1
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=611 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Experimental studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=638 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Experimental or intervention studies involve an active attempt to change a disease
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=651 b=0 i=0 f=f5] determinant – such as an exposure or a behaviour – or the progress of a disease through
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=664 b=0 i=0 f=f5] treatment, and are similar in design to experiments in other sciences. However, they

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 40 Chapter 3
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=87 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Table 3.1. Types of epidemiological study
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=109 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Type of study Alternative name Unit of study
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=125 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Observational studies
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=137 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Descriptive studies
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Analytical studies
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=161 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Ecological Correlational Populations
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=173 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Cross-sectional Prevalence Individuals
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=185 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Case-control Case-reference Individuals
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=197 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Cohort Follow-up Individuals
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=209 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Experimental studies Intervention studies
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=221 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Randomized controlled Clinical trials Individuals
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=230 b=0 i=0 f=f14] trials
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=242 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Cluster randomized Groups
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=251 b=0 i=0 f=f14] controlled trials
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=263 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Field trials
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=275 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Community trials Community intervention Healthy people
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=249 y=284 b=0 i=0 f=f14] studies Communities
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=311 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are subject to extra constraints, since the health of the people in the study group may
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=324 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be at stake. Major experimental study designs include the following:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=346 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) randomized controlled trials using patients as subjects (clinical trials),
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=358 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) field trials in which the participants are healthy people, and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=370 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) community trials in which the participants are the communities themselves.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=393 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In all epidemiological studies it is essential to have a clear definition of a case of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=406 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease being investigated by delineating the symptoms, signs or other characteristics
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=419 b=0 i=0 f=f5] indicating that a person has the disease. A clear definition of an exposed person is also
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=432 b=0 i=0 f=f5] necessary. This definition must include all the characteristics that identify a person as
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=445 b=0 i=0 f=f5] being exposed to the factor in question. In the absence of clear definitions of disease
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=458 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and exposure, it is very difficult to interpret the data from an epidemiological study.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=140 y=480 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Observational epidemiology
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=513 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Descriptive studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=540 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A simple description of the health status of a community, based on routinely avail-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=553 b=0 i=0 f=f5] able data or on data obtained in special surveys as described in Chapter 2, is often
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=566 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the first step in an epidemiological investigation. In many countries this type of study
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=579 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is undertaken by a national centre for health statistics. Pure descriptive studies make
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=592 b=0 i=0 f=f5] no attempt to analyse the links between exposure and effect. They are usually based
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=605 b=0 i=0 f=f5] on mortality statistics and may examine patterns of death by age, sex or ethnicity
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=618 b=0 i=0 f=f5] during specified time periods or in various countries.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=631 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A classic example of descriptive data is shown in Figure 3.1, which charts the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=644 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pattern of maternal mortality in Sweden since the middle of the eighteenth century,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=657 b=0 i=0 f=f5] showing maternal death rates per 100 000 live births.2 Such data can be of great
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=670 b=0 i=0 f=f5] value when identifying factors that have caused such a clear downward trend. It is

[PAGE 54]
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=55 y=86 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Figure 3.1. Maternal mortality rates in Sweden, 1750–19752
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=378 b=0 i=0 f=f5] interesting to speculate on the possible changes in the living conditions of young
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=391 b=0 i=0 f=f5] women in the 1860s and 1870s which might have caused the temporary rise in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=404 b=0 i=0 f=f5] maternal mortality at that time. In fact, this was a time of great poverty in Sweden
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=417 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and almost one million Swedes emigrated; most went to the United States of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=430 b=0 i=0 f=f5] America.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=443 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Figure 3.2 is also based on routine death statistics and provides an example of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=456 b=0 i=0 f=f5] change in death rates of heart disease over time in six countries. It shows that death
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=469 b=0 i=0 f=f5] rates from heart disease have fallen by up to 70% in the last three decades in several
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=482 b=0 i=0 f=f5] countries, including Australia, Canada, the United Kingdom and the United States
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=495 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of America. Yet during the same time, the rates in other countries – such as Brazil
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=508 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and the Russian Federation – have remained the same or increased.3 The next step
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=521 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in investigating this difference would require information about the comparability of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=534 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the death certificates, changes in the incidence and case-fatality of the disease, and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=547 b=0 i=0 f=f5] changes in the risk factors to which the relative populations have been exposed.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=571 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Ecological studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=599 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Ecological (or correlational) studies are useful for generating hypotheses. In an
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=612 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ecological study, the units of analysis are groups of people rather than individuals.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=625 b=0 i=0 f=f5] For example, a relationship was found between average sales of an anti-asthma
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=638 b=0 i=0 f=f5] drug and the occurrence of an unusually high number of asthma deaths in differ-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=651 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ent provinces of New Zealand.4 Such an observation would need to be tested by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=664 b=0 i=0 f=f5] controlling for all the potential confounders to exclude the possibility that other

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=343 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Ecological studies can also be done by comparing populations in different places
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=356 b=0 i=0 f=f5] at the same time or, in a time series, by comparing the same population in one place
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=369 b=0 i=0 f=f5] at different times. One example is the use of ecological data in the World Health

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=408 b=0 i=0 f=f5] very short, as it is in daily time series studies (Figure 3.3), confounding is virtually
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=421 b=0 i=0 f=f5] zero as the people in the study serve as their own controls.

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=391 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Types of studies 43
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Although simple to conduct and thus attractive, ecological studies are often
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] difficult to interpret since it is seldom possible to examine directly the various potential
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] explanations for findings. Ecological studies usually rely on data collected for other
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] purposes; data on different exposures and on socioeconomic factors may not be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] available. In addition, since the unit of analysis is a group, the link between exposure
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and effect at the individual level can not be made. One attraction of ecological studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is that data can be used from populations with widely differing characteristics or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=175 b=0 i=0 f=f5] extracted from different data sources.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=188 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The increasing death rate during the heat wave in France in 2003 (Figure 3.3)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] correlated well with increasing temperature, although increasing daily air pollution
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f5] also played a role. This increase of deaths occurred mainly among elderly people and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=227 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the immediate cause of death was often recorded as heart or lung disease.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=251 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Ecological fallacy
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=279 b=0 i=0 f=f5] An ecological fallacy or bias results if inappropriate conclusions are drawn on the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=292 b=0 i=0 f=f5] basis of ecological data. The bias occurs because the association observed between
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=304 b=0 i=0 f=f5] variables at the group level does not necessarily represent the association that exists
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=318 b=0 i=0 f=f5] at the individual level (see Chapter 2). An example of an ecological fallacy would be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=331 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the lack of relationship between maternal deaths and absence of skilled birth atten-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=344 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dants in the four regions to the right in Figure 3.4.6 Clearly many factors other than
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the presence of a skilled birth attendant impact on the outcome of a delivery. Such
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=370 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ecological inferences, however limited, can provide a fruitful start for more detailed
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=383 b=0 i=0 f=f5] epidemiological work.
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=55 y=410 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Figure 3.4. Neonatal and maternal mortality are related to the absence of a skilled birth attendant6
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=55 y=423 b=0 i=0 f=f13] 100

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 44 Chapter 3
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Cross-sectional studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=111 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Cross-sectional studies measure the prevalence of disease and thus are often called
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=124 b=0 i=0 f=f5] prevalence studies. In a cross-sectional study the measurements of exposure and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=137 b=0 i=0 f=f5] effect are made at the same time. It is not easy to assess the reasons for associations
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=150 b=0 i=0 f=f5] shown in cross-sectional studies. The key question to be asked is whether the expo-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=163 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sure precedes or follows the effect. If the exposure data are known to represent
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=176 b=0 i=0 f=f5] exposure before any effect occurred, the data from a cross-sectional study can be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=189 b=0 i=0 f=f5] treated like data generated from a cohort study.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=202 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Cross-sectional studies are relatively easy and inexpensive to conduct and are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=215 b=0 i=0 f=f5] useful for investigating exposures that are fixed characteristics of individuals, such as
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=228 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ethnicity or blood group. In sudden outbreaks of disease, a cross-sectional study to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=241 b=0 i=0 f=f5] measure several exposures can be the most convenient first step in investigating
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=254 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the cause.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=267 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Data from cross-sectional studies are helpful in assessing the health care needs
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=280 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of populations. Data from repeated cross-sectional surveys using independent ran-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=293 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dom samples with standardized definitions and survey methods provide useful
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=306 b=0 i=0 f=f5] indications of trends.7,8 Each survey should have a clear purpose. Valid surveys need
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=319 b=0 i=0 f=f5] well-designed questionnaires, an appropriate sample of sufficient size, and a good
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=332 b=0 i=0 f=f5] response rate.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=345 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Many countries conduct regular cross-sectional surveys on representative sam-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=358 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ples of their populations focusing on personal and demographic characteristics,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=371 b=0 i=0 f=f5] illnesses and health-related habits. Frequency of disease and risk factors can then be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=384 b=0 i=0 f=f5] examined in relation to age, sex and ethnicity. Cross-sectional studies of risk factors
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=397 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for chronic diseases have been done in a wide range of countries (Box 3.1)
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=147 y=432 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Box 3.1. WHO Global InfoBase: an online tool
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=448 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The WHO Global InfoBase (http://infobase.who.int) is a data warehouse that collects,
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=459 b=0 i=0 f=f5] stores and displays information on chronic diseases and their risk factors (overweight/
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=470 b=0 i=0 f=f5] obesity, blood pressure, cholesterol, alcohol, tobacco use, fruit/vegetable intake, physical
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=481 b=0 i=0 f=f5] inactivity, diabetes) for 186 countries. The InfoBase was initiated in 2002 to improve the
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=492 b=0 i=0 f=f5] access of health professionals and researchers to country-reported chronic disease risk
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=503 b=0 i=0 f=f5] factor data. It has the advantage of providing traceable sources and full survey methodol-
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=514 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ogy. The following options are available online:
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=528 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120)compare countries using WHO estimates for certain risk factors
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=540 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120)make country profiles showing the most recent most nationally-representative data
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=551 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120)use a survey search tool for all country data on particular risk factors
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=587 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Case-control studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=614 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Case-control studies provide a relatively simple way to investigate causes of diseases,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=627 b=0 i=0 f=f5] especially rare diseases. They include people with a disease (or other outcome vari-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=640 b=0 i=0 f=f5] able) of interest and a suitable control (comparison or reference) group of people
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=653 b=0 i=0 f=f5] unaffected by the disease or outcome variable. The study compares the occurrence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=666 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the possible cause in cases and in controls. The investigators collect data on disease
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=679 b=0 i=0 f=f5] occurrence at one point in time and exposures at a previous point in time.

[PAGE 58]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=391 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Types of studies 45
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Case-control studies are longitudinal, in contrast to cross-sectional studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (Figure 3.5). Case-control studies have been called retrospective studies since the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] investigator is looking backward from the disease to a possible cause. This can be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] confusing because the terms retrospective and prospective are also used to describe
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the timing of data collection in relation to the current date. In this sense a case-control
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] study may be either retrospective, when all the data deal with the past, or prospective,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in which data collection continues with the passage of time.
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=55 y=192 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Figure 3.5. Design of a case-control study
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=161 y=224 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Direction of inquiry
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=238 y=243 b=1 i=0 f=f16] Start with:
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=87 y=258 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Exposed
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=244 y=265 b=1 i=0 f=f16] Cases
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=222 y=274 b=0 i=0 f=f13] (people with disease)
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=81 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Not exposed
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=322 y=307 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Population
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=87 y=332 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Exposed
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=242 y=340 b=1 i=0 f=f16] Controls
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=220 y=348 b=0 i=0 f=f13] (people without disease)
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=81 y=360 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Not exposed
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=392 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Selection of cases and controls
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=403 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A case-control study begins with the selection of cases; these cases should represent
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=416 b=0 i=0 f=f5] all the cases in a specified population group. Cases are selected on the basis of dis-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=429 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ease, not exposure. Controls are people without the disease. A critical and challenging
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=442 b=0 i=0 f=f5] aspect of population-based case control studies is finding a cost-effective way to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=455 b=0 i=0 f=f5] identify and enroll control subjects.9 The most difficult task is to select controls so
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=468 b=0 i=0 f=f5] as to sample the exposure prevalence in the population that generated the cases.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=481 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Furthermore, the choice of controls and cases must not be influenced by exposure
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=494 b=0 i=0 f=f5] status, which should be determined in the same manner for both. It is not necessary
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=507 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for cases and controls to be all-inclusive; in fact they can be restricted to any specified
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=520 b=0 i=0 f=f5] subgroup, such as elderly people, males or females.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=533 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The controls should represent people who would have been designated study
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=546 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cases if they had developed the disease. Ideally, case-control studies use new (inci-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=559 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dent) cases to avoid the difficulty of separating factors related to causation and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=572 b=0 i=0 f=f5] survival (or recovery), although studies have often been conducted using prevalence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=585 b=0 i=0 f=f5] data (for example, case-control studies of congenital malformations). Case control
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=598 b=0 i=0 f=f5] studies can estimate relative risk of disease, but they can not determine the absolute
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=611 b=0 i=0 f=f5] incidence of disease.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=634 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Exposure
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=645 b=0 i=0 f=f5] An important aspect of case-control studies is the determination of the start and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=658 b=0 i=0 f=f5] duration of exposure for cases and controls. In the case-control design, the exposure
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=671 b=0 i=0 f=f5] status of the cases is usually determined after the development of the disease

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (retrospective data) and usually by direct questioning of the affected person or a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] relative or friend (Box 3.2). The informant’s answers may be influenced by knowledge
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] about the hypothesis under investigation or the disease experience itself.

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=585 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Cohort studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=613 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Cohort studies, also called follow-up or incidence studies, begin with a group of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=626 b=0 i=0 f=f5] people who are free of disease, and who are classified into subgroups according to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=639 b=0 i=0 f=f5] exposure to a potential cause of disease or outcome (Figure 3.6). Variables of interest
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=652 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are specified and measured and the whole cohort is followed up to see how the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=665 b=0 i=0 f=f5] subsequent development of new cases of the disease (or other outcome) differs
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=678 b=0 i=0 f=f5] between the groups with and without exposure. Because the data on exposure and

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=293 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Cohort studies have been called prospective studies, but this terminology is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=306 b=0 i=0 f=f5] confusing and should be avoided. As mentioned previously, the term “prospective”
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=319 b=0 i=0 f=f5] refers to the timing of data collection and not to the relationship between exposure
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=332 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and effect. Thus there can be both prospective and retrospective cohort studies.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=345 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Cohort studies provide the best information about the causation of disease and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=358 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the most direct measurement of the risk of developing disease. Although conceptu-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=371 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ally simple, cohort studies are major undertakings and may
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=384 b=0 i=0 f=f5] require long periods of follow-up since disease may occur

[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=529 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Experimental epidemiology
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=562 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Intervention or experimentation involves attempting to change a variable in one or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=575 b=0 i=0 f=f5] more groups of people. This could mean the elimination of a dietary factor thought
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=588 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to cause allergy, or testing a new treatment on a selected group of patients. The effects
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=601 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of an intervention are measured by comparing the outcome in the experimental group
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=614 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with that in a control group. Since the interventions are strictly determined by the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=626 b=0 i=0 f=f5] study protocol, ethical considerations are of paramount importance in the design of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=640 b=0 i=0 f=f5] these studies. For example, no patient should be denied appropriate treatment as a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=653 b=0 i=0 f=f5] result of participation in an experiment, and the treatment being tested must be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=665 b=0 i=0 f=f5] acceptable in the light of current knowledge. Informed consent from study partici-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=679 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pants is required in almost all circumstances.

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=239 b=0 i=0 f=f5] An interventional study is usually designed as a randomized controlled trial, a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=252 b=0 i=0 f=f5] field trial, or a community trial.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=276 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Randomized controlled trials
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=304 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A randomized controlled trial is an epidemiological experiment designed to study the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=317 b=0 i=0 f=f5] effects of a particular intervention, usually a treatment for a specific disease (clinical
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=330 b=0 i=0 f=f5] trial). Subjects in the study population are randomly allocated to intervention and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=343 b=0 i=0 f=f5] control groups, and the results are assessed by comparing outcomes.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=356 b=0 i=0 f=f5] To ensure that the groups being compared are equivalent, patients are allocated
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=369 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to them randomly, i.e. by chance. If the initial selection and randomization is done
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=382 b=0 i=0 f=f5] properly, the control and treatment groups will be comparable at the start of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=395 b=0 i=0 f=f5] investigation; any differences between groups are chance occurrences unaffected by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=408 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the conscious or unconscious biases of the investigators.
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=438 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Figure 3.8. Design of a field trial
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=413 y=457 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Disease
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=293 y=472 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Preventive
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=291 y=480 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Intervention
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=408 y=493 b=0 i=0 f=f13] No disease

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=649 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Field trials, in contrast to clinical trials, involve people who are healthy but pre-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=662 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sumed to be at risk; data collection takes place “in the field,” usually among
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=675 b=0 i=0 f=f5] non-institutionalized people in the general population (Figure 3.8). Since the subjects

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=391 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Types of studies 51
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are disease-free and the purpose is to prevent diseases that may occur with relatively
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] low frequency, field trials are often logistically complicated and expensive endeavours.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] One of the largest field trials was that testing the Salk vaccine for the prevention of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] poliomyelitis, which involved over one million children.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Field trials can be used to evaluate interventions aimed at reducing exposure
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] without necessarily measuring the occurrence of health effects. For instance, different
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] protective methods for pesticide exposure have been tested in this way and mea-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=175 b=0 i=0 f=f5] surement of blood lead levels in children has shown the protection provided by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=188 b=0 i=0 f=f5] elimination of lead paint in the home environment. Such intervention studies can be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] done on a smaller scale, and at lower cost, as they do not involve lengthy follow-up
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or measurement of disease outcomes.

[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=621 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Potential errors in epidemiological studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=654 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemiological investigations aim to provide accurate measures of disease occur-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=667 b=0 i=0 f=f5] rence (or other outcomes). However, there are many possibilities for errors in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=680 b=0 i=0 f=f5] measurement. Epidemiologists devote much attention to minimizing errors and as-

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or systematic.
