[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=353 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Random error
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=380 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Random error is when a value of the sample measurement diverges – due to chance
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=393 b=0 i=0 f=f5] alone – from that of the true population value. Random error causes inaccurate mea-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=406 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sures of association. There are three major sources of random error:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=429 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) individual biological variation;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=441 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) sampling error; and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=453 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) measurement error.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=475 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Random error can never be completely eliminated since we can study only a sample
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=488 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the population. Sampling error is usually caused by the fact that a small sample is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=501 b=0 i=0 f=f5] not representative of all the population’s variables. The best way to reduce sampling
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=514 b=0 i=0 f=f5] error is to increase the size of the study. Individual variation always occurs and no
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=527 b=0 i=0 f=f5] measurement is perfectly accurate. Measurement error can be reduced by stringent
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=540 b=0 i=0 f=f5] protocols, and by making individual measurements as precise as possible. Investiga-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=553 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tors need to understand the measurement methods being used in the study, and the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=566 b=0 i=0 f=f5] errors that these methods can cause. Ideally, laboratories should be able to docu-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=579 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ment the accuracy and precision of their measurements by systematic quality control
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=592 b=0 i=0 f=f5] procedures.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=616 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Sample size
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=644 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The sample size must be large enough for the study to have sufficient statistical power
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=657 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to detect the differences deemed important. Sample size calculations can be done

[PAGE 66]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=391 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Types of studies 53
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with standard formulae as provided in Chapter 4. The following information is needed
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] before the calculation can be done:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=119 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) required level of statistical significance of the ability to detect a difference
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=131 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) acceptable error, or chance of missing a real effect
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=143 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) magnitude of the effect under investigation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=155 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) amount of disease in the population
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=167 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) relative sizes of the groups being compared.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=190 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In reality, sample size is often determined by logistic and financial considerations,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=203 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and a compromise always has to be made between sample size and costs. A practical
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=216 b=0 i=0 f=f5] guide to determining sample size in health studies has been published by WHO.22
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=229 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The precision of a study can also be improved by ensuring that the groups are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=242 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of appropriate relative size. This is often an issue of concern in case-control studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=255 b=0 i=0 f=f5] when a decision is required on the number of controls to be chosen for each case. It
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=268 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is not possible to be definitive about the ideal ratio of controls to cases, since this
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=281 b=0 i=0 f=f5] depends on the relative costs of accumulating cases and controls. If cases are scarce
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=294 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and controls plentiful, it is appropriate to increase the ratio of controls to cases. For
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=307 b=0 i=0 f=f5] example, in the case-control study of the effects of thalidomide (Box 3.2), 46 affected
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=320 b=0 i=0 f=f5] children were compared with 300 normal children. In general, however, there may
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=333 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be little point in having more than four controls for each case. It is important to ensure
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=346 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that there is sufficient similarity between cases and controls when the data are to be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=359 b=0 i=0 f=f5] analysed by, for example, age group or social class; if most cases and only a few
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=372 b=0 i=0 f=f5] controls were in the older age groups, the study would not be able to account for the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=385 b=0 i=0 f=f5] confounding factor of age.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=409 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Systematic error
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=437 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Systematic error (or bias) occurs in epidemiology when results differ in a systematic
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=450 b=0 i=0 f=f5] manner from the true values. A study with a small systematic error is said to have a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=463 b=0 i=0 f=f5] high accuracy. Accuracy is not affected by sample size.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=476 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The possible sources of systematic error in epidemiology are many and varied;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=489 b=0 i=0 f=f5] over 30 specific types of bias have been identified. The principal biases are:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=511 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) selection bias
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=523 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) measurement (or classification) bias.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=547 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Selection bias
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=574 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Selection bias occurs when there is a systematic difference between the characteristics
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=587 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the people selected for a study and the characteristics of those who are not. An
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=600 b=0 i=0 f=f5] obvious source of selection bias occurs when participants select themselves for a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=613 b=0 i=0 f=f5] study, either because they are unwell or because they are particularly worried about
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=626 b=0 i=0 f=f5] an exposure. It is well known, for example, that people who respond to an invitation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=639 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to participate in a study on the effects of smoking differ in their smoking habits
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=652 b=0 i=0 f=f5] from non-responders; the latter are usually heavier smokers. In studies of children’s
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=665 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health, where parental cooperation is required, selection bias may also occur. In a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=678 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cohort study of newborn children,23 successful 12-month follow-up varied

[PAGE 67]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 54 Chapter 3
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] according to income level of the parents. If individuals entering or remaining in a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] study have different characteristics from those who are not selected initially, or who
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] drop out before completion, the result is a biased estimate of the association between
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] exposure and outcome.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] An important selection bias is introduced when the disease or factor under
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] investigation itself makes people unavailable for study. For example, in a factory where
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] workers are exposed to formaldehyde, those who suffer most from eye irritation are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=175 b=0 i=0 f=f5] most likely to leave their jobs. The remaining workers are less affected and a prevalence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=188 b=0 i=0 f=f5] study of the association between formaldehyde exposure and eye irritation that is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] done only in the workplace may be very misleading.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In such occupational epidemiology studies this important selection bias is called
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=227 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the healthy worker effect (Chapter 9). Workers have to be healthy enough to perform
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=240 b=0 i=0 f=f5] their duties; the severely ill and disabled are usually excluded from employment.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=253 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Similarly, if a study is based on examinations done in a health centre and there is no
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=266 b=0 i=0 f=f5] follow-up of participants who do not turn up, biased results may be produced: unwell
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=279 b=0 i=0 f=f5] patients may be in bed either at home or in hospital. All epidemiological study designs
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=292 b=0 i=0 f=f5] need to account for selection bias.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=316 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Measurement bias
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=343 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Measurement bias occurs when the individual measurements or classifications of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease or exposure are inaccurate – that is, they do not measure correctly what they
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=369 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are supposed to measure. There are many sources of measurement bias and their
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=382 b=0 i=0 f=f5] effects are of varying importance. For instance, biochemical or physiological mea-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=395 b=0 i=0 f=f5] surements are never completely accurate and different laboratories often produce
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=408 b=0 i=0 f=f5] different results on the same specimen. If specimens from the exposed and control
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=421 b=0 i=0 f=f5] groups are analysed randomly by different laboratories, there is less chance for sys-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=434 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tematic measurement bias than in the situation where all specimens from the exposed
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=447 b=0 i=0 f=f5] group are analysed in one laboratory and all those from the control group are analysed
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=460 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in another.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=473 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A form of measurement bias of particular importance in retrospective case-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=487 b=0 i=0 f=f5] control studies is known as recall bias. This occurs when there is a differential recall
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=499 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of information by cases and controls; for instance, cases may be more likely to recall
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=512 b=0 i=0 f=f5] past exposure, especially if it is widely known to be associated with the disease under
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=526 b=0 i=0 f=f5] study – for example, lack of exercise and heart disease. Recall bias can either exag-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=538 b=0 i=0 f=f5] gerate the degree of effect associated with the exposure – as with people affected by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=551 b=0 i=0 f=f5] heart disease being more likely to admit to a past lack of exercise – or underestimate
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=564 b=0 i=0 f=f5] it – if cases are more likely than controls to deny past exposure.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=577 b=0 i=0 f=f5] If measurement bias occurs equally in the groups being compared, it almost
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=590 b=0 i=0 f=f5] always results in an underestimate of the true strength of the relationship. Such
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=603 b=0 i=0 f=f5] non-differential bias may account for apparent discrepancies in the results of different
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=616 b=0 i=0 f=f5] epidemiological studies.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=629 b=0 i=0 f=f5] If the investigator, laboratory technician or the participant knows the exposure
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=642 b=0 i=0 f=f5] status, this knowledge can influence measurements and cause observer bias. To avoid
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=655 b=0 i=0 f=f5] this bias, measurements can be made in a blind or double-blind fashion. A blind
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=669 b=0 i=0 f=f5] study means that the investigators do not know how participants are classified.

[PAGE 68]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=391 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Types of studies 55
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A double-blind study means that neither the investigators, nor the participants, know
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] how the latter are classified.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=119 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Confounding
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=152 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Confounding is another major issue in epidemiological studies. In a study of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=165 b=0 i=0 f=f5] association between exposure to a cause (or risk factor) and the occurrence of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=178 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease, confounding can occur when another exposure exists in the study popu-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=191 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lation and is associated both with the disease and the exposure being studied. A
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=204 b=0 i=0 f=f5] problem arises if this extraneous factor – itself a determinant or risk factor for the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=217 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health outcome – is unequally distributed between the exposure subgroups. Con-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=230 b=0 i=0 f=f5] founding occurs when the effects of two exposures (risk factors) have not been
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=243 b=0 i=0 f=f5] separated and the analysis concludes that the effect is due to one variable rather than
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=256 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the other. To be a confounding factor, two conditions must be met (Figure 3.10).
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=55 y=286 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Figure 3.10. Confounding: relationship between coffee drinking (exposure), heart
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=55 y=297 b=1 i=0 f=f11] disease (outcome), and a third variable (tobacco use)
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=118 y=310 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Exposure Disease
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=106 y=320 b=0 i=0 f=f13] (coffee drinking) (heart disease)
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=173 y=338 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Confounded association
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=114 y=362 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Two True
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=114 y=373 b=0 i=0 f=f13] exposures association
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=114 y=384 b=0 i=0 f=f13] associated with the disease
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=114 y=394 b=0 i=0 f=f13] with each other Confounding
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=203 y=405 b=0 i=0 f=f13] variable
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=194 y=416 b=0 i=0 f=f13] (tobacco use)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=444 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Confounding arises because non-random distribution of risk factors in the source
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=457 b=0 i=0 f=f5] population also occurs in the study population thus providing misleading estimates
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=470 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of effect (see Box 3.7). In this sense, it might appear to be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=483 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a bias, but in fact it does not result from systematic error
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=289 y=488 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Box 3.7. Confounding: difficult to control
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=496 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in research design.25
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=289 y=503 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The word “confounding” comes from the Latin confun-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=509 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Age and social class are often confounders in epi-
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=288 y=514 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dere, meaning to mix together. Confounding can have
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=522 b=0 i=0 f=f5] demiological studies. An association between high blood
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=289 y=525 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a very important influence, and may even change the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=535 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pressure and coronary heart disease may in truth represent apparent direction of an association. A variable that ap-
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=547 b=0 i=0 f=f5] concomitant changes in the two variables that occur with pears to be protective may, after control of confounding,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=558 b=0 i=0 f=f5] increasing age; the potential confounding effect of age has be found to be harmful. The most common concern
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=289 y=569 b=0 i=0 f=f5] about confounding is that it may create the appearance
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=574 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to be considered, and when this is done it is seen that high
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=289 y=580 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of a cause-effect relationship that does not actually exist.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=587 b=0 i=0 f=f5] blood pressure indeed increases the risk of coronary heart
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=289 y=591 b=0 i=0 f=f5] For a variable to be a confounder, it must, in its own
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=600 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease. right, be a determinant of the occurrence of disease (i.e.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=73 y=613 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In the example in Figure 3.10, confounding may be a risk factor) and associated with the exposure under
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=624 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the explanation for the relationship demonstrated be- investigation. Thus, in a study of radon exposure and
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=289 y=636 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lung cancer, smoking is not a confounder if the smoking
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=639 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tween coffee drinking and the risk of coronary heart
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=289 y=646 b=0 i=0 f=f5] habits are identical in the radon-exposed and control
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=652 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease, since it is known that coffee consumption is as-
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=289 y=658 b=0 i=0 f=f5] groups.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=665 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sociated with tobacco use: people who drink coffee are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=678 b=0 i=0 f=f5] more likely to smoke than people who do not drink coffee.

[PAGE 69]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 56 Chapter 3
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] It is also well known that cigarette smoking is a cause of coronary heart disease.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] It is thus possible that the relationship between coffee drinking and coronary heart
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease merely reflects the known causal association of tobacco use and heart
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease. In this situation, smoking confounds the apparent relationship between
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] coffee consumption and coronary heart disease because smoking is correlated with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] coffee drinking and is a risk factor even for those who do not drink coffee.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=173 b=0 i=0 f=f10] The control of confounding
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Several methods are available to control confounding, either through study design or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f5] during the analysis of results.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=226 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The methods commonly used to control confounding in the design of an epi-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=240 b=0 i=0 f=f5] demiological study are:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=262 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) randomization
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=274 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) restriction
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=286 b=0 i=0 f=f20] (cid:120) matching.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=309 b=0 i=0 f=f5] At the analysis stage, confounding can be controlled by:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=331 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) stratification
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=343 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) statistical modeling.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=366 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Randomization
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=376 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In experimental studies, randomization is the ideal method for ensuring that potential
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=390 b=0 i=0 f=f5] confounding variables are equally distributed among the groups being compared. The
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=403 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sample sizes have to be sufficiently large to avoid random maldistribution of such
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=415 b=0 i=0 f=f5] variables. Randomization avoids the association between potentially confounding
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=428 b=0 i=0 f=f5] variables and the exposure that is being considered.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=452 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Restriction
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=462 b=0 i=0 f=f5] One way to control confounding is to limit the study to people who have particular
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=475 b=0 i=0 f=f5] characteristics. For example, in a study on the effects of coffee on coronary heart
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=489 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease, participation in the study could be restricted to nonsmokers, thus removing
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=501 b=0 i=0 f=f5] any potential effect of confounding by cigarette smoking.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=525 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Matching
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=535 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Matching is used to control confounding by selecting study participants so as to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=549 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ensure that potential confounding variables are evenly distributed in the two groups
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=562 b=0 i=0 f=f5] being compared. For example, in a case-control study of exercise and coronary heart
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=574 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease, each patient with heart disease can be matched with a control of the same
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=587 b=0 i=0 f=f5] age group and sex to ensure that confounding by age and sex does not occur. Match-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=601 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ing has been used extensively in case-control studies but it can lead to problems in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=613 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the selection of controls if the matching criteria are too strict or too numerous; this
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=626 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is called overmatching.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=639 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Matching can be expensive and time-consuming, but is particularly useful if the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=652 b=0 i=0 f=f5] danger exists of there being no overlap between cases and controls, such as in a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=665 b=0 i=0 f=f5] situation where the cases are likely to be older than the controls.

[PAGE 70]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=391 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Types of studies 57
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=84 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Stratification and statistical modelling
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=95 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In large studies it is usually preferable to control for confounding in the analytical
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=108 b=0 i=0 f=f5] phase rather than in the design phase. Confounding can then be controlled by strat-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=121 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ification, which involves the measurement of the strength of associations in well
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=134 b=0 i=0 f=f5] defined and homogeneous categories (strata) of the confounding variable. If age is a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=147 b=0 i=0 f=f5] confounder, the association may be measured in, say, 10-year age groups; if sex or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=160 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ethnicity is a confounder, the association is measured separately in men and women
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=173 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or in the different ethnic groups. Methods are available for summarizing the overall
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=186 b=0 i=0 f=f5] association by producing a weighted average of the estimates calculated in each
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=199 b=0 i=0 f=f5] separate stratum.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=212 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Although stratification is conceptually simple and relatively easy to carry out, it
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=225 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is often limited by the size of the study and it can not help to control many factors
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=238 b=0 i=0 f=f5] simultaneously, as is often necessary. In this situation, multivariate statistical mod-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=251 b=0 i=0 f=f5] eling is required to estimate the strength of the associations while controlling for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=264 b=0 i=0 f=f5] several confounding variables simultaneously; a range of statistical techniques is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=277 b=0 i=0 f=f5] available for these analyses (Chapter 4).
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=301 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Validity
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=328 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Validity is an expression of the degree to which a test is capable of measuring what
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=342 b=0 i=0 f=f5] it is intended to measure. A study is valid if its results correspond to the truth; there
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=354 b=0 i=0 f=f5] should be no systematic error and the random error should be as small as possible.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=367 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Figure 3.11 indicates the relationship between the true value and measured values for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=380 b=0 i=0 f=f5] low and high validity and reliability. With low reliability but high validity the measured
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=393 b=0 i=0 f=f5] values are spread out, but the mean of the measured values is close to the true value.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=406 b=0 i=0 f=f5] On the other hand, a high reliability (or repeatability) of the measurements does not
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=419 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ensure validity since they may all be far from the true value. There are two types of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=433 b=0 i=0 f=f5] validity: internal and external.
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=55 y=462 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Figure 3.11. Validity and reliability
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=227 y=475 b=1 i=0 f=f12] Validity
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=177 y=492 b=0 i=0 f=f13] High Low
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=159 y=514 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Measured values Measured values
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=104 y=535 b=0 i=0 f=f13] High
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=168 y=564 b=0 i=0 f=f13] True value True value
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=66 y=573 b=1 i=0 f=f12] Reliability
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=159 y=589 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Measured values Measured values
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=104 y=611 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Low
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=168 y=640 b=0 i=0 f=f13] True value True value

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 58 Chapter 3
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=84 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Internal validity
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=95 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Internal validity is the degree to which the results of an observation are correct for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=108 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the particular group of people being studied. For example, measurements of blood
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=121 b=0 i=0 f=f5] haemoglobin must distinguish accurately participants with anaemia as defined in the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=134 b=0 i=0 f=f5] study. Analysis of the blood in a different laboratory may produce different results
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=147 b=0 i=0 f=f5] because of systematic error, but the evaluation of associations with anaemia, as mea-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=160 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sured by one laboratory, may still be internally valid.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=173 b=0 i=0 f=f5] For a study to be of any use it must be internally valid, although even a study
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=186 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that is perfectly valid internally may be of no consequence because the results can
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=199 b=0 i=0 f=f5] not be compared with other studies. Internal validity can be threatened by all sources
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=212 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of systematic error but can be improved by good design and attention to detail.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=235 b=1 i=1 f=f18] External validity
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=246 b=0 i=0 f=f5] External validity or generalizability is the extent to which the results of a study apply
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=259 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to people not in it (or, for example, to laboratories not involved in it). Internal validity
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=272 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is necessary for, but does not guarantee, external validity, and is easier to achieve.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] External validity requires external quality control of the measurements and judgements
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=298 b=0 i=0 f=f5] about the degree to which the results of a study can be extrapolated. This does
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=311 b=0 i=0 f=f5] not require that the study sample be representative of a reference population. For
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=324 b=0 i=0 f=f5] example, evidence that the effect of lowering blood cholesterol in men is also relevant
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=337 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to women requires a judgment about the external validity of studies in men. External
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=350 b=0 i=0 f=f5] validity is assisted by study designs that examine clearly-stated hypotheses in well
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=363 b=0 i=0 f=f5] defined populations. The external validity of a study is supported if similar results are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=376 b=0 i=0 f=f5] found in studies in other populations.24
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=400 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Ethical issues
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=428 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Ethical issues are those involving actions and policies that are right or wrong, fair or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=441 b=0 i=0 f=f5] unfair, just or unjust. Ethical dilemmas arise frequently in the practice of epidemiology
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=454 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and ethical principles govern the conduct of epidemiology, as they do all human
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=467 b=0 i=0 f=f5] activities. Guidelines on the general conduct of research on human beings is discussed
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=480 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in Chapter 11. Research and monitoring are essential to ensure that public health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=493 b=0 i=0 f=f5] interventions do not have serious unintended and harmful consequences as occurred
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=506 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in Bangladesh following the installation of wells (Box 3.8).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=519 b=0 i=0 f=f5] All epidemiological studies must be reviewed and approved by ethical review
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=532 b=0 i=0 f=f5] committees (see Chapter 11). The ethical principles that apply to epidemiologic prac-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=545 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tice and research include:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=567 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) informed consent
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=579 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) confidentiality
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=591 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) respect for human rights
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=603 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) scientific integrity.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=626 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Informed consent
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=637 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Free and voluntary informed consent must be obtained from participants in epidemi-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=650 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ological studies and they must retain the right to withdraw at any time. However, it
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=663 b=0 i=0 f=f5] may prove impracticable for informed consent to be given for access to routine med-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=676 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ical records. In such cases, as is the norm in any other research study, epidemiologists

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=391 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Types of studies 59
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=62 y=92 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Box 3.8. Unintended consequences: arsenic in tube wells in Bangladesh
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=107 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The installation of tube wells to improve rural standards of water and hygiene in Bangladesh
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=118 b=0 i=0 f=f5] over the past few decades, has been an important element in the control of cholera and
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=129 b=0 i=0 f=f5] other waterborne enteric diseases. Although 95% of the population now relies on ground-
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=140 b=0 i=0 f=f5] water from these wells, no testing for microbial counts, heavy metals or toxic chemicals
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=151 b=0 i=0 f=f5] was carried out in the initial stages. It was only in 1985 when a local physician in West
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Bengal, India, began noticing patients with clinical signs of arsenic intoxication (skin pig-
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=173 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mentation and increased rates of a variety of cancers), that the tube wells were checked.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=184 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Currently about 30 million people, one quarter of Bangladesh’s population, are drinking
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=195 b=0 i=0 f=f5] water with significantly high levels of arsenic. All of the possible interventions to lower
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=206 b=0 i=0 f=f5] arsenic intake from water (treating water at the pump, in-home treatment of water, com-
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=217 b=0 i=0 f=f5] munity level water treatment, sealing wells with high arsenic content, and sinking deeper
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=228 b=0 i=0 f=f5] wells below the water table with high arsenic content) are either costly or require contin-
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=62 y=239 b=0 i=0 f=f5] uing maintenance and monitoring.25, 26
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=272 b=0 i=0 f=f5] must respect personal privacy and confidentiality at all times. They have an obligation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to tell communities what they are doing and why, and to transmit the results of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=298 b=0 i=0 f=f5] studies, and their significance, to the communities involved. All proposals for epi-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=311 b=0 i=0 f=f5] demiological studies should be submitted to properly constituted institutional ethics
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=324 b=0 i=0 f=f5] committees before the research work begins.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=347 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Confidentiality
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=358 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemiologists also have an obligation to preserve confidentiality of information
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=371 b=0 i=0 f=f5] they obtain through their studies. This also extends to the right of a person to with-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=384 b=0 i=0 f=f5] hold information from others. As information in medical records, case registers, and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=397 b=0 i=0 f=f5] other data files and databases are generally confidential, epidemiologists are required
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=410 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to obtain permission before being given access to these data.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=433 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Respect for individual rights
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=444 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Tension can often arise in epidemiological studies between the interests of the group
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=457 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and the interests of the individual. An example is provided by efforts to limit the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=470 b=0 i=0 f=f5] public health impact of HIV/AIDS. Cuba successfully contained the spread of HIV/
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=483 b=0 i=0 f=f5] AIDS by testing individuals at risk and segregating infected people from the general
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=496 b=0 i=0 f=f5] population.27 Others argue that individual human rights are key to preventing infec-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=509 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tion because spread of disease was facilitated by their denial; for example, women in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=522 b=0 i=0 f=f5] many affected countries cannot refuse demands for unprotected sex. In addition,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=535 b=0 i=0 f=f5] much of the behaviour that places individuals at risk of HIV/AIDS happens in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=548 b=0 i=0 f=f5] private, beyond the reach of the state. Public health efforts to modify the behaviour
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=561 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of vulnerable people are unlikely to be successful without assurances that their in-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=574 b=0 i=0 f=f5] terests will be protected.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=597 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Scientific integrity
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=608 b=0 i=0 f=f5] All scientists have the potential to behave in an unethical manner, perhaps in part
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=621 b=0 i=0 f=f5] because of the pressure to succeed. Epidemiologists are not immune to unethical
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=634 b=0 i=0 f=f5] behaviour. Examples include research results apparently influenced by conflict of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=647 b=0 i=0 f=f5] interests and the publication of fabricated data.28, 29 Minimization of unethical be-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=660 b=0 i=0 f=f5] haviour requires vigilance on the part of ethical review committees and close

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 60 Chapter 3
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] attention to peer review of publications.30 The training and mentoring of epidemiol-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ogists must include serious and repeated discussion of these issues.

[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=85 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Chapter 4
[L s=31.9 r=3.20 x=55 y=102 b=0 i=0 f=f7] Basic biostatistics:
[L s=31.9 r=3.20 x=55 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f7] concepts and tools
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=181 b=0 i=0 f=f10] O. Dale Williams
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=158 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Key messages
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=244 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Basic epidemiology requires a knowledge of biostatistics.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=256 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Good quality tables and graphs provide effective means of communicating
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=270 b=0 i=0 f=f5] data.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=279 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Confidence intervals are valuable estimation tools and can be used to test
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=292 b=0 i=0 f=f5] hypotheses.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=302 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Although calculations can be complex, the concepts underlying statistical
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=315 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tests are often quite simple.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=344 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Biostatistical concepts and tools are needed for summarizing and analysing data.1-5
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Doing and interpreting epidemiological research requires the use of samples to make
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=370 b=0 i=0 f=f5] inferences aboutpopulations. This chapter will describe basic concepts and methods,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=383 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and how to summarize data.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=396 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In the event that students wish more details on these basic concepts, there are nu-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=409 b=0 i=0 f=f5] merous online courses and texts freely available; see Chapter 11 for some suggestions.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=422 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Before describing the basic concepts and tools, it is useful to become familiar
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=435 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with the various methods for interpreting and communicating data. This section is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=448 b=0 i=0 f=f5] intended to provide the most common ways of summarizing data; examples in other
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=460 b=0 i=0 f=f5] chapters are used to illustrate the general principles.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=483 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Summarizing data
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=515 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Data exist as either numerical or categorical variables.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=537 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Numerical variables include counts, such as the number of children of a spe-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=549 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cific age, and measurements,such as height and weight.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=560 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Categorical variables are the result of classifying. For instance, individuals can
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=572 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be classified into categories according to their blood group; A, B, O, or AB.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=583 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Ordinal data – which express ranks– are a type of categorical data.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=605 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Tables and graphs can be used for summarizing data. Summary numbers include
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=618 b=0 i=0 f=f5] medians, means, ranges, standard deviations, standard errors and variances. These
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=631 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are explained below, along with suggestions and cautions as to their appropriate use.

[PAGE 77]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 64 Chapter 4
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Tables and graphs
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=112 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Tables and graphs are important means of summarizing and displaying data, but they
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=125 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are seldom prepared with sufficient care. Their purpose is to display data in a way
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=138 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that can be quickly and easily understood. Each table or graph should contain enough
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=151 b=0 i=0 f=f5] information so that it can be interpreted without reference to the text.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=256 y=164 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Titles play a critical role in making a table or graph
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=239 y=177 b=0 i=0 f=f5] useful to the reader. Titles should specifically describe the
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=34 y=185 b=1 i=0 f=f4] Box 4.1. The advantages of graphs versus tables
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=239 y=190 b=0 i=0 f=f5] numbers included in the cells of a table or represented by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Graphs have the advantages of: the points plotted on a graph. For tables, the title should
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=47 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •simplicity and clarity clearly state what the numbers in the cell represent, how
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=47 y=226 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •memorable visual images the cells are classified, and where and when the data were
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=47 y=237 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •being able to show complex relationships.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=239 y=242 b=0 i=0 f=f5] collected. A common problem is for the title to state the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=253 b=0 i=0 f=f5] They also emphasize numbers and tend to be popular, purpose of the table or graph rather than to describe what
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=264 b=0 i=0 f=f5] as evidenced by their use in general publications where
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=239 y=267 b=0 i=0 f=f5] it contains.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=275 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tables are rarely used.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=256 y=280 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemiologists often have to decide how to present
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=286 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Tables have the advantages of:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=239 y=293 b=0 i=0 f=f5] data and whether to choose a table or a graph. While these
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=47 y=298 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •displaying more complex data with precision and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=239 y=306 b=0 i=0 f=f5] two share some common features, one is likely to be more
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=53 y=311 b=0 i=0 f=f5] flexibility
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=47 y=319 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •requiring less technical skill or facilities to suitable than the other in specific cases (Box 4.1).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=53 y=332 b=0 i=0 f=f5] prepare There are several types of graphs to consider. Here are
[L s=9.5 r=0.95 x=47 y=344 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •using less space for a given amount of information. some of the more popular, along with some guidance on
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=239 y=358 b=0 i=0 f=f5] their use.
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=147 y=403 b=1 i=0 f=f4] Box 4.2. World health chart
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=419 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The world health chart (http://www.gapminder.org/) shows global health development by
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=430 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a series of interactive charts linked to existing data. These charts are designed to promote
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=441 b=0 i=0 f=f5] better use of such data, informadvocacy efforts and stimulate hypothesis generation. The
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=452 b=0 i=0 f=f5] charts show time trends in a dynamic fashion, similar to a computer game. The world
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=463 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health chart can help to answer questions such as:
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=476 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •How do wealth and health relate historically?
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=487 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •Has the world become healthier over the past 50-100 years?
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=499 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •How have the differences in health between countries changed?
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=536 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Pie charts and component band charts
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=563 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Pie charts (Figure 7.1) and componentband charts (Figure 6.2) display how a whole
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=576 b=0 i=0 f=f5] entity is divided into its parts. A pie chart represents this information with a circle
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=589 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and a component band chartrepresents it with a bar – both are divided into sections
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=602 b=0 i=0 f=f5] representing the different components. For pie charts, a useful rule is to place the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=615 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pieces of the pie in order according to their size, starting at the equivalent of twelve
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=628 b=0 i=0 f=f5] o’clock and then progressing clockwise. In general, it is better to use component band
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=641 b=0 i=0 f=f5] charts for comparing how twoor more whole entities are divided into their component
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=654 b=0 i=0 f=f5] parts than it is to place pie charts side by side.

[PAGE 78]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=319 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Basic biostatistics: concepts and tools 65
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Spot maps and rate maps
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=112 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Spot maps and rate maps display geographical locations of cases or rates. John Snow
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=125 b=0 i=0 f=f5] used this spot map to display where the cases of cholera occurred relative to the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=138 b=0 i=0 f=f5] famous pump (Figure 4.1). Rate maps are slightly different in that geographical areas
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=151 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are shaded according to thedifferences in values;prevalence, incidence or mortality
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=164 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are often shown on rate maps. Areas with the highest rates are typically shaded with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=177 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the darkest shades or the brightest colors (Figure 4.2).
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=55 y=206 b=1 i=0 f=f4] Figure 4.1. Deaths from cholera in central London, September 18546,7
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=518 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Maps, charts, and atlases are used todisplay data in both a static form – such
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=531 b=0 i=0 f=f5] as in the Mental Health Atlas, the Tobacco Atlas, and the Cancer Atlas – and in an
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=544 b=0 i=0 f=f5] interactive form (Box 4.2), but these will not be discussed further in this chapter. A
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=557 b=0 i=0 f=f5] free online coursefor use of interactivemaps based on datafrom the Human Devel-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=569 b=0 i=0 f=f5] opment Report can be found at http://hdr.undp.org/statistics/data/animation.cfm.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=593 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Bar charts
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=621 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Bar charts are best suited for displaying numbers or percentages that compare two
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=634 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or more categories of data, such as the proportions of male to female smokers. The
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=647 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lengths of the bars convey the essence of this comparison so that any alterations
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=660 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or distortions of these lengths – such as scale breaks – are usually inappropriate
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=673 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (Box 4.3).

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=607 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Frequency distributions and histograms
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=635 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A frequency distribution is the organization of a data set into contiguous mutually
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=648 b=0 i=0 f=f5] exclusive intervals sothat the number or proportion of observations falling in each
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=661 b=0 i=0 f=f5] interval is apparent. They are often displayed with a histogram, which looks like a bar
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=674 b=0 i=0 f=f5] chart with all the bars stacked together in an orderly fashion, with no space between

[PAGE 80]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=319 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Basic biostatistics: concepts and tools 67
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=85 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the bars (Figure 6.7). The heights of the bars represent either the number or percent
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=98 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of observations within each interval. The overall shape of this distribution can be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=111 b=0 i=0 f=f5] highly informative. Frequency polygons, which are essentially a line that connects
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=124 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the middle of each of the bars of the histogram, are also used extensively. The bell-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] shaped curve of the normal distribution is one important example (Figure 4.3).

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=356 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Normal distributions
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=384 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The normal distribution has extremely useful characteristics. A large number of sta-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=397 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tistical tests and calculations can be used if the observations follow a normal
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=410 b=0 i=0 f=f5] distribution. It is useful to know that about two-thirds offthe values under a normal
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=422 b=0 i=0 f=f5] distribution curve fall within one standard deviation (SD) of the mean, and approxi-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=435 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mately 95% fall within two standard deviations of the mean.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=458 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Summary numbers
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=491 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Means, medians and mode
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=519 b=0 i=0 f=f5] One set of summary numbers are those that measure central tendency, that is, they
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=532 b=0 i=0 f=f5] attempt to characterize the center of a sample of measurements.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=555 b=1 i=1 f=f15] The mean
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=566 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Most prominent, and often the most appropriate, is the sample average or mean,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=579 b=0 i=0 f=f5] which for a sample with n values for a variable such as x ==body weight, would be:
[L s=7.0 r=0.70 x=265 y=583 b=0 i=0 f=f5] i
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=173 y=606 b=0 i=1 f=f21] mean =
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=207 y=601 b=0 i=1 f=f21] x=∑n
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=233 y=607 b=0 i=1 f=f21] x/n
[L s=6.0 r=0.60 x=237 y=614 b=0 i=1 f=f21] i
[L s=6.0 r=0.60 x=221 y=619 b=0 i=1 f=f21] i=1
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=641 b=1 i=1 f=f15] The median
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=652 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The median is defined as the middle after all the measurements have been put in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=665 b=0 i=0 f=f5] order according to their values. The median is especially useful when a few values
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=678 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are much larger than others. For this reason, personal income tends tobe reported as

[PAGE 81]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 68 Chapter 4
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=85 b=0 i=0 f=f5] median income rather than average income, since the median is not affected unduly
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=98 b=0 i=0 f=f5] by the very high incomes for a very few members of the sample. Note that income
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=111 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for a country is sometimesreported as per capita income. This number can be quite
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=124 b=0 i=0 f=f5] different from median income which is the middle of the distribution of the individual
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] incomes, most of which are likely to represent the income supporting an entire family,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] whereas per capita income represents these incomes averaged over the number of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] persons in the country.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=185 b=1 i=1 f=f15] The mode
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=196 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Another important measure is the mode,, which is the valueof the measurement in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=209 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the sample that occurs most frequently.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=233 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Variances, standard deviations and standard errors
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=261 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Measures of variability are another group of summary numbers. The threemost useful
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=274 b=0 i=0 f=f5] measures of variability are the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=295 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • variance
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=307 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • standard deviation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=319 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • standard error.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=343 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Each of these is related to how unlike each other the individuals are in a sample of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=356 b=0 i=0 f=f5] measurements. Thesemeasures of variability can be calculated on;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=377 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • the differences between members for all possible pairs of measurements in the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=389 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sample or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=400 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • the difference between each observation in the sample and the sample mean,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=412 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that is((( )2 : the squareddeviation from the mean.
[L s=4.7 r=0.47 x=196 y=419 b=0 i=1 f=f21] i
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=436 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Such calculations, while appealing, are rather cumbersome. An algebraic equivalent
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=449 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is often used. This is a formula for the sample variance – with subscripts dropped for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=462 b=0 i=0 f=f5] simplicity:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=276 y=481 b=0 i=0 f=f22] ∑x2222 (( ∑ x)2/n
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=256 y=490 b=0 i=1 f=f21] s2=
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=298 y=495 b=0 i=1 f=f21] n−1
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=520 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The numerator of theabove equation can also be written as:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=226 y=540 b=0 i=0 f=f22] SS (x)=∑ (x−x)2=∑ x2−( ∑ x)2/n
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=571 b=0 i=0 f=f5] This term is often called the sum of squared deviations about the mean, or simply the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=251 y=592 b=0 i=0 f=f22] Sum of Squares = SS(x).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=615 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Note that the variance is very close tothe average of the squared deviations from the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=629 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mean. The standard deviation is simply the square root of the variance or s s2 and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=643 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the standard error, which is:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=264 y=665 b=0 i=1 f=f21] SE s s// n,
[L s=6.0 r=0.60 x=287 y=671 b=0 i=1 f=f21] xx

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=85 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is typically called the standard error of the mean. The standard error of the mean
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=98 b=0 i=0 f=f5] reflects how unlike each other all possible means of samples of size n might be if
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=111 b=0 i=0 f=f5] each sample were randomly selected from the same population as the initial sample.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=133 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Basic concepts of statistical inference
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=165 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The process of using a sample to make inferences about a population is perhaps the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=178 b=0 i=0 f=f5] most vital aspect of epidemiologic research. The conceptual underpinnings for sta-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=191 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tistical inference are based on the process of taking a single random sample of a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=204 b=0 i=0 f=f5] specific size from a population and using this sample to make judgments about the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=217 b=0 i=0 f=f5] population as a whole. Typically, these judgments are made in terms of means, vari-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=230 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ances or other summarizing numbers. Summarizing numbers for the population are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=243 b=0 i=0 f=f5] called parameters and are represented byGreek letters such as:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=264 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • μ = mean,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=276 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • (cid:305) = standard deviation and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=288 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • ß = regression coefficient.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=312 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Estimates of these parameters obtained from a sample are represented by x, s and b,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=327 b=0 i=0 f=f5] respectively.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=351 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Using samples to understand populations
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=378 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Random samples
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=389 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The process of selectinga sample from a population is essential to statistical inference.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=402 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The first step is to select a random sample, whereby each member of the population
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=415 b=0 i=0 f=f5] has an equal chance of being selected for the sample (see Chapter 3). There are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=428 b=0 i=0 f=f5] numerous sampling strategies and texts to help guide this process.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=441 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Example: calculating a sample mean
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=454 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 10 people are randomly selected from a population and their weights are mea-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=467 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sured in kilograms as 82.3, 67.3, 68.6, 57.7, 67.3, 60.5, 61.8, 54.5, 73.2 and 85.9 so
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=480 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that
[L s=10.5 r=1.05 x=182 y=501 b=0 i=0 f=f23] ∑n
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=507 b=0 i=0 f=f22] x xx /// 67.9 kg,
[L s=6.0 r=0.60 x=199 y=513 b=0 i=1 f=f21] ii
[L s=6.0 r=0.60 x=184 y=519 b=0 i=1 f=f21] i=1
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=531 b=0 i=0 f=f5] which is an estimate of μ = population mean weight.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=552 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Of course, another random sample selected from this same population and the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=565 b=0 i=0 f=f5] weights measured for this new group could lead to a different sample mean; say,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=56 y=578 b=0 i=0 f=f5] x = 68.2 kg, as an estimate of the same population mean, μ. One of these sample
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=593 b=0 i=0 f=f5] means is not better than the other, but this does raise the question as to the value of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=606 b=0 i=0 f=f5] an individual sample mean as an estimate of the population mean when it is so easy
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=619 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to take another sample and obtain a different value for x. To put this into context,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=634 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the value is derived from the processused to obtain the estimate.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=647 b=0 i=0 f=f5] If this process were repeated a very large number of times, a very long list of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=660 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sample means could be calculated (Box 4.4). How well a sample mean estimates the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=673 b=0 i=0 f=f5] population mean can be assessed by examining the characteristics of this long list of

[PAGE 83]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 70 Chapter 4
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=85 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sample means. If the mean of all these sample means, that is the mean of the means,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=98 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is the same as the population mean, then the sample mean is an unbiased estimate
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=111 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the population mean. That is, on average, it provides the correct answer.

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=239 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to prepare and relatively easy to understand.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=221 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mean. The standard deviation for this long list of sample
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=232 b=0 i=0 f=f5] means, a measure of how similar these sample means

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=646 b=0 i=0 f=f5] confidence interval obtained from the study sample is tios, among many others. The interpretation is similar to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=658 b=0 i=0 f=f5] one of the 95% or one of the 5%. that described below for means. Interpreting a confidence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=239 y=670 b=0 i=0 f=f5] interval can sometimes bea bit confusing (Box 4.5).

[PAGE 84]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=319 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Basic biostatistics: concepts and tools 71
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=83 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Interpreting measures outside the confidence interval
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=93 b=0 i=0 f=f5] When interpreting confidence intervals, one needs to know what to do with measures
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=105 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that fall outside of the interval. In the example of weights, data range from 54.5 to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=117 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 85.9 and the 95% confidence interval from 60.61 to 75.19. Is it reasonable to believe
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=129 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that the population mean could be 80.0 kg? One would expect that 95% of such
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=140 b=0 i=0 f=f5] confidence intervals would in fact contain the population mean. It seems unlikely
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=152 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that the population mean is μ = 80.0 kg, although it could be, if this interval happens
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=164 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to be one of the 5% rather than one of the 95%. Even though there is some risk in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=176 b=0 i=0 f=f5] claiming that μ ≠ 80.0 kg, the risk is small, and further, it was set to be small when
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=54 y=191 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:734)(cid:3)= 0.05 was used to create a 95% confidence interval. It is important to understand
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=203 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that this risk of saying that μ ≠ 80.0 (when in fact it is 80.0 kg) is set by the investigator
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=218 b=0 i=0 f=f5] who calculates the confidence interval. Values other than (cid:734)(cid:3)= 0.05 can be used, with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=54 y=231 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:734)(cid:3)= 0.01 being perhaps the other value most commonly used; however (cid:734)(cid:3)= 0.05 is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=242 b=0 i=0 f=f5] most common and most readily accepted. Figure 5.2. shows an example of a confi-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=254 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dence interval.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=69 y=267 b=0 i=0 f=f5] We can use a confidence interval to test a hypothesis, namely, the hypothesis
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=280 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that μ = 80.0 kg. In this case, the hypothesis was tested and rejected based on the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=293 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lower and upper limits of the confidence interval. In general, confidence intervals can
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=306 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be used in this way to test hypotheses; however, there is a more formal approach
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=319 b=0 i=0 f=f5] described in Box 4.6.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=343 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Hypothesis tests, p-values, statistical power
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=370 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Hypothesis-testing is relatively straightforward. We need to make a careful statement
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=383 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the statistical hypothesis to be tested, the p-value associated with this test and the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=396 b=0 i=0 f=f5] statistical power the test has for “detecting” a difference of a specified magnitude.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=420 b=0 i=0 f=f10] The p-value
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=448 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In the above situation, the null hypothesis was rejected because the observed out-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=461 b=0 i=0 f=f5] come was deemed to be too unlikely or rare under the assumption that the null
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=474 b=0 i=0 f=f5] hypothesis was true. The cut point for rarity in this circumstance was set when the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=54 y=488 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:734)-level was set at (cid:734) = 0.05, for which the cut points are -2.26 and +2.26. A more
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=501 b=0 i=0 f=f5] precise measure of the rarity of this observed outcome, again under the assump-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=514 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tion that the null hypothesis is true, can be obtained readily. It is simply the area
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=527 b=0 i=0 f=f5] below -3.75 plus the area above +3.75 in a t distribution with 9 degrees of freedom.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=540 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The area below -3.75 is 0.002, the area above +3.75 is also 0.002 so that the total is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=553 b=0 i=0 f=f5] p = 0.004. This area is called the p-value and it represents the likelihood that a value
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=566 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for the mean of a random sample from this population, would be as far away as 67.9
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=579 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or farther from μ = 80 kg on either side. That is, the observed outcome is so rare that
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=593 b=0 i=0 f=f5] it is difficult to believe that μ = 80 kg. The p-value and (cid:734)-level are related in the sense
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=607 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that if (cid:734) = 0.05, then the null hypothesis would be rejected when p < 0.05.

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=671 b=0 i=0 f=f5] hypothesis:

[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=307 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Basic methods
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=339 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The basic methods for epidemiology are:

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=474 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are sufficiently different to lead one to conclude thatthe two populations they rep-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=487 b=0 i=0 f=f5] resent have different means. The t-test uses a statistic that, under the null hypothesis,

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Correlation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=112 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In general, correlation quantifies the degree to which two

[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=288 y=134 b=1 i=0 f=f4] two variables

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=150 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the value for the other. If they are correlated, then the value It is always useful to examine a picture of the relation-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=161 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for one is related to the value of the other, either high when ship between the two variables with a scatter plot (see
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=288 y=172 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Figure 1.1). Plots with groups of dots in more than one
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=177 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the other is high or being high when the other is low. There
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=288 y=183 b=0 i=0 f=f5] location or with dots that seemto fall along a curved
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=190 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are several tools available for measuring correlation. The
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=288 y=194 b=0 i=0 f=f5] line may well imply that the correlation coefficient is not
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=203 b=0 i=0 f=f5] most commonly used is the Pearson Product Moment providing a meaningful summary of the relationship be-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=216 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Correlation Coefficient,calculated as: tween the two variables.

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=369 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Regression
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=397 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Using and interpreting regression models
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=408 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Regression models are vital tools for data analysis and are used extensively in epi-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=420 b=0 i=0 f=f5] demiological research. Their underlying concepts are not complex, although the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=433 b=0 i=0 f=f5] calculations can be. Fortunately, computer programs can take care of the calculations.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=446 b=0 i=0 f=f5] As such complexity is not needed in this text, our focus is on using and interpreting
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=459 b=0 i=0 f=f5] these models.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=482 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Different regression models
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=493 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Three types of regression models are fundamental to epidemiological research:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=515 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • linear regression
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=527 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • logistic regression
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=539 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Cox proportional hazards regression, a typeof survival analysis.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=562 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Key concept for regression models
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=573 b=0 i=0 f=f5] To use these models, we assume that variables influence each other. For example,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=586 b=0 i=0 f=f5] we can consider that bodyweight is influenced by factorssuch as age or gender. The
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=599 b=0 i=0 f=f5] value of interest is the dependent variable (e.g. body weight) and the identifiable
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=612 b=0 i=0 f=f5] factors are independent variables. It is the nature of the dependent variable that most
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=625 b=0 i=0 f=f5] distinguishes the three models from each other.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=646 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •Linear regression modelsss:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=658 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the dependent variable needs to bea continuous variable with its frequency
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=670 b=0 i=0 f=f5] distribution being the normal distribution.

[PAGE 89]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 76 Chapter 4
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •Logistic regression modelsss:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=95 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the dependent variable is derived from the presence or absence of a charac-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=107 b=0 i=0 f=f5] teristic, typically represented by 0 or 1.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=118 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •Cox proportional hazards models:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=130 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the dependent variable represents the time from a baseline of some type to the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=141 b=0 i=0 f=f5] occurrence of an event of interest.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=163 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Survival analysis – as donewith Cox proportional hazardsmodels – has an additional
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=176 b=0 i=0 f=f5] complexity in that censoring status needs to be considered as well.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=200 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Linear regression
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=228 b=0 i=0 f=f5] We can use the linear regression tool to address a broad set of issues, ranging from
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=241 b=0 i=0 f=f5] standard Analysis of Variance (ANOVA), to simple linear regression, and multiple
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=254 b=0 i=0 f=f5] linear regression. In all these cases, thedependent variable is a continuous measure
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=267 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (such as body weight), and the independent variables may be both continuous and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=280 b=0 i=0 f=f5] categorical.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=303 b=1 i=1 f=f15] Dependent variable

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 78 Chapter 4
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Logistic regression
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=112 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In the example above, the value for the dependent variable was body weight, a con-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=125 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tinuous measurement. We may also be interested in factors associated with the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=138 b=0 i=0 f=f5] presence or absence of obesity, defined perhaps as having a BMI ≥ 30. Logistic re-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=151 b=0 i=0 f=f5] gression is a powerful and flexible analytical tool for such situations. Our outcome
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=164 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of interest is typically an odds ratio comparing the odds, (for example, of obesity for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=177 b=0 i=0 f=f5] males versus females), adjusted for a collection of other factors.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=190 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The logistic regression model, as shown below, is ideal for this purpose. The
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=203 b=0 i=0 f=f5] regression model is based on the dependent variable ln(odds) where ln represents
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=216 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the natural logarithmic scale (base ee) and odds are defined as the likelihood p
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=229 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the event occurring dividedby the likelihood of it not occurring,1 - pp, sometimes
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=242 b=0 i=0 f=f5] listed as
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=260 y=263 b=0 i=0 f=f22] odds pp ///(((111 p),
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=286 b=0 i=0 f=f5] So the model becomes
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=216 y=305 b=0 i=0 f=f20] ln( ) = ß ++ xx ++ xx ++ ++ß x +ε
[L s=6.0 r=0.60 x=267 y=313 b=0 i=0 f=f22] 00 11 11 22 22 k k
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=332 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or equivalently,
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=260 y=351 b=0 i=1 f=f21] ß + x + x + +ß x +ε
[L s=5.0 r=0.50 x=225 y=359 b=0 i=0 f=f22] odds e 00 11 11 22 22 k k
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=382 b=0 i=0 f=f5] where thex are defined as they were for the linear regression model above. To in-
[L s=7.0 r=0.70 x=184 y=387 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ii
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=397 b=0 i=0 f=f5] terpret the coefficients for these models, we need to focus onodds and odds ratios
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=410 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (OR) rather than means, as was the case for linear regression. For example, for the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=423 b=0 i=0 f=f5] independent variable, x = gender, with x = 1 for males and x = 0 for females, then
[L s=7.0 r=0.70 x=226 y=427 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1 1 1
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=437 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the coefficientß is used in the equation:
[L s=7.0 r=0.70 x=201 y=442 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=260 y=459 b=0 i=1 f=f21] eß 1 OR
[L s=7.0 r=0.70 x=295 y=466 b=0 i=1 f=f21] males/females
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=486 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and the term is interpreted as the obesity odds ratio for males versusfemales, adjusted
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=499 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for the other terms in the model. The termeß 1, obtained from analyzing the data, is

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=569 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Kaplan-Meier survival curves
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=596 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Kaplan-Meier survival curves are commonly used to display survival data (see
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=609 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Figure 8.4). They can be used to display any type of time-to-event data. If the event
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=622 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that we are recording is death, then we use thevertical axis toshow the proportion
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=635 b=0 i=0 f=f5] alive at a specific point in time, and put time on the horizontal axis. These proportions
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=648 b=0 i=0 f=f5] range from 1.0 at the outset down to 0.0, if all members of the group die during
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=661 b=0 i=0 f=f5] follow-up. Kaplan-Meier curves are clear and easy to interpret and relatively easy to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=674 b=0 i=0 f=f5] make. The only complication is dealing with the censoring, as discussed above.

[PAGE 93]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 80 Chapter 4
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=85 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Kaplan and Meier solved this problem, which is why these curves carry their name.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=98 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Their solution was to plot the curves with survival time on the horizontal axis rather
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=111 b=0 i=0 f=f5] than calendar time. Then, usingfollow-up time as a reference, they assumed that the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=124 b=0 i=0 f=f5] individual who was censored at 15 months survived until the next event, in follow-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] up time, occurred. That is, they allowed the individual to “live” a little longer, but
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] only as long as the next person to “die.”
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=173 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Sample size issues
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] One of the problems we often have in epidemiological investigations is figuring out
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f5] how large a sample we need to answer a specific question.Our sample size must be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=227 b=0 i=0 f=f5] big enough for the study to have appropriate statistical power – the ability to demon-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=240 b=0 i=0 f=f5] strate an association if one exists (see Chapter 3). We base sample size calculations
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=253 b=0 i=0 f=f5] on a number of study design factors:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=274 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • prevalence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=286 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • acceptable error
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=298 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • the detectable difference.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=322 b=0 i=0 f=f5] There are numerous formulae and computer programs that simplify the task consid-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=335 b=0 i=0 f=f5] erably. Two helpful and relatively simple formulae are:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=356 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • the two sample t-test and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=368 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • the test comparing two proportions.

[L s=7.0 r=0.70 x=228 y=304 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1 2
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=324 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Meta-analysis
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=356 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Meta-analysis is defined as the statistical synthesis of the data from separate but
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=369 b=0 i=0 f=f5] similar (comparable) studies, leading to a quantifiable summary of the pooled results
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=382 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to identify the overall trend (see Chapter 5). An example is shown in Figure 5.7.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=395 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Meta-analysis differs frommost medical andepidemiological studies in that no
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=408 b=0 i=0 f=f5] new data are collected. Instead, results from previous studies are combined. Steps in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=421 b=0 i=0 f=f5] carrying out meta-analysis include:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=442 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • formulating the problem and study design;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=454 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • identifying relevant studies;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=466 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • excluding poorly conducted studies or those with major methodological flaws;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=478 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=489 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • measuring, combining and interpreting the results.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=513 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Which studies are identified and whether they are included or excluded from the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=526 b=0 i=0 f=f5] meta-analysis are crucial factors. Another important step is measuring the results of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=539 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the studies on a single scale. This allows comparisons to be made between studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=551 b=0 i=0 f=f5] even if they used different measures of outcome. Meta-analysis is a relatively new
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=564 b=0 i=0 f=f5] scientific method; research into the best techniques to use is still ongoing and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=577 b=0 i=0 f=f5] expanding into new areas. It is not yet as well-acceptedas other statistical techniques
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=590 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that have a longer tradition of use.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=603 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The use of meta-analysis in medicine and epidemiology has increased in recent
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=616 b=0 i=0 f=f5] years for ethical reasons, cost issues, and the need tohave an overall idea of effects
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=629 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of a particular intervention in different populationgroups. This is particularly true in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=642 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the area of clinical trials, where the sample size of individual trials is often too small
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=655 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to permit conclusions to be drawn from any one trial,although conclusions can be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=668 b=0 i=0 f=f5] drawn from aggregatedresults. For example, meta-analysis showedthat aspirin has

[PAGE 95]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=54 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 82 Chapter 4
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=85 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a significant effect in preventing a second heart attack or stroke, even though no
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=98 b=0 i=0 f=f5] single study had convincingly shown this. Theseissues are taken up in greater detail
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=111 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in the next chapterron causation.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=140 y=133 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Study questions
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=167 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 7.1 Calculate the mean, median, variance, standard deviationand standard error
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=179 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for the sample of n = 10 body weights given in this chapter.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=191 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 7.2 Why is personal income often reported as median income rather than average
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=203 b=0 i=0 f=f5] income?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=215 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 7.3 What are the major differences among linear regression, logistic regression
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=227 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and regression models for survival analyses?
