[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=470 y=53 b=1 i=1 f=f8] 83
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Chapter 5
[L s=32.0 r=3.20 x=55 y=101 b=0 i=0 f=f7] Causation in epidemiology
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=167 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Key messages
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=180 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • The study of causation of diseases and injuries is fundamental to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=194 b=0 i=0 f=f5] epidemiology.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=202 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • There is seldom one single cause of a specific health outcome.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Causal factors can be arranged into a hierarchy from the most proximal to the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=228 b=0 i=0 f=f5] distal socio-economic factors.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=237 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Criteria for judging the evidence of causality include: temporal relationship,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=251 b=0 i=0 f=f5] plausibility, consistency, strength, dose–response relationship, reversibility
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=263 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and study design.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=291 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A major focus of epidemiology is informing efforts to prevent and control disease and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=304 b=0 i=0 f=f5] promote health. To do this, we need to know the causes of disease or injury and the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=317 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ways in which these causes can be modified. This chapter describes the epidemio-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=330 b=0 i=0 f=f5] logical approach to causation.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=352 b=0 i=0 f=f9] The concept of cause
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=385 b=0 i=0 f=f5] An understanding of the causes of disease or injury is important not only for pre-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=398 b=0 i=0 f=f5] vention, but also for correct diagnosis and treatment. The concept of cause is the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=411 b=0 i=0 f=f5] source of much controversy in epidemiology. The process by which we make causal
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=424 b=0 i=0 f=f5] inferences – judgments linking postulated causes and their outcomes – is a major
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=437 b=0 i=0 f=f5] theme of the general philosophy of science, and the concept of cause has different
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=450 b=0 i=0 f=f5] meanings in different contexts.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=474 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Sufficient or necessary
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=502 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A cause of a disease or injury is an event, condition, characteristic or a combination
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=514 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of these factors which plays an important role in producing the health outcome.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=528 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Logically, a cause must precede an outcome. A cause is termed sufficient when it
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=541 b=0 i=0 f=f5] inevitably produces or initiates an outcome and is termed necessary if an outcome
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=553 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cannot develop in its absence. Some diseases are caused completely by genetic factors
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=567 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in the individual, and other causes of a disease interact with genetic factors in making
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=580 b=0 i=0 f=f5] certain individuals more vulnerable than others. The term environmental causes is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=592 b=0 i=0 f=f5] often used to distinguish these other causes from the genetic causes. It has been
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=606 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pointed out1 that there are nearly always some genetic and some environmental
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=619 b=0 i=0 f=f5] component causes in every causal mechanism.

[PAGE 97]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 84 Chapter 5
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=84 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Multiple factors
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=95 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A sufficient cause is not usually a single factor, but often comprises several compo-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=108 b=0 i=0 f=f5] nents (multi-factorial causation). In general, it is not necessary to identify all the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=121 b=0 i=0 f=f5] components of a sufficient cause before effective prevention can take place, since the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=134 b=0 i=0 f=f5] removal of one component may interfere with the action of the others and thus
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=147 b=0 i=0 f=f5] prevent the disease or injury. For example, cigarette smoking is one component of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=160 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the sufficient cause of lung cancer. Smoking is not sufficient in itself to produce the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=173 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease: some people smoke for 50 years without developing lung cancer. Other
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=186 b=0 i=0 f=f5] factors, mostly unknown, are involved and genetic factors may play a role. However,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=199 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the cessation of smoking reduces the number of cases of lung cancer greatly in a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=212 b=0 i=0 f=f5] population even if the other component causes are not altered (Figure 8.5).
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=235 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Attributable fraction
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=246 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The attributable fraction (see chapter 2) can be used to quantify the likely preventive
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=259 b=0 i=0 f=f5] impact of eliminating a specific causal factor. For instance, Table 1.2 shows what
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=272 b=0 i=0 f=f5] would be expected if the smoking asbestos workers had either never smoked or never
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] been exposed to asbestos: never smoking would have decreased the lung cancer
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=298 b=0 i=0 f=f5] death rate from 602 per 100 000 to 58 per 100 000 (a 90% reduction) and never
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=311 b=0 i=0 f=f5] exposed to asbestos, but still smoking, would have reduced the rate from 602 to 123
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=324 b=0 i=0 f=f5] per 100 000 (an 80% reduction). (Study question 5.3 will explore this further.)
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=348 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Sufficient and necessary
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=375 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Each sufficient cause has a necessary cause as a component. For example, in a study
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=388 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of an outbreak of foodborne infection, it may be found that chicken salad and creamy
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=402 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dessert were both sufficient causes of salmonella diarrhoea. However, the ingestion
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=415 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of Salmonella bacteria is a necessary cause of this disease. Similarly, there are different
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=427 b=0 i=0 f=f5] components in the causation of tuberculosis, but the infection with Mycobac-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=139 y=441 b=0 i=0 f=f5] terium tuberculosis is a necessary cause (Figure 5.1). However, a causal factor on its
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=454 b=0 i=0 f=f5] own is often neither necessary nor sufficient, such as tobacco use as a factor for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=466 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cerebrovascular disease.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=480 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The usual approach in epidemiology is to begin with a disease and search for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=493 b=0 i=0 f=f5] its causes, although it is also possible to start with a potential cause (such as air
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=505 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pollution) and search for its effects. Epidemiology encompasses a whole set of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=518 b=0 i=0 f=f5] relationships. For example, social class is associated with a range of health problems.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=531 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Low social class, as measured by income, education, housing and occupation, leads
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=545 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to a general susceptibility to poor health, rather than to a specific effect.2 A gamut of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=558 b=0 i=0 f=f5] specific causes of disease could explain why poor people have poor health, among
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=571 b=0 i=0 f=f5] them excessive exposure to infectious agents due to overcrowding, lack of clean water
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=584 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and sanitation, insufficient and unsafe food, and dangerous working conditions. In
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=597 b=0 i=0 f=f5] addition, being at the bottom of the social ladder is in itself associated with poorer
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=610 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health even after taking all the other factors into account.3 One example of a strong
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=623 b=0 i=0 f=f5] relationship between socioeconomic status and disease is shown in Figure 5.2.4

[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=126 y=275 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Risk factors for tuberculosis Mechanisms for tuberculosis
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=302 b=0 i=0 f=f10] A causal pathway
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=330 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemiologists have been criticized, particularly by laboratory scientists, for not us-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=343 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ing the concept of cause in the sense of being the sole requirement for the production
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=356 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of disease. Such a restrictive view of causation, does not take into account the fact
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=369 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that diseases commonly have multiple causes. Prevention strategies often need to be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=382 b=0 i=0 f=f5] directed simultaneously at more than one factor. In addition, causes can be linked to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=395 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a causal pathway where one factor leads to another until eventually the specific
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=408 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pathogenic agent becomes present in the organ that gets damaged; this can also be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=421 b=0 i=0 f=f5] called a hierarchy of causes. Laboratory scientists might, for example, suggest that
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=434 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the basic cause of coronary heart disease relates to cellular mechanisms involved in
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=55 y=474 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Figure 5.2. Infant mortality rate and socioeconomic status in Islamic Republic of
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=55 y=485 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Iran4
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=96 y=498 b=0 i=0 f=f13] 60
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=132 y=664 b=0 i=0 f=f13] 1 3 5
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=180 y=680 b=1 i=0 f=f12] Socioeconomic quintiles

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 86 Chapter 5
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the proliferation of tissue in the arterial wall. Research directed at determining
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pathogenic relationships is obviously important, but concepts of causation need to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be understood in a wider epidemiological context.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] It is often possible to make major progress in prevention by dealing only with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the more remote or “upstream” causes. It was possible to prevent cholera cases
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] decades before the responsible organism – let alone its mechanism of action – had
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] been identified (Figure 5.3). However, it is of interest that even in 1854, John Snow
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=175 b=0 i=0 f=f5] thought that a living organism was responsible for the disease (see Chapter 1).
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=199 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Single and multiple causes
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=226 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Pasteur’s work on microorganisms led to the formulation, first by Henle and then by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=240 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Koch, of the following rules for determining whether a specific living organism causes
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=253 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a particular disease:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=274 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • the organism must be present in every case of the disease;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=286 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • the organism must be able to be isolated and grown in pure culture;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=298 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • the organism must, when inoculated into a susceptible animal, cause the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=310 b=0 i=0 f=f5] specific disease;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=321 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • the organism must then be recovered from the animal and identified.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=344 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Anthrax was the first disease demonstrated to meet these rules, which have since
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] proved useful with many other infectious diseases and with chemical poisoning.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=370 b=0 i=0 f=f5] However, for many diseases, both communicable and non-communicable,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=383 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Koch’s rules for determining causation are inadequate. Many causes act together, and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=397 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a single factor – such as tobacco use – may be a cause of many diseases. In addition,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=409 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the causative organism may disappear when a disease has developed, making it
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=422 b=0 i=0 f=f5] impossible to demonstrate the organism in the sick person. Koch’s postulates are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=436 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of most value when the specific cause is a highly pathogenic infectious agent,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=448 b=0 i=0 f=f5] chemical poison or other specific factor, and there are no healthy carriers of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=461 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pathogen: a relatively uncommon occurrence.
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=492 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Figure 5.3. Causes of cholera
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=255 y=505 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Exposure Effect of cholera
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=235 y=514 b=0 i=0 f=f13] to contaminated water toxins on bowel wall cells
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=181 y=526 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Genetic factors
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=142 y=541 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Malnutrition
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=292 y=579 b=0 i=0 f=f13] INFECTION BY
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=143 y=638 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Crowded
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=145 y=647 b=0 i=0 f=f13] housing
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=183 y=659 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Poverty

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=358 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Causation in epidemiology 87
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Factors in causation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=111 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Four types of factors play a part in the causation of disease, all may be necessary but
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=124 b=0 i=0 f=f5] they are rarely sufficient to cause a particular disease or state:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=145 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •Predisposing factors, such as age, sex, or specific genetic traits that may result
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=80 y=158 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in a poorly functioning immune system or slow metabolism of a toxic chemical.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=80 y=169 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Previous illness may also create a state of susceptibility to a disease agent.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=180 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •Enabling (or disabling) factors such as low income, poor nutrition, bad housing
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=80 y=192 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and inadequate medical care may favour the development of disease. Con-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=80 y=204 b=0 i=0 f=f5] versely, circumstances that assist in recovery from illness or in the maintenance
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=80 y=215 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of good health could also be called enabling factors. The social and economic
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=80 y=227 b=0 i=0 f=f5] determinants of health are just as important as the precipitating factors in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=80 y=238 b=0 i=0 f=f5] designing prevention approaches.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=249 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •Precipitating factors such as exposure to a specific disease agent may be as-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=80 y=261 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sociated with the onset of a disease.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=72 y=272 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •Reinforcing factors such as repeated exposure, environmental conditions and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=80 y=284 b=0 i=0 f=f5] unduly hard work may aggravate an established disease or injury.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=307 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The term “risk factor” is commonly used to describe factors that are positively asso-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=320 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ciated with the risk of development of a disease but that are not sufficient to cause
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=333 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the disease. The concept has proved useful in several practical prevention
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=346 b=0 i=0 f=f5] programmes. Some risk factors (such as tobacco smoking) are associated with several
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=359 b=0 i=0 f=f5] diseases, and some diseases (such as coronary heart disease) are associated with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=372 b=0 i=0 f=f5] several risk factors (Figure 5.4).
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=55 y=411 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Figure 5.4. Risk factors common to major noncommunicable diseases5

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 88 Chapter 5
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemiological studies can measure the relative contribution of each factor to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease occurrence, and the corresponding potential reduction in disease from the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] elimination of each risk factor. However, multi-causality means that the sum of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] attributable fractions for each risk factor may be greater than 100%.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=147 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Interaction
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=174 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The effect of two or more causes acting together is often greater than would be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=187 b=0 i=0 f=f5] expected on the basis of summing the individual effects. This phenomenon, called
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=199 b=0 i=0 f=f5] interaction, is illustrated by the particularly high risk of lung cancer in people who
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=212 b=0 i=0 f=f5] both smoke and are exposed to asbestos dust (Table 1.2). The risk of lung cancer in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=224 b=0 i=0 f=f5] this group is much higher than would be indicated by a simple addition of the risks
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=237 b=0 i=0 f=f5] from smoking (ten times) and exposure to asbestos dust (five times); the risk is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=249 b=0 i=0 f=f5] multiplied fifty times.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=273 b=0 i=0 f=f10] A hierarchy of causes
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=300 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Multiple causes and risk factors can often be displayed in the form of a hierarchy of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=313 b=0 i=0 f=f5] causes, where some are the proximal or most immediate causes (precipitating factors)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=325 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and others are distal or indirect causes (enabling factors). Inhaled tobacco smoke is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=338 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a proximal cause of lung cancer, while low socio-economic status is a distal cause
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=350 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that is associated with smoking habits and indirectly with lung cancer. Various
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=363 b=0 i=0 f=f5] frameworks have been devised for visualizing the relationships between the distal
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=375 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and proximal causes and the eventual health effects. One such multi-layer framework,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=388 b=0 i=0 f=f5] DPSEEA (driving forces, pressure, state, exposure, effect, action), was used by WHO
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=400 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to analyse different elements of causation, prevention and indicators in relation to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=413 b=0 i=0 f=f5] environmental health hazards (Figure 5.5).
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=27 y=445 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Figure 5.5. The DPSEEA framework6

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=72 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A similar framework was developed for the WHO Global Burden of Disease
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] project.7 The Multiple Exposures, Multiple Effects framework emphasizes the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] complex relationships between environmental exposures and child health outcomes.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] This model takes into account that individual exposures can lead to many different
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health outcomes, and specific health outcomes can be attributed to many different
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] exposures.8
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=72 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In epidemiological studies linking one or more causes to a health outcome, it is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=175 b=0 i=0 f=f5] important to consider to what extent different causes are at the same or different
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=188 b=0 i=0 f=f5] levels in the hierarchy. If a “cause of a cause” is included in the analysis together with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the cause itself, the statistical method of analysis has to take this into account. The
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f5] identification of the hierarchy of causes and the quantitative relationships between
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=227 b=0 i=0 f=f5] them will provide one way of describing the mechanism of causation. For example,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=240 b=0 i=0 f=f5] low socio-economic status is associated in many industrialized nations with more
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=253 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tobacco smoking, which is associated with higher blood pressure, which in turn
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=266 b=0 i=0 f=f5] increases the risk of stroke.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=288 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Establishing the cause of a disease
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=321 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Causal inference is the term used for the process of determining whether observed
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=334 b=0 i=0 f=f5] associations are likely to be causal; the use of guidelines and the making of judgements
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=347 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are involved. The process of judging causation can be difficult and contentious. It
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=360 b=0 i=0 f=f5] has been argued that causal inference should be restricted to the measurement of an
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=373 b=0 i=0 f=f5] effect, rather than as a criterion-guided process for deciding whether an effect is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=386 b=0 i=0 f=f5] present or not.1,9 Before an association is assessed for the possibility that it is causal,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=399 b=0 i=0 f=f5] other explanations, such as chance, bias and confounding, have to be excluded. How
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=412 b=0 i=0 f=f5] these factors are assessed has been described in Chapter 3. The steps in assessing
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=425 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the nature of the relationship between a possible cause and an outcome are shown
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=438 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in Figure 5.6.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=462 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Considering causation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=490 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A systematic approach to determining the nature of an association was used by the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=503 b=0 i=0 f=f5] United States Surgeon General to establish that cigarette smoking caused lung can-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=516 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cer.10 This approach was further elaborated by Hill.11 On the basis of these concepts,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=529 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a set of “considerations for causation,” listed in the sequence of testing that the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=542 b=0 i=0 f=f5] epidemiologist should follow to reach a conclusion about a cause of disease, is shown
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=555 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in Table 5.1.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=579 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Temporal relationship
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=606 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The temporal relationship is crucial—the cause must precede the effect. This is usually
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=619 b=0 i=0 f=f5] self-evident, although difficulties may arise in case-control and cross-sectional studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=632 b=0 i=0 f=f5] when measurements of the possible cause and effect are made at the same time. In
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=645 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cases where the cause is an exposure that can be at different levels, it is essential that
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=658 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a high enough level be reached before the disease occurs for the correct temporal

[L s=8.0 r=1.00 x=27 y=516 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Table 5.1. Considerations for causation
[L s=8.0 r=1.00 x=27 y=541 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Temporal relation Does the cause precede the effect? (essential)

[L s=8.0 r=1.00 x=138 y=562 b=0 i=0 f=f14] experimental animals)

[L s=8.0 r=1.00 x=27 y=586 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Strength What is the strength of the association between the cause and the effect? (relative risk)
[L s=8.0 r=1.00 x=27 y=598 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Dose–response relationship Is increased exposure to the possible cause associated with increased effect?
[L s=8.0 r=1.00 x=27 y=610 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Reversibility Does the removal of a possible cause lead to reduction of disease risk?

[L s=8.0 r=1.00 x=27 y=634 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Judging the evidence How many lines of evidence lead to the conclusion?

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Techniques are available for pooling the results of several studies that have ex-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] amined the same issue, particularly randomized controlled trials. This technique is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] called meta-analysis (see Chapter 4), and is used to combine the results of several
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] trials, each of which may deal with a relatively small sample, to obtain a better overall
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] estimate of effect (Figure 5.7).18
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=175 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Systematic review uses standardized methods to select and review all relevant
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=187 b=0 i=0 f=f5] studies on a particular topic with a view to eliminating bias in critical appraisal and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=200 b=0 i=0 f=f5] synthesis. Systematic review as part of the Cochrane Collaboration is sometimes, but
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=212 b=0 i=0 f=f5] not always, coupled with meta-analysis.19 Figure 5.7 illustrates the results of 113
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=225 b=0 i=0 f=f5] case-control studies and two cohort studies on the relationship between oral clefts

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=262 b=0 i=0 f=f5] early studies were based on small samples. The estimated relative risk in each study
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=275 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is marked by a box; the horizontal lines indicate the 95% confidence intervals. For
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=287 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the aggregated data from all the trials, covering a large number of events, the 95%
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=300 b=0 i=0 f=f5] confidence interval is very narrow. Overall, maternal smoking appears to be associated
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=312 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with a 22% increase in cleft palates: the 95% confidence interval shows that the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=325 b=0 i=0 f=f5] increase could be at least 10% and as much as 35%.20

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Reversibility
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=111 b=0 i=0 f=f5] When the removal of a possible cause results in a reduced disease risk, there is a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=124 b=0 i=0 f=f5] greater likelihood that the association is causal. For example, the cessation of cigarette
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=137 b=0 i=0 f=f5] smoking is associated with a reduction in the risk of lung cancer relative to that in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=150 b=0 i=0 f=f5] people who continue to smoke (see Figure 8.5). This finding strengthens the likeli-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=163 b=0 i=0 f=f5] hood that cigarette smoking causes lung cancer. If the cause leads to rapid irreversible
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=176 b=0 i=0 f=f5] changes that subsequently produce disease whether or not there is continued expo-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=189 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sure, then reversibility cannot be a condition for causality.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Study design
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=241 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The ability of a study design to prove causation is an important consideration.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=254 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Table 5.3 outlines the different types of study and relative strengths in establishing
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=267 b=0 i=0 f=f5] causality. These study designs were introduced in Chapter 3; their use in providing
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=280 b=0 i=0 f=f5] evidence for causal relationships is discussed below.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=303 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Experimental studies
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=282 y=310 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Table 5.3. Relative ability of different types of study
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=314 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The best evidence comes from well-designed randomized
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=282 y=321 b=1 i=0 f=f11] to “prove” causation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=327 b=0 i=0 f=f5] controlled trials. However, evidence is rarely available from
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=340 b=0 i=0 f=f5] this type of study, and often only relates to the effects of
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=282 y=343 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Type of study Ability to “prove”
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=352 b=0 i=0 f=f5] treatment and prevention campaigns. Other experimental causation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=366 b=0 i=0 f=f5] studies, such as field and community trials, are seldom Randomized controlled trials Strong
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=379 b=0 i=0 f=f5] used to study causation. Evidence comes most often from Cohort studies Moderate
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=392 b=0 i=0 f=f5] observational studies (Table 3.1); almost all the evidence Case-control studies Moderate
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=404 b=0 i=0 f=f5] on the health consequences of smoking comes from ob- Cross-sectional studies Weak
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=55 y=416 b=0 i=0 f=f14] servational studies. Ecological studies Weak
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=441 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Cohort and case control studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=452 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Cohort studies are the next best design because, when well conducted, bias is min-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=465 b=0 i=0 f=f5] imized. Again, they are not always available. Although case-control studies are subject
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=478 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to several forms of bias, the results from large, well-designed investigations of this
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=491 b=0 i=0 f=f5] kind provide good evidence for the causal nature of an association; judgements often
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=504 b=0 i=0 f=f5] have to be made in the absence of data from other sources.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=527 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Cross-sectional studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=538 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Cross-sectional studies are less able to prove causation as they provide no direct
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=551 b=0 i=0 f=f5] evidence on the time sequence of events. However, the time sequence can often be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=564 b=0 i=0 f=f5] inferred from the way exposure and effect data is collected. For instance, if it is clear
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=577 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that the health effect is recent and the exposure to the potential causes is recorded
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=590 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in a questionnaire, questions about the past may clearly identify exposures before the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=603 b=0 i=0 f=f5] effect occurred.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=626 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Ecological studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=637 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Ecological studies provide the weakest evidence for causality because of the danger
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=650 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of incorrect extrapolation to individuals from regional or national data. However, for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=663 b=0 i=0 f=f5] certain exposures that cannot normally be measured individually (such as air pollu-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=676 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tion, pesticide residues in food, fluoride in drinking water), evidence from ecological

[PAGE 109]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 96 Chapter 5
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] studies is very important. When causal relationships have already been established,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] well-designed ecological studies, particularly time series studies, can be very useful
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to quantify effects.27
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] However, there are occasions when ecological studies provide good evidence for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] establishing causation. One such example relates to epidemics of asthma deaths. In
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1968 the sale of inhaled bronchodilators without prescription in England and Wales
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] was stopped because the increase in asthma deaths in the period 1959–66 had been
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=175 b=0 i=0 f=f5] shown to coincide with a rise in bronchodilator sales. After the availability of inhaled
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=188 b=0 i=0 f=f5] bronchodilators was restricted, the death rate fell. A similar pattern was observed
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] following restrictions on the availability of the inhaled bronchodilator fenoterol in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f5] New Zealand in 1989.28
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=238 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Judging the evidence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=266 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Regrettably, there are no completely reliable criteria for determining whether an as-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=279 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sociation is causal or not. Causal inference is usually tentative and judgements must
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=292 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be made on the basis of the available evidence: uncertainty always remains. Evidence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=305 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is often conflicting and due weight must be given to the different types when deci-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=318 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sions are made. In judging the different aspects of causation referred to above, the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=331 b=0 i=0 f=f5] correct temporal relationship is essential; once that has been established, the greatest
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=344 b=0 i=0 f=f5] weight may be given to plausibility, consistency and the dose–response relationship.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The likelihood of a causal association is heightened when many different types of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=370 b=0 i=0 f=f5] evidence lead to the same conclusion.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=383 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Evidence from well-designed studies is particularly important, especially if they
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=396 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are conducted in a variety of locations. The most important use of information about
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=409 b=0 i=0 f=f5] causation of diseases and injuries may be in the area of prevention, which we will
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=422 b=0 i=0 f=f5] discuss in the following chapters. When the causal pathways are established on the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=435 b=0 i=0 f=f5] basis of quantitative information from epidemiological studies, the decisions about
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=448 b=0 i=0 f=f5] prevention may be uncontroversial. In situations where the causation is not so well
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=461 b=0 i=0 f=f5] established, but the impacts have great potential public health importance, the “pre-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=474 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cautionary principle”29 may be applied to take preventive action as a safety measure;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=487 b=0 i=0 f=f5] this is called “precautionary prevention”.

[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=168 y=217 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Key messages
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=248 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Chronic noncommunicable diseases are major public health challenges in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=262 b=0 i=0 f=f5] most countries.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=271 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • The causes of chronic diseases are generally known and cost-effective inter-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ventions are available.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=294 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • A comprehensive approach is required for the prevention and control of these
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=308 b=0 i=0 f=f5] diseases.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=317 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Ultimately, primary prevention and control is the best strategy for the pre-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=331 b=0 i=0 f=f5] vention of modern epidemics.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=340 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Targeting high-risk individuals with secondary and tertiary prevention are also
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=354 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ways to reduce the burden of chronic disease.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=382 b=0 i=0 f=f9] The scope of prevention
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=414 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The decline in death rates that occurred during the nineteenth century in high-income
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=427 b=0 i=0 f=f5] countries was principally due to a decrease in deaths from infectious disease.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=440 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Figure 6.1 shows tuberculosis death rates in England and Wales for the period
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=453 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1840–1968 and indicates the times of introduction of specific preventive and thera-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=466 b=0 i=0 f=f5] peutic measures. Most of the decline in mortality took place before these interventions
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=479 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and has been attributed to improvements in nutrition, housing, sanitation and other
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=492 b=0 i=0 f=f5] environmental health measures.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=516 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Recent trends in death rates
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=544 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In the last decades of the twentieth century, the declines in death rates from cardio-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=557 b=0 i=0 f=f5] vascular disease have accelerated in high-income countries. Since the 1970s, death
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=570 b=0 i=0 f=f5] rates from heart disease and stroke have fallen by up to 70% in Australia, Canada,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=583 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Japan, the United Kingdom and the United States of America. There have also been
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=596 b=0 i=0 f=f5] improvements in cardiovascular mortality rates in middle-income countries, such as
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=609 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Poland. These gains have been the result of a wide range of measures directed at both
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=622 b=0 i=0 f=f5] whole populations and individuals. The preventive potential for chronic diseases is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=635 b=0 i=0 f=f5] enormous. (Box 6.1). A decline in death rates of an additional 2% per annum over
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=648 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 10 years has the potential to avert the untimely deaths of 35 million people.2

[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=140 y=655 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the population, as well as by waxing and waning epidemics. The changes in mor-
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=140 y=668 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tality rates in high-income countries have been particularly dramatic in the youngest

[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=231 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Epidemiology and prevention: chronic noncommunicable diseases 101
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] age groups, where infectious diseases used to account for most mortality. Traffic
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=56 y=96 b=0 i=0 f=f5] crashes are now the leading cause of death in childhood in many high-income
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=55 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] countries.
[L s=14.0 r=1.56 x=55 y=134 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Preventive potential
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=55 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The changing patterns of mortality and morbidity indicate that major causes of disease
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=55 y=174 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are preventable. Yet even the healthiest person will succumb at some age, and the
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=55 y=187 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lifetime mortality risk for any population is 100%. However, most populations are
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=55 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] affected by specific diseases which can be prevented. Studies of migrants show that
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=55 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f5] they often develop the patterns of disease of host populations. For example, the rates
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=55 y=226 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of gastric cancer in people born in Hawaii to Japanese parents are lower than those
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=55 y=239 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of people born in Japan. After two generations in the USA, people of Japanese heri-
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=55 y=253 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tage have the same gastric cancer rate as the US population in general. The fact that
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=55 y=265 b=0 i=0 f=f5] it takes a generation or more for the rates to fall suggests the importance of an
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=55 y=278 b=0 i=0 f=f5] exposure – such as diet – in early life.
[L s=8.0 r=0.89 x=62 y=313 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Box 6.1. Chronic disease epidemiology: the basis of prevention
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=329 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Chronic diseases are the major cause of death in almost all countries and account for 36 million deaths each year (see Figure 7.1).
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=340 b=0 i=0 f=f5] This translates to 61% of the world’s deaths and 48% of the global burden of disease.3 20% of chronic noncommunicable
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=351 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease deaths occur in high-income countries, and 80% occur in low- and middle-income countries – where most of the
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=362 b=0 i=0 f=f5] world’s population live.
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=75 y=373 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The leading chronic diseases are:
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=75 y=386 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •cardiovascular disease (CVD), especially coronary heart disease and stroke (17.5 million deaths);
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=75 y=398 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •cancer (7.5 million deaths);
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=75 y=409 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •chronic respiratory disease (4 million deaths); and
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=75 y=421 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •diabetes (1.1 million deaths).
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=436 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Regional estimates indicate that chronic diseases are more frequent causes of death than communicable diseases worldwide,
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=447 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with the exception of the African region.
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=75 y=458 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Injuries – accounting for almost one in ten deaths – feature prominently in all regions, caused mostly by traffic crashes,
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=469 b=0 i=0 f=f5] occupational injuries and interpersonal violence. The burden of injuries is increasing in most low- and middle-income
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=480 b=0 i=0 f=f5] countries.
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=75 y=491 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Mental health problems are leading contributors to the burden of disease in many countries and contribute significantly
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=502 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to the incidence and severity of many chronic diseases, including cardiovascular diseases and cancer. Visual impairment and
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=513 b=0 i=0 f=f5] blindness, hearing impairment and deafness, oral diseases and genetic disorders are other chronic conditions that account
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=524 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for a substantial portion of the global burden of disease.
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=75 y=535 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Without greater attention to prevention, it has been estimated that by 2030 myocardial infarct, stroke and diabetes will
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=546 b=0 i=0 f=f5] account for four in ten deaths among adults (35–64 years) in low- and middle-income countries, compared with one in eight
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=557 b=0 i=0 f=f5] deaths in the same age group in the United States of America and other high-income countries.4 Projections suggest that
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=568 b=0 i=0 f=f5] over the next 10 years deaths due to chronic noncommunicable diseases will increase by 17%. This means that of the
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=579 b=0 i=0 f=f5] projected 64 million people who will die in 2015, 41 million will die of a chronic disease. However, large-scale prevention is
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=590 b=0 i=0 f=f5] feasible, as the causes of the major chronic diseases are known and are the same in all regions and population sub
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=601 b=0 i=0 f=f5] groups.5–7 A small number of modifiable risk factors explain most new cases, and evidence-based interventions are available,
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=62 y=612 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cost-effective and widely applicable.9
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=73 y=645 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Geographical variation in disease occurrence within and between countries
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=55 y=658 b=0 i=0 f=f5] also provides important clues to preventive potential (Figure 6.3). In the United
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=55 y=671 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Kingdom age-standardized male lung cancer rates fell from 18 per 100 000 in 1950

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lung cancer rates increased. In France, the increase in tobacco use occurred some
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] decades later than in the United Kingdom, and smoking rates started to decrease only

[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=162 y=210 b=0 i=0 f=f13] - Heredity
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=245 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Public health nurses, medical sociologists, psychologists, health economists,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=258 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ergonometrists, sanitary engineers, pollution control experts and occupational
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=271 b=0 i=0 f=f5] hygienists are all involved in disease-prevention efforts. As the limits of curative
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=284 b=0 i=0 f=f5] medicine become apparent and the costs of medical care escalate in all countries,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=297 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease prevention is gaining prominence.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=319 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Levels of prevention
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=352 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The four levels of prevention, corresponding to different phases in the development
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=365 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of disease, are primordial, primary, secondary and tertiary.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=378 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Each of these levels targets factors or conditions which have an established role
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=391 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in causing disease. In situations where the evidence of such a role is incomplete, but
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=404 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the risk of not preventing a public health threat is deemed too high, preventive actions
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=417 b=0 i=0 f=f5] may still be taken and can be labelled “precautionary prevention.” This approach is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=430 b=0 i=0 f=f5] common in the environmental field, where the “precautionary principle” is used to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=443 b=0 i=0 f=f5] avoid public health risks from processes or products.17
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=456 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Approaches to prevention overlap and merge, yet all levels are important and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=469 b=0 i=0 f=f5] complementary. Primordial and primary prevention contribute most to the health of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=482 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the whole population, while secondary and tertiary prevention are generally focused
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=495 b=0 i=0 f=f5] on people who already have signs of disease (Table 6.1).
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=517 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Primordial prevention
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=550 b=0 i=0 f=f5] This level of prevention was identified as a result of increasing knowledge about
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=563 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the epidemiology of cardiovascular diseases. It is known that coronary heart disease
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=576 b=0 i=0 f=f5] occurs on a large scale only if the basic underlying cause is present, i.e. a diet
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=589 b=0 i=0 f=f5] high in saturated animal fat. Where this cause was largely absent – as in China and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=602 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Japan – coronary heart disease remained a rare cause of mortality and morbidity,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=615 b=0 i=0 f=f5] despite the high frequencies of other important risk factors such as cigarette smoking
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=628 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and high blood pressure. However, smoking-induced lung cancer is on the increase
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=641 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and strokes induced by high blood pressure are common in China and Japan. In some
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=653 b=0 i=0 f=f5] middle-income countries, cardiovascular disease is becoming important in the urban
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=667 b=0 i=0 f=f5] middle- and upper-income groups, who have already acquired high-risk behaviour.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=680 b=0 i=0 f=f5] As socioeconomic development occurs, such risk factors can be expected to become

[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=361 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Primary prevention
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=393 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The purpose of primary prevention is to limit the incidence of disease by controlling
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=406 b=0 i=0 f=f5] specific causes and risk factors. Primary prevention efforts can be directed at:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=427 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • the whole population with the aim of reducing average risk (the population or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=440 b=0 i=0 f=f5] “mass” strategy); or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=450 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • people at high risk as a result of particular exposures (the high-risk-individual
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=463 b=0 i=0 f=f5] strategy).
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=486 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Population strategy
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=514 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The population approach aims to shift the whole population distribution to the left
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=527 b=0 i=0 f=f5] on an imaginary x-axis; i.e. to reduce the mean population level of cholesterol (or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=540 b=0 i=0 f=f5] blood pressure). The major advantage of the population strategy is that one does not
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=553 b=0 i=0 f=f5] have to identify the high-risk group but simply aim to reduce – by a small amount –
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=566 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the level of a given risk factor in the entire population. Its main disadvantage is that
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=579 b=0 i=0 f=f5] it offers little benefit to many individuals because their absolute risks of disease are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=592 b=0 i=0 f=f5] quite low. For example, most people will wear a seat-belt while driving a car for their
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=605 b=0 i=0 f=f5] entire life without being involved in a crash. The widespread wearing of seat-belts
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=618 b=0 i=0 f=f5] has been very beneficial to the population as a whole, but little apparent benefit is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=631 b=0 i=0 f=f5] accrued by those individuals who are never personally involved in a crash. This phe-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=644 b=0 i=0 f=f5] nomenon has been called the prevention paradox.18
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=657 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The high incidence of cardiovascular disease in most industrialized countries is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=670 b=0 i=0 f=f5] due to the high levels of risk factors in the population as a whole, not to the problems

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=394 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to the right. Only a small minority of the population have a serum cholesterol level
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=407 b=0 i=0 f=f5] above 8 mmol/l, i.e. a very high risk of coronary heart disease. Most of the deaths
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=420 b=0 i=0 f=f5] attributable to coronary heart disease occur in the middle range of the cholesterol
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=433 b=0 i=0 f=f5] level, where most of the population is. In this case, primary prevention depends on
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=446 b=0 i=0 f=f5] changes that reduce the average risk in the whole population, thus shifting the whole
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=459 b=0 i=0 f=f5] distribution to a lower level.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=472 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Figure 6.7 compares the distribution of total cholesterol in three populations with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=485 b=0 i=0 f=f5] different means. There is little overlap between people with high cholesterol levels in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=498 b=0 i=0 f=f5] population A and population C. People with high cholesterol in population A would
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=511 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be considered to have low levels in population C.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=524 b=0 i=0 f=f5] These data come from the WHO MONICA (MONItoring of trends and deter-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=537 b=0 i=0 f=f5] minants in CArdiovascular disease) Project, which comprised population surveys
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=550 b=0 i=0 f=f5] done at least twice in a decade in 38 geographically defined populations in 21
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=563 b=0 i=0 f=f5] countries.12, 20
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=576 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The figure also illustrates the principle that any cut-off point for determining
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=589 b=0 i=0 f=f5] prevalence is arbitrary, but shifting the population mean by a small amount has a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=602 b=0 i=0 f=f5] large impact. Shifting the population distribution from high levels to low levels is the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=615 b=0 i=0 f=f5] purpose of primary prevention. In Figure 6.7, we can observe that:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=637 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Population A with low mean cholesterol (4.0 mmol/l) also has a low prevalence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=649 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of hypercholesterolaemia (6%), even if the cut-off point for determining preva-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=661 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lence is set at ≥ 5.0 mmol/l.

[PAGE 120]
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=82 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Population B with a mean cholesterol of 5.4 mmol/l would classify almost two
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=95 b=0 i=0 f=f5] thirds of the population (64%) as having “high” cholesterol if the cut-off point

[L s=20.0 r=2.50 x=140 y=536 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Secondary prevention
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=140 y=568 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Secondary prevention aims to reduce the more serious consequences of disease
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=140 y=580 b=0 i=0 f=f5] through early diagnosis and treatment. It comprises the measures available to
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=140 y=593 b=0 i=0 f=f5] individuals and populations for early detection and effective intervention. It is directed
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=140 y=605 b=0 i=0 f=f5] at the period between the onset of disease and the normal time of diagnosis, and
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=140 y=618 b=0 i=0 f=f5] aims to reduce the prevalence of disease.
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=158 y=631 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Secondary prevention can be applied only to diseases in which the natural history
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=140 y=643 b=0 i=0 f=f5] includes an early period when it is easily identified and treated, so that progression
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=140 y=655 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to a more serious stage can be stopped. The two main requirements for a useful
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=140 y=668 b=0 i=0 f=f5] secondary prevention programme are a safe and accurate method of detecting the
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=140 y=680 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease – preferably at a preclinical stage – and effective methods of intervention.

[PAGE 122]
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Cervical cancer provides an example of the importance of secondary prevention
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and of the difficulties of assessing the value of prevention programmes.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Figure 6.8 shows an association between screening rates and reductions in the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] death rate from cervical cancer in selected Canadian provinces in the 1970s.23, 24 The
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] data were initially questioned because the mortality rates for cervical cancer were
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] already decreasing before organized screening programmes started. Other studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] have since supported the value of such screening programmes, which are now widely
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=175 b=0 i=0 f=f5] applied in many – but not all – countries. Few low- and middle-income countries
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=188 b=0 i=0 f=f5] have the infrastructure for organized screening programs, and most women in low-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] income countries do not have access to routine screening.25 With the advent of an

[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=573 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Tertiary prevention
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=605 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Tertiary prevention is aimed at reducing the progress or complications of established
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=618 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease and is an important aspect of therapeutic and rehabilitation medicine. It
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=630 b=0 i=0 f=f5] consists of the measures intended to reduce impairments and disabilities, minimize
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=643 b=0 i=0 f=f5] suffering caused by poor health and promote patients’ adjustment to incurable
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=655 b=0 i=0 f=f5] conditions. Tertiary prevention is often difficult to separate from treatment, since
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=668 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the treatment of chronic disease has as one of its central aims the prevention of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=680 b=0 i=0 f=f5] recurrence.

[PAGE 123]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 110 Chapter 6
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The rehabilitation of patients with poliomyelitis, strokes, injuries, blindness and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] other chronic conditions is essential to their ability to take part in daily social life.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Tertiary prevention can improve individual and family well being and income. An
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] important aspect of tertiary prevention – particularly for younger people afflicted by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] illness or injury – is restoring their ability to work and earn a livelihood. If welfare
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] systems are not functioning, even a temporary period of ill-health may cause severe
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] economic hardship for the patient and his or her family. Epidemiological studies need
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=175 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to include the economic situation of people with ill-health as one of the crucial social
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=188 b=0 i=0 f=f5] determinants of health outcomes.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=140 y=210 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Screening
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=243 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Screening people for disease – or risk factors which predict disease – is motivated by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=256 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the potential benefits of secondary prevention through early detection and treatment.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=280 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Definition
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=307 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Screening is the process of using tests on a large scale to identify the presence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=320 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of disease in apparently healthy people. Screening tests do not usually establish a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=333 b=0 i=0 f=f5] diagnosis, but rather the presence or absence of an identified risk factor, and thus
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=346 b=0 i=0 f=f5] require individual follow-up and treatment. As the recipients of screening are usually
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=359 b=0 i=0 f=f5] people who have no illness it is important that the screening test itself is very unlikely
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=372 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to cause harm.26 Screening can also be used to identify high exposure to risk factors.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=385 b=0 i=0 f=f5] For instance, children’s blood samples can be screened for lead in areas of high use
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=398 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of lead in paint.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=240 y=423 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Types of screening
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=34 y=433 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Box 6.5. Targeted screening
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=449 b=0 i=0 f=f5] When targeted screening is done in groups with occu- There are different types of screening, each with specific
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=460 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pational exposures, the criteria for screening are not aims:
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=471 b=0 i=0 f=f5] necessarily as strict as those for general population
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=482 b=0 i=0 f=f5] screening. The health effect that is prevented may be • mass screening aims to screen the whole population (or
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=493 b=0 i=0 f=f5] minor (nausea or headache), but screening may be a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=249 y=497 b=0 i=0 f=f5] subset);
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=504 b=0 i=0 f=f5] high priority if the effect reduces the patient’s ability to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=240 y=508 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • multiple or multiphasic screening uses several screening
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=515 b=0 i=0 f=f5] work. Many health effects from exposure to environ-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=249 y=520 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tests at the same time;
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=526 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mental hazards are graded, and preventing a minor effect
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=240 y=531 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • targeted screening of groups with specific exposures,
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=537 b=0 i=0 f=f5] may also prevent more serious effects. Targeted screen-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=249 y=543 b=0 i=0 f=f5] e.g. workers in lead battery factories, is often used in
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=548 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ing can be legally required – for example, in miners or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=249 y=554 b=0 i=0 f=f5] environmental and occupational health (Box 6.5); and
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=559 b=0 i=0 f=f5] people working with lead or chromium – and used in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=240 y=565 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • case-finding or opportunistic screening is aimed at pa-
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=570 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the follow-up to environmental pollution incidents,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=249 y=577 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tients who consult a health practitioner for some other
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=581 b=0 i=0 f=f5] such as methyl-mercury poisoning (Minamata disease)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=249 y=589 b=0 i=0 f=f5] purpose.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=34 y=592 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in Japan in the 1960s (see Chapter 1 and Chapter 9).
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=635 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Criteria for screening
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=663 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Table 6.4 lists the main criteria for establishing a screening programme.27 These relate
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=676 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to the characteristics of the disorder or disease, its treatment and the screening test.

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cancers, such as cancer of the cervix.
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=55 y=140 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Table 6.4. Requirements for instituting a medical screening programme

[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=122 y=276 b=0 i=0 f=f14] acceptable to both the screening authorities and to those screened.
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=55 y=288 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Equity Equity of access to screening services; effective, acceptable and safe
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=122 y=297 b=0 i=0 f=f14] treatment available
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=324 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In addition, several issues need to be addressed before establishing a screening
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=337 b=0 i=0 f=f5] programme.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=360 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Costs
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=371 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The costs of a screening programme must be balanced against the number of cases
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=384 b=0 i=0 f=f5] detected and the consequences of not screening. Generally, the prevalence of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=397 b=0 i=0 f=f5] preclinical stage of the disease should be high in the population screened, but occa-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=410 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sionally it may be worthwhile to screen even for diseases of low prevalence which
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=423 b=0 i=0 f=f5] have serious consequences, such as phenylketonuria. If children with phenylketonuria
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=436 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are identified at birth, they can be given a special diet that will allow them to develop
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=449 b=0 i=0 f=f5] normally. If they are not given the diet, they become mentally retarded and require
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=462 b=0 i=0 f=f5] special care throughout life. In spite of the low incidence of this metabolic disease
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=475 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (2–4 per 100 000 births), secondary prevention screening programmes are highly
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=488 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cost-effective.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=511 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Lead time
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=522 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The disease must have a reasonably long lead time; that is, the interval between the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=535 b=0 i=0 f=f5] time when the disease can be first diagnosed by screening and when it is usually
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=548 b=0 i=0 f=f5] diagnosed in patients presenting with symptoms. Noise-induced hearing loss has a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=561 b=0 i=0 f=f5] very long lead time; pancreatic cancer usually has a short one. A short lead time
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=574 b=0 i=0 f=f5] implies a rapidly progressing disease, and treatment initiated after screening is unlikely
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=587 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to be more effective than that begun after the more usual diagnostic procedures.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=610 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Length bias
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=621 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Early treatment should be more effective in reducing mortality or morbidity than
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=634 b=0 i=0 f=f5] treatment begun after the development of overt disease, as, for example, in the treat-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=647 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ment of cervical cancer in situ. A treatment must be effective and acceptable to people
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=660 b=0 i=0 f=f5] who are asymptomatic. If treatment is ineffective, earlier diagnosis only increases the

[PAGE 125]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 112 Chapter 6
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] time period during which the participant is aware of the disease; this effect is known
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] as length bias or length/time bias.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=120 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Screening test
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=131 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The screening test itself must be cheap, easy to apply, acceptable to the public, reliable
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=144 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and valid. A test is reliable if it provides consistent results, and valid if it correctly
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=157 b=0 i=0 f=f5] categorizes people into groups with and without disease, as measured by its sensi-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=170 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tivity and specificity.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=191 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •Sensitivity is the proportion of people with the disease in the screened popu-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=204 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lation who are identified as ill by the screening test. (When the disease is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=215 b=0 i=0 f=f5] present, how often does the test detect it?)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=226 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •Specificity is the proportion of disease-free people who are so identified by the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=238 b=0 i=0 f=f5] screening test. (When the disease is absent, how often does the test provide
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=250 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a negative result?)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=272 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The methods for calculating these measures and the positive and negative predictive
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] values are given in Table 6.5.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=298 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Although a screening test ideally is both highly sensitive and highly specific, we
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=311 b=0 i=0 f=f5] need to strike a balance between these characteristics, because most tests cannot do
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=324 b=0 i=0 f=f5] both. We determine this balance by an arbitrary cut-off point between normal and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=337 b=0 i=0 f=f5] abnormal. If we want to increase sensitivity and to include all true positives, we are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=350 b=0 i=0 f=f5] obliged to increase the number of false positives, which means decreasing specificity.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=363 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Reducing the strictness of the criteria for a positive test can increase sensitivity, but
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=376 b=0 i=0 f=f5] by doing this the test’s specificity is reduced. Likewise, increasing the strictness of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=389 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the criteria increases specificity but decreases sensitivity. We also need to account
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=402 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for predictive value when interpreting the results of screening tests.
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=432 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Table 6.5. Validity of a screening test
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=292 y=454 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Disease status
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=292 y=467 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Present Absent Total
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=483 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Screening test Positive a b a+b
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=230 y=495 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Negative c d c+d
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=230 y=507 b=0 i=1 f=f19] Total a+c b+d a+b+c+d
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=230 y=528 b=0 i=0 f=f14] a = No. of true positives, b = No. of false positives,
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=230 y=540 b=0 i=0 f=f14] c = No. of false negatives, d = No. of true negatives
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=552 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Sensitivity = probability of a positive test in people with the disease
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=237 y=561 b=0 i=1 f=f19] = a/(a+c)
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=573 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Specificity = probability of a negative test in people without the disease
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=237 y=582 b=0 i=1 f=f19] = d/(b+d)
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=594 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Positive predictive value = probability of the person having the disease when the test
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=244 y=603 b=0 i=0 f=f14] is positive
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=237 y=612 b=0 i=1 f=f19] = a/(a+b)
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=624 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Negative predictive = probability of the person not having the disease when the
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=633 b=0 i=0 f=f14] value test is negative
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=237 y=642 b=0 i=1 f=f19] = d/(c+d)

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=231 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Epidemiology and prevention: chronic noncommunicable diseases 113
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Decisions on the appropriate criteria for a screening test depend on the conse-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] quences of identifying false negatives and false positives. For a serious condition in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] newborn children, it might be preferable to have high sensitivity and to accept the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] increased cost of a high number of false positives (reduced specificity). Further follow-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] up would then be required to identify the true positives and true negatives.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=159 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Natural history
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=170 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Above all, establishing appropriate criteria requires considerable knowledge of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=183 b=0 i=0 f=f5] natural history of the disease in question and of the benefits and costs of treatment.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=196 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Adequate facilities must exist for formal diagnosis, treatment and follow-up of newly
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=209 b=0 i=0 f=f5] diagnosed cases, which could otherwise overwhelm the health services. Finally, the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=222 b=0 i=0 f=f5] screening policy and programme must be accepted by all the people involved: ad-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=235 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ministrators, health professionals and the public.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=258 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Impact

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=292 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Ideally, information should be available on disease rates A randomized controlled trial of 60 000 insured women
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=289 y=303 b=0 i=0 f=f5] aged 40–64 who were followed for up to 23 years found
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=308 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in people whose disease was identified through screening
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=289 y=314 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that mammography was effective in reducing mortality
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=321 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and in those whose disease was diagnosed on the basis
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=289 y=325 b=0 i=0 f=f5] from breast cancer (Table 6.6). Ten years after entry into
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=334 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of symptoms. Because differences are likely to exist be- the study, the breast cancer mortality was about 29%
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=347 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tween people who take part in screening programmes and lower among women who been screened than among
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=358 b=0 i=0 f=f5] people who do not, the best evidence for the effectiveness those who had not; at 18 years, the rate was about 23%
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=289 y=369 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lower.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=373 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of screening comes from the results of randomized con-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=386 b=0 i=0 f=f5] trolled trials (Box 6.6).

[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=359 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Introduction
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=391 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Definitions
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=418 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A communicable (or infectious) disease is one caused by transmission of a specific
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=431 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pathogenic agent to a susceptible host. Infectious agents may be transmitted to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=444 b=0 i=0 f=f5] humans either:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=466 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) directly, from other infected humans or animals, or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=478 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) indirectly, through vectors, airborne particles or vehicles.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=501 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Vectors are insects or other animals that carry the infectious agent from person to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=514 b=0 i=0 f=f5] person. Vehicles are contaminated objects or elements of the environment (such as
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=527 b=0 i=0 f=f5] clothes, cutlery, water, milk, food, blood, plasma, parenteral solutions or surgical
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=540 b=0 i=0 f=f5] instruments).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=553 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Contagious diseases are those that can be spread (contagious literally means “by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=566 b=0 i=0 f=f5] touch”) between humans without an intervening vector or vehicle. Malaria is there-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=579 b=0 i=0 f=f5] fore a communicable but not a contagious disease, while measles and syphilis are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=592 b=0 i=0 f=f5] both communicable and contagious. Some pathogens cause disease not only through
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=605 b=0 i=0 f=f5] infection but through the toxic effect of chemical compounds that these produce.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=618 b=0 i=0 f=f5] For example, Staphylococcus aureus is a bacteria that can infect humans directly, but
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=631 b=0 i=0 f=f5] staphylococcal food poisoning is caused by ingestion of food contaminated with a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=644 b=0 i=0 f=f5] toxin that the bacteria produces.

[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=145 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Epidemic and endemic disease
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=177 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Epidemics
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=205 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemics are defined as the occurrence of cases in excess of what is normally
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=218 b=0 i=0 f=f5] expected in a community or region. When describing an epidemic, the time period,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=231 b=0 i=0 f=f5] geographical region and particulars of the population in which the cases occur must
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=244 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be specified.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=257 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The number of cases needed to define an epidemic
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=270 b=0 i=0 f=f5] varies according to the agent, the size, type and suscep-

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=283 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tibility of population exposed, and the time and place of disease in disability adjusted life years (DALYs), all
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=296 b=0 i=0 f=f5] occurrence. The identification of an epidemic also de- ages, 20051
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=309 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pends on the usual frequency of the disease in the area Communicable diseases,

[L s=7.0 r=0.70 x=333 y=472 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Injuries including mental disorders
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=478 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The dynamic of an epidemic is determined by the 13% 28%
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=491 b=0 i=0 f=f5] characteristics of its agent and its pattern of transmission,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=504 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and by the susceptibility of its human hosts. The three
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=517 b=0 i=0 f=f5] main groups of pathogenic agents act very differently in this respect. A limited number
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=530 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of bacteria, viruses and parasites cause most epidemics, and a thorough understand-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=543 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ing of their biology has improved specific prevention measures. Vaccines, the most
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=556 b=0 i=0 f=f5] effective means of preventing infectious diseases, have been developed so far only
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=569 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for some viral and bacterial diseases. If the attempt to make a malaria vaccine is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=582 b=0 i=0 f=f5] successful, this will be the first vaccine for a parasitic disease. Vaccines work on both
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=595 b=0 i=0 f=f5] an individual basis, by preventing or attenuating clinical disease in a person exposed
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=608 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to the pathogen, and also on a population basis, by affecting herd immunity
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=621 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (Figure 7.4).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=634 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In a point-source epidemic, susceptible individuals are exposed more or less
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=647 b=0 i=0 f=f5] simultaneously to one source of infection. This results in a very rapid increase in the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=660 b=0 i=0 f=f5] number of cases, often in a few hours. The cholera epidemic (a bacterial disease)

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=608 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In a contagious, or propagated, epidemic the disease is passed from person to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=621 b=0 i=0 f=f5] person and the initial rise in the number of cases is slower. The number of susceptible
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=634 b=0 i=0 f=f5] individuals and the potential sources of infection are the critical factors in determining
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=648 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the spread of disease. For example, SARS was first recognized as a global threat in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=660 b=0 i=0 f=f5] March 2003. It spread rapidly to 26 countries, affecting adult men and women, with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=673 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a fifth of all cases occurring among health-care workers (see Chapter 1).

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=350 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Endemic diseases
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=378 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Communicable diseases are termed endemic when they have a relatively stable
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=391 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pattern of occurrence in a given geographical area or population group at relatively
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=404 b=0 i=0 f=f5] high prevalence and incidence. Endemic diseases such as malaria are among the major
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=417 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health problems in low-income tropical countries. If conditions change in the host,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=430 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the agent or the environment, an endemic disease may become epidemic. For exam-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=443 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ple, improved smallpox control in Europe was reversed during the First World War
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=456 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (Table 7.1).
