[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=491 b=0 i=0 f=f10] The infectious agent
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=518 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A large number of microorganisms cause disease in humans. Infection is the entry
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=531 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and development or multiplication of an infectious agent in the host. Infection is not
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=544 b=0 i=0 f=f5] equivalent to disease, as some infections do not produce clinical disease. The specific
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=557 b=0 i=0 f=f5] characteristics of each agent are important in determining the nature of the infection,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=570 b=0 i=0 f=f5] which is determined by such factors as:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=593 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) The pathogenicity of the agent: its ability to produce disease, measured by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=83 y=604 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the ratio of the number of persons developing clinical illness to the number
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=83 y=616 b=0 i=0 f=f5] exposed.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=627 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Virulence: a measure of the severity of disease, which can vary from very low
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=83 y=639 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to very high. Once a virus has been attenuated in a laboratory and is of low
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=83 y=650 b=0 i=0 f=f5] virulence, it can be used for immunization, as with the poliomyelitis virus.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=662 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Infective dose: the amount required to cause infection in susceptible subjects.

[PAGE 137]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 124 Chapter 7
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) The reservoir of an agent: its natural habitat, which may include humans,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=95 b=0 i=0 f=f5] animals and environmental sources.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=106 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) The source of infection: the person or object from which the host acquires
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=118 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the agent. Knowledge of both the reservoir and the source is necessary if
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=129 b=0 i=0 f=f5] effective control measures are to be developed. An important source of in-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=141 b=0 i=0 f=f5] fection may be a carrier – an infected person who shows no evidence of clinical
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=152 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease. The duration of the carrier state varies between agents. Carriers can
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=164 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be asymptomatic throughout the course of infection or the carrier state may
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=175 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be limited to a particular phase of the disease. Carriers played a large role in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=187 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the worldwide spread of the human immunodeficiency virus due to inadver-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=198 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tent sexual transmission during the long asymptomatic period.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=221 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Transmission
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=249 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The second link in the chain of infection is the transmission or spread of an infectious
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=262 b=0 i=0 f=f5] agent through the environment or to another person. Transmission may be direct or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=275 b=0 i=0 f=f5] indirect (Table 7.2).
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=305 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Table 7.2. Modes of transmission of an infectious agent
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=327 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Direct transmission Indirect transmission
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=343 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Touching Vehicle-borne (contaminated food, water,
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=352 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Kissing towels, farm tools, etc.)
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=364 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Sexual intercourse Vector-borne (insects, animals)
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=376 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Other contact (e.g. childbirth, medical Airborne, long-distance (dust, droplets)
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=386 b=0 i=0 f=f14] procedures, injection of drugs, Parenteral (injections with contaminated
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=394 b=0 i=0 f=f14] breastfeeding) syringes)
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=406 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Airborne, short-distance (via droplets,
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=415 b=0 i=0 f=f14] coughing, sneezing)
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=427 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Transfusion (blood)
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=439 b=0 i=0 f=f14] Transplacental
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=467 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Direct transmission
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=477 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Direct transmission is the immediate transfer of the infectious agent from an infected
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=490 b=0 i=0 f=f5] host or reservoir to an appropriate entry point through which human infection can
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=503 b=0 i=0 f=f5] take place. This may be by direct contact such as touching, kissing or sexual inter-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=516 b=0 i=0 f=f5] course, or by the direct spread of droplets by sneezing or coughing. Blood transfusions
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=529 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and transplacental infection from mother to fetus are other important means of direct
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=542 b=0 i=0 f=f5] transmission.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=566 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Indirect transmission
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=576 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Indirect transmission may be vehicle-borne, vector-borne or airborne. Vehicle-borne
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=589 b=0 i=0 f=f5] transmission occurs through contaminated materials such as food, clothes, bedding
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=602 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and cooking utensils. Vector-borne transmission occurs when the agent is carried by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=615 b=0 i=0 f=f5] an insect or animal (the vector) to a susceptible host; the agent may or may not
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=628 b=0 i=0 f=f5] multiply in the vector. Long-distance airborne transmission occurs when there is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=641 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dissemination of very small droplets to a suitable point of entry, usually the respiratory
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=654 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tract. Dust particles also facilitate airborne transmission, for example, of fungal spores.

[PAGE 138]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=232 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Communicable diseases: epidemiology surveillance and response 125
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] It is important to distinguish between types of transmission when selecting
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] control methods. Direct transmission can be interrupted by preventing contact with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the source; indirect transmission requires different approaches, such as the provision
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of mosquito nets, adequate ventilation, cold storage for foods or sterile syringes and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] needles.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=160 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Host
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=187 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The host is the third link in the chain of infection and is defined as the person or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] animal that provides a suitable place for an infectious agent to grow and multiply
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f5] under natural conditions. The points of entry to the host vary with the agent and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=226 b=0 i=0 f=f5] include the skin, mucous membranes, and the respiratory and gastrointestinal tracts.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=239 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The reaction of the host to infection is extremely variable, being determined by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=253 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the interaction between host, agent and mode of transmission. The spectrum of this
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=265 b=0 i=0 f=f5] reaction ranges from no apparent signs or symptoms to severe clinical illness, with all
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=278 b=0 i=0 f=f5] possible variations between these two extremes. The incubation period—the time
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=292 b=0 i=0 f=f5] between entry of the infectious agent and the appearance of the first sign or symptom
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=304 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the disease—varies from a few hours (staphylococcal food poisoning) to years
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=317 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (AIDS).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=331 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The consequences of infection are largely determined by the host's resistance.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=343 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Such resistance is usually acquired through previous exposure to or immunization
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=356 b=0 i=0 f=f5] against the agent in question. Immunization (or vaccination) is the protection of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=369 b=0 i=0 f=f5] susceptible individuals from communicable disease by the administration of a vac-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=382 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cine, which can be:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=405 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) a living modified infectious agent (as for measles)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=417 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) inactivated organisms (as for pertussis)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=429 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) an inactive toxin (as for tetanus)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=441 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) bacterial polysaccharides.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=463 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Antibodies – which are formed as part of the natural immune response to pathogens –
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=476 b=0 i=0 f=f5] can be pooled from blood donations and given as post-exposure prophylaxis for a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=490 b=0 i=0 f=f5] few diseases (such as rabies, diphtheria, varicella-zoster and hepatitis B) to people
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=502 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that have not been adequately immunized. This is called passive immunization, and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=515 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is done on a much smaller scale than active immunization due to its risks, indications
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=528 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and cost. Passive transmission of maternal antibodies through the placenta can also
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=541 b=0 i=0 f=f5] confer resistance to infection in the fetus.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=566 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Environment
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=593 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The environment plays a critical role in the development of communicable diseases.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=606 b=0 i=0 f=f5] General sanitation, temperature, air pollution and water quality are among the factors
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=619 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that influence all stages in the chain of infection (see Chapter 9). In addition, so-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=632 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cioeconomic factors – such as population density, overcrowding and poverty – are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=645 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of great importance.

[PAGE 139]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 126 Chapter 7
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=140 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Investigation and control of epidemics
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=116 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The purpose of investigating a communicable disease epidemic is to identify its cause
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=129 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and the best means to control it. This requires detailed and systematic epidemiological
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=142 b=0 i=0 f=f5] work, in the following sequential or simultaneous steps:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=164 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) undertaking preliminary investigation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=176 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) identifying and notifying cases
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=188 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) collecting and analysing data
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=200 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) managing and controlling
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=212 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) disseminating findings and follow-up.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=236 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Investigation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=263 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The initial stage of investigation should verify the diagnoses of suspected cases and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=276 b=0 i=0 f=f5] confirm that an epidemic exists. The preliminary investigation also leads to the for-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=289 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mulation of hypotheses about the source and spread of the disease, and this in turn
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=302 b=0 i=0 f=f5] may lead to immediate control measures. Early reports of a possible epidemic may be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=315 b=0 i=0 f=f5] based on observations made by a small number of health workers or may reflect figures
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=328 b=0 i=0 f=f5] gathered by the formal communicable disease notification system that operates in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=341 b=0 i=0 f=f5] most countries. Sometimes reports from several health districts are needed; the num-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=354 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ber of cases in a single area may be too small to draw attention to an epidemic.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=378 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Identifying cases
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=406 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The investigation of a suspected epidemic requires that new cases be systematically
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=419 b=0 i=0 f=f5] identified, and this means that what constitutes a case must be clearly defined (see
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=432 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Chapter 2). Often, detailed information on at least a sample of the cases needs to be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=445 b=0 i=0 f=f5] collected. The cases reported early in an epidemic are often only a small proportion
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=458 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the total; a thorough count of all cases is necessary to permit a full description of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=471 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the extent of the epidemic. As soon as an epidemic is confirmed, the first priority is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=484 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to control it. In severe contagious epidemics, it is often necessary to follow up con-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=497 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tacts of reported cases to ensure the identification of all cases and limit the spread of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=510 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the disease.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=534 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Management and control
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=562 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The management of an epidemic involves treating the cases, preventing further spread
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=575 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the disease and monitoring the effects of control measures. Treatment is straight-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=588 b=0 i=0 f=f5] forward except in large-scale epidemics – especially when these occur as a result of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=601 b=0 i=0 f=f5] social or environmental disruption – for which external resources may be needed.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=614 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The public health action required in emergencies caused by epidemics of different
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=627 b=0 i=0 f=f5] diseases has been described in detail.16
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=640 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Control measures can be directed against the source and spread of infection and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=653 b=0 i=0 f=f5] towards protecting people exposed to it. Usually all of these approaches are required.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=666 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In some cases, however, removing the source of infection may be all that is necessary,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=679 b=0 i=0 f=f5] as when a contaminated food is withdrawn from sale. An essential component of

[PAGE 140]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=232 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Communicable diseases: epidemiology surveillance and response 127
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] control measures is to inform health professionals and the public of the likely causes,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the risk of contracting the disease and the essential control steps. This is particularly
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] important if exposed people need to be protected through immunization, for example
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in containing an outbreak of measles (Box 7.5)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Once control measures have been implemented,

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=253 b=0 i=0 f=f5] have had some effect. Since the first cases were identified,

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=292 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Likewise, needle exchange programs for intravenous drug users have been used suc-

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=344 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The HIV epidemic may have peaked in some African countries and in India. Inci-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dence (new infections) of HIV apparently peaked in Kenya in the early to mid-1990s.18
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=370 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Because of the latency between HIV infection and death, prevalence continued to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=383 b=0 i=0 f=f5] rise as incidence fell, peaking around 1997 when mortality rose to match incidence.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=396 b=0 i=0 f=f5] HIV prevalence (the rate of existing infections) has also declined in south India. This
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=409 b=0 i=0 f=f5] reversal in trends can be partly attributed to intervention efforts that aim to decrease
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=422 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the number of concurrent sexual partners and increase effective use of condoms.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=446 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Surveillance and response
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=474 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Definition
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=485 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Health surveillance is the ongoing systematic collection, analysis and interpretation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=498 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of health data essential for planning, implementing and evaluating public health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=511 b=0 i=0 f=f5] activities. Surveillance needs to be linked to timely dissemination of the data, so that
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=524 b=0 i=0 f=f5] effective action can be taken to prevent disease. Surveillance mechanisms include
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=537 b=0 i=0 f=f5] compulsory notification regarding specific diseases, specific disease registries
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=550 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (population-based or hospital-based), continuous or repeated population surveys and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=563 b=0 i=0 f=f5] aggregate data that show trends of consumption patterns and economic activity.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=586 b=1 i=1 f=f18] The scope of surveillance
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=597 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The scope of surveillance is broad, from early warning systems for rapid response in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=610 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the case of communicable diseases, to planned response in the case of chronic dis-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=623 b=0 i=0 f=f5] eases which generally have a longer lag time between exposure and disease. Most
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=636 b=0 i=0 f=f5] countries have regulations for mandatory reporting of a list of diseases. These lists
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=649 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of notifiable diseases often include vaccine-preventable diseases such as polio,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=662 b=0 i=0 f=f5] measles, tetanus and diphtheria as well as other communicable diseases such as
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=675 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tuberculosis, hepatitis, meningitis and leprosy. Reporting may be required also for

[PAGE 141]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 128 Chapter 7
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] non-communicable conditions, such as maternal deaths, injuries and occupational
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=240 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and environmental diseases such as pesticide poisoning.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=240 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Mandatory reporting of specific conditions is a subset of

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=52 y=183 b=0 i=0 f=f5] assess trends; surveillance can effectively lead to prevention. Another

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=257 y=328 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) information gaps on new diseases.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=361 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Sources of data
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=372 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Sources of data may be general or disease-specific, and include the following:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=394 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) mortality and morbidity reports
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=406 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) hospital records
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=418 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) laboratory diagnoses
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=430 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) outbreak reports
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=442 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) vaccine utilization
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=454 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) sickness absence records
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=466 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) biological changes in agent, vectors, or reservoirs
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=478 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) blood banks.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=501 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Surveillance can collect data on any element of the causal chain of disease –
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=514 b=0 i=0 f=f5] behavioural risk factors, preventive actions, cases and program or treatment costs.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=527 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The scope of a surveillance system is constrained by human and financial resources.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=550 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Surveillance in practice
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=561 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Surveillance relies upon a routine system of reporting suspected cases within the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=574 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health system, followed by validation and confirmation. Active and appropriate
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=587 b=0 i=0 f=f5] responses ranging from local containment measures to investigation and containment
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=600 b=0 i=0 f=f5] by a highly specialized team, are then put in place.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=613 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Surveillance requires continuing scrutiny of all aspects of the occurrence and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=626 b=0 i=0 f=f5] spread of disease, generally using methods distinguished by their practicability, uni-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=639 b=0 i=0 f=f5] formity and, frequently, their rapidity, rather than by complete accuracy. The analysis
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=652 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of data from a surveillance system indicates whether there has been a significant
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=665 b=0 i=0 f=f5] increase in the reported number of cases. In many countries, unfortunately, surveil-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=678 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lance systems are inadequate, particularly if they depend on voluntary notification.

[L s=8.0 r=0.89 x=147 y=92 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Box 7.8. Millennium Development Goals (MDGs)
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=107 b=0 i=0 f=f5] United Nations Member States unanimously adopted the Millennium Declaration in
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=118 b=0 i=0 f=f5] September 2000, setting 2015 as the year by which these overall development goals should
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=129 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be achieved. Eight MDGs were established as part of the road map for implementing the
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=140 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Millennium Declaration. These goals concern poverty and hunger, education, gender in-
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=151 b=0 i=0 f=f5] equality, child mortality, maternal mortality, HIV/AIDS and other major communicable
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] diseases, environmental sustainability, and the need for global partnership in development
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=173 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (see http://millenniumindicators.un.org/unsd/mi/mi_goals.asp for specific goals, targets,
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=184 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and indicators).
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=160 y=195 b=0 i=0 f=f5] While only three goals are explicitly health-focused, all have strong links to health. The
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=206 b=0 i=0 f=f5] MDGs emphasize reciprocal obligations between high-income and low- and middle-
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=217 b=0 i=0 f=f5] income countries.20 They hold to account the authorities responsible for providing health
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=228 b=0 i=0 f=f5] services, and they help define the role of health in development. By setting quantitative
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=239 b=0 i=0 f=f5] targets and encouraging steady monitoring of progress, the MDGs maintain awareness of
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=250 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the urgent need for action. One of the challenges raised by the MDGs is measuring progress.
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=261 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Sound epidemiological information is essential for tracking progress, evaluating impact and
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=272 b=0 i=0 f=f5] attributing changes to different interventions, as well as for guiding decisions on program
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=283 b=0 i=0 f=f5] scope and focus.
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=158 y=316 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Factors that influence effectiveness of surveillance systems are listed in
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=140 y=329 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Table 7.4.

[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=168 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Key messages
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=182 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Clinical epidemiology is the application of epidemiological principles and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=194 b=0 i=0 f=f5] methods to the practice of medicine.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=205 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) With rising health-care costs, clinical practice has become a common subject
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=217 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of epidemiological research.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=228 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Evidence-based guidelines have improved clinical outcomes.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=240 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) However, effective treatments are not fully used, and ineffective or costly and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=251 b=0 i=0 f=f5] unnecessary treatments are still being prescribed.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=279 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Introduction
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=311 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Clinical epidemiology is the application of epidemiological principles and methods
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=324 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to the practice of clinical medicine. It usually involves a study conducted in a clinical
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=337 b=0 i=0 f=f5] setting, most often by clinicians, with patients as the subjects of study. The discipline
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=350 b=0 i=0 f=f5] refines methods developed in epidemiology and integrates them with the science of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=363 b=0 i=0 f=f5] clinical medicine. The aim of clinical epidemiology is to aid decision-making about
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=376 b=0 i=0 f=f5] identified cases of disease. Clinical epidemiology – which includes the methods used
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=389 b=0 i=0 f=f5] by clinicians to audit the processes and outcomes of their work – is a basic medical
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=402 b=0 i=0 f=f5] science.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=415 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Because epidemiology deals with populations and clinical medicine deals with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=428 b=0 i=0 f=f5] individuals, it has been suggested that clinical epidemiology is a contradiction in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=441 b=0 i=0 f=f5] terms. However, clinical epidemiology is simply concerned with a defined patient
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=454 b=0 i=0 f=f5] population rather than the usual community-based population.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=467 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The central concerns of clinical epidemiology are:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=490 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) definitions of normality and abnormality
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=502 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) accuracy of diagnostic tests
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=514 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) natural history and prognosis of disease
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=526 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) effectiveness of treatment and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=538 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) prevention in clinical practice.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=560 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Definitions of normality and abnormality
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=592 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The first priority in any clinical consultation is to determine whether the patient’s
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=605 b=0 i=0 f=f5] symptoms, signs or diagnostic test results are normal or abnormal. This is necessary
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=617 b=0 i=0 f=f5] before any further investigations or treatment. It would be easy if a clear distinction
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=630 b=0 i=0 f=f5] could always be made between measurements of normal and abnormal people. Re-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=642 b=0 i=0 f=f5] grettably, this is rarely the case, except in genetic disorders determined by a single
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=655 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dominant gene. Measurements of health-related variables can be expressed as fre-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=667 b=0 i=0 f=f5] quency distributions in the population of patients. Occasionally the frequency
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=680 b=0 i=0 f=f5] distributions for abnormal and normal measurements are quite different, but more

[PAGE 147]
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] often there is only one distribution and the so-called abnormal people are at the tail
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=96 b=0 i=0 f=f5] end of the normal distribution (see Chapter 4). There are three ways of distinguishing
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=109 b=0 i=0 f=f5] results in such a distribution:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=131 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) normal as common
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=143 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) abnormal as associated with disease
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=155 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) abnormal as treatable.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=178 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Normal as common
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=206 b=0 i=0 f=f5] This definition classifies values that occur frequently as normal and those that occur
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=218 b=0 i=0 f=f5] infrequently as abnormal. We assume that an arbitrary cut-off point on the frequency
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=231 b=0 i=0 f=f5] distribution (often two standard deviations above or below the mean) is the limit
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=243 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of normality and consider all values beyond this point abnormal. This is called an
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=256 b=0 i=0 f=f5] operational definition of abnormality. If the distribution is in fact Gaussian (normal
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=268 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in the statistical sense), we would identify 2.5% of the population as abnormal by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=281 b=0 i=0 f=f5] using this cut-off. An alternative approach, which does not assume a statistically
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=293 b=0 i=0 f=f5] normal distribution, is to use percentiles: we can consider that the 95th percentile
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=306 b=0 i=0 f=f5] point is the dividing line between normal and abnormally high values, thus classifying
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=318 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 5% of the population as abnormal (see Chapter 4).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=331 b=0 i=0 f=f5] However, there is no biological basis for using an arbitrary cut-off point as a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=344 b=0 i=0 f=f5] definition of abnormality for most variables. For example, there is a continuous
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] association between systolic blood pressure and cardiovascular disease (Figure 8.1).
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=27 y=387 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Figure 8.1.Associations between blood pressure and heart disease and stroke1
[L s=7.0 r=0.70 x=66 y=585 b=0 i=0 f=f13] 70
[L s=7.0 r=0.70 x=89 y=600 b=1 i=0 f=f12] Usual diastolic blood pressure (mm Hg)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=625 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Even within the normal ranges, as determined statistically, there is an increased
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=638 b=0 i=0 f=f5] risk of disease compared with lower levels. Risk is a graded process; there is no cut-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=651 b=0 i=0 f=f5] off point at which risk suddenly begins to increase. Most deaths from coronary heart
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=664 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease happen in people who have serum cholesterol levels in the middle of the

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=375 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Clinical epidemiology 135
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] population range; only a small proportion of deaths occur in people who have very
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] high serum cholesterol levels.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=121 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Abnormality associated with disease
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=148 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The distinction between normal and abnormal can be based on the distribution of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the measurements for both healthy and diseased people, and we can attempt to define
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=174 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a cut-off point that clearly separates the two groups. A comparison of two frequency
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=187 b=0 i=0 f=f5] distributions often shows considerable overlap – as illustrated by serum cholesterol
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=200 b=0 i=0 f=f5] distributions for people with and without coronary heart disease. Choosing a cut-off
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f5] point that neatly separates cases from non-cases is clearly impossible (see Figure 6.7).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=226 b=0 i=0 f=f5] There are always some healthy people on the abnormal side of the cut-off point, and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=239 b=0 i=0 f=f5] some true cases on the normal side.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=253 b=0 i=0 f=f5] These two types of classification error can be expressed quantitatively in terms
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=265 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the sensitivity and specificity of a test, as discussed in Chapter 6.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=288 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Sensitivity is the proportion of truly diseased people who are categorized as
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=299 b=0 i=0 f=f5] abnormal by the test.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=311 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Specificity is the proportion of truly normal people categorized as normal by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=322 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the test. A balance always has to be struck between sensitivity and specificity;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=334 b=0 i=0 f=f5] increasing one reduces the other.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Abnormal as treatable
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=385 b=0 i=0 f=f5] These difficulties in distinguishing accurately between normal and abnormal have led
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=398 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to the use of criteria determined by evidence from randomized controlled trials, which
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=411 b=0 i=0 f=f5] can be designed to detect the point at which treatment does more good than harm.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=424 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Unfortunately, many treatment decisions have to be made in the absence of such
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=437 b=0 i=0 f=f5] evidence.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=450 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The treatment of high blood pressure is a good example – early clinical trials
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=463 b=0 i=0 f=f5] provided firm evidence that treating very high diastolic blood pressure (≥ 120mmHg)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=476 b=0 i=0 f=f5] was beneficial. Subsequent trials have indicated that the benefits of treatment out-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=489 b=0 i=0 f=f5] weigh the problems at lower levels of diastolic pressure, perhaps as low as 90mmHg.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=502 b=0 i=0 f=f5] However, such trials are usually not designed to account for other risk factors
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=515 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or the cost of treatment. More sophisticated cost-effectiveness studies may make it
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=528 b=0 i=0 f=f5] possible to factor the economic consequences of treatment into clinical decisions.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=541 b=0 i=0 f=f5] We could then determine blood pressure levels at which treatment makes economic
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=554 b=0 i=0 f=f5] as well as medical sense for men and women in specific groups at risk. Treating a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=567 b=0 i=0 f=f5] young woman with a diastolic blood pressure of 90 mmHg, who is at low overall
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=580 b=0 i=0 f=f5] risk of cardiovascular disease, will be much less cost-effective than treating an older
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=593 b=0 i=0 f=f5] man with a diastolic blood pressure of 90 mmHg, who has a much greater risk of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=606 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cardiovascular disease. However, if the treatment of the young woman has no neg-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=619 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ative side-effects for her except the cost, she may choose to pay for the treatment
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=632 b=0 i=0 f=f5] herself.2
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=645 b=0 i=0 f=f5] What is considered to be worth treating changes with time; Figure 8.2 shows
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=658 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the changing definition of treatable levels of blood pressure. As clinical trials gather
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=671 b=0 i=0 f=f5] new evidence, treatment recommendations tend to change.

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=581 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Value of a test
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=609 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A disease may be either present or absent and a test result either positive or negative.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=622 b=0 i=0 f=f5] There are thus four possible combinations of disease status and test result, as shown
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=634 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in Figure 8.3 and described in relation to screening tests in Chapter 6.

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to be expensive and invasive, simpler and cheaper tests False True
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=307 y=220 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Negative
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=225 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are used in routine clinical practice. Even then, we still negative negative
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=240 b=0 i=0 f=f5] need to know and account for the validity, accuracy
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=253 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and precision of these tests when interpreting the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=266 b=0 i=0 f=f5] results.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=279 b=0 i=0 f=f5] To determine the practical utility of a given test, we need to know more about
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=292 b=0 i=0 f=f5] how it performs. A test’s positive and negative predictive value are particularly im-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=305 b=0 i=0 f=f5] portant. The positive predictive value is the probability of disease in a patient with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=318 b=0 i=0 f=f5] an abnormal test result, while the negative predictive value is the probability of a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=331 b=0 i=0 f=f5] patient not having a disease when the test result is negative.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=344 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Predictive value depends on the sensitivity and specificity of the test and, most
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] importantly, on the prevalence of the disease in the population being tested. Even
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=370 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with a high sensitivity and high specificity (Chapter 6), if the prevalence is low the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=383 b=0 i=0 f=f5] positive predictive value of a test may be very low. The predictive values of a test in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=396 b=0 i=0 f=f5] clinical practice depend critically on the prevalence of the abnormality in the patients
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=409 b=0 i=0 f=f5] being tested; this may well differ from the prevalence in a published study on the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=422 b=0 i=0 f=f5] usefulness of the test.5
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=444 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Natural history and prognosis
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=477 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The term natural history refers to the stages of a disease, which include:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=499 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) pathological onset;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=511 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) the presymptomatic stage, from onset of pathological changes to the first
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=523 b=0 i=0 f=f5] appearance of symptoms or signs; and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=534 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) the stage when the disease is clinically obvious and may be subject to remis-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=546 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sions and relapses, regress spontaneously or progress to death.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=568 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Detection and treatment at any stage can alter the natural history of a disease, but
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=581 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the effects of treatment can only be determined if the natural history of the disease
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=594 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in the absence of treatment is known.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=618 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Prognosis
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=645 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Prognosis is the prediction of the course of a disease and is expressed as the prob-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=658 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ability that a particular event will occur in the future. Predictions are based on defined
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=671 b=0 i=0 f=f5] groups of patients, and the outcome may be quite different for individual patients.

[PAGE 151]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 138 Chapter 8
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] However, knowledge of the likely prognosis is helpful in determining the most useful
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] treatment. Prognostic factors are characteristics associated with outcome in patients
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with the disease in question. For example, in a patient with acute myocardial infarc-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tion, the prognosis is directly related to residual heart muscle function.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemiological information from many patients is necessary to provide sound
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] predictions on prognosis and outcome. Clinical experience alone is inadequate for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] this purpose, since it is often based on a limited set of patients and inadequate follow-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=175 b=0 i=0 f=f5] up. For example, patients who are seen by a doctor are not necessarily representative
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=188 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of all patients with a particular disease. Patients may be selected according to severity
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or other features of their disease, or by demographic, social or personal characteristics
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the patients themselves. Furthermore, since many doctors do not systematically
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=227 b=0 i=0 f=f5] follow their patients, they have a limited, and often pessimistic, view of the prognosis
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=240 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of disease. A clinical observation of improved prognosis over time can be real and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=253 b=0 i=0 f=f5] due to better treatment, but it can also be an artefact because of an increase in milder
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=266 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cases receiving treatment. Properly designed epidemiological research can produce
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=279 b=0 i=0 f=f5] reliable information about prognosis.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=303 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Quality of life
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=331 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Ideally, the assessment of prognosis should include measurement of all clinically
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=343 b=0 i=0 f=f5] relevant outcomes and not just death, since patients are usually as interested in the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] quality of life as they are in its duration. In studies to determine natural history and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=369 b=0 i=0 f=f5] prognosis, the group of patients should be randomly selected; otherwise selection
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=382 b=0 i=0 f=f5] bias may compromise the quality of information obtained. For example, the prognosis
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=395 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of patients with chest pain admitted to hospital is likely to be worse than that of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=408 b=0 i=0 f=f5] patients with chest pain seen by health workers in the community.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=433 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Quantity of life
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=460 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Prognosis in terms of mortality is measured as case-fatality rate or probability of sur-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=473 b=0 i=0 f=f5] vival. Both the date of onset and the duration of follow-up must be clearly specified.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=486 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Survival analysis is a simple method of measuring prognosis. The pattern of survival
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=499 b=0 i=0 f=f5] following acute myocardial infarction is shown in Figure 8.4. Survival analyses may
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=512 b=0 i=0 f=f5] include selected groups, such as patients who survive the initial month after an event.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=525 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Significantly more people in the later cohort (1991–92) survived three years after a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=538 b=0 i=0 f=f5] myocardial infarction than did their counterparts 10 years earlier, which suggests
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=551 b=0 i=0 f=f5] improvement in secondary prevention of coronary heart disease.6
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=564 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Life-table analysis is a more sophisticated method that attempts to predict
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=577 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the onset of events over time from previous patterns for all patients at risk. In the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=590 b=0 i=0 f=f5] follow-up of cohorts to determine prognosis, bias is often introduced by the initial
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=603 b=0 i=0 f=f5] selection strategy and incomplete follow-up.

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=450 b=0 i=0 f=f5] countries are unlikely to be immediately feasible in low- and middle-income countries.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=463 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Specific national guidelines are essential. Guidelines can help curb practices like the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=476 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sale of drugs without prescription by providers who may have financial incentives for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=489 b=0 i=0 f=f5] selling certain products.13 Up to 70% of drug expenditure may be unnecessary in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=502 b=0 i=0 f=f5] some countries.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=140 y=525 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Prevention in clinical practice
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=557 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Sound epidemiological knowledge encourages the practice of prevention in the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=570 b=0 i=0 f=f5] context of ordinary clinical practice. Much of this prevention is at the secondary or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=583 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tertiary level, but primary prevention can also be implemented on a routine basis
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=596 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (see Chapter 6). Paediatricians have long been involved in child immunization pro-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=609 b=0 i=0 f=f5] grammes, screening for inborn metabolic defects such as phenylketonuria, and the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=622 b=0 i=0 f=f5] regular weighing of children and use of standard growth charts. Antenatal care is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=635 b=0 i=0 f=f5] another good example of the integration of prevention into routine clinical practice.

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Reducing risks
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=111 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Doctors, dentists and other health workers are able to convince at least some of their
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=124 b=0 i=0 f=f5] patients to stop smoking. A controlled trial of different anti-smoking interventions in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=137 b=0 i=0 f=f5] general practice showed that routine advice about tobacco use is useful, and that its
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=150 b=0 i=0 f=f5] effectiveness can be improved with a variety of techniques (Figure 8.5). In some
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=163 b=0 i=0 f=f5] countries, as many as 60% of current tobacco users reported receiving advice to quit
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=176 b=0 i=0 f=f5] smoking from their doctors.15 Clinicians can improve their efforts to persuade patients
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=189 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to stop smoking by:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=211 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) enhancing the quality of the intervention offered
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=223 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) focusing on smokers who are ready to quit
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=235 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) increasing frequency of advice to patients
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=247 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) linking with other tobacco-control intervention channels.

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=524 b=0 i=0 f=f5] There are many other opportunities for health professionals to offer practical advice
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=537 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and support to patients with the hope of preventing new diseases or exacerbation of

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=587 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Reducing risks in patients with established disease
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=615 b=0 i=0 f=f5] For cardiovascular disease and diabetes, evidence-based approaches to reducing the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=628 b=0 i=0 f=f5] risk of adverse outcomes in those with the disease are very similar to the approaches
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=641 b=0 i=0 f=f5] used to reduce disease onset. The major difference is that the risk for future clinical
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=654 b=0 i=0 f=f5] events is much greater once disease is established. Both behavioural and pharmaco-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=667 b=0 i=0 f=f5] logical interventions, amongst others, have been shown to affect the prognosis of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=680 b=0 i=0 f=f5] these diseases.

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=470 y=53 b=1 i=1 f=f8] 145
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Chapter 9
[L s=32.0 r=3.20 x=55 y=101 b=0 i=0 f=f7] Environmental and
[L s=32.0 r=3.20 x=55 y=135 b=0 i=0 f=f7] occupational epidemiology
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=167 y=183 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Key messages
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • The environment in which we live and work strongly influences the causation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=228 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of disease and injuries.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=237 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Exposures to environmental factors can be quantified as a “dose” which is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=251 b=0 i=0 f=f5] used to establish dose-effect and dose–response relationships.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=259 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Health impact assessments are used to forecast the likely health impact of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=274 b=0 i=0 f=f5] major human interventions in the environment.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=283 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Injury epidemiology has been used to identify which specific preventive ac-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=297 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tions are most likely to be effective.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=325 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Environment and health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The human environment consists of very basic elements: the air we breathe, the water
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=370 b=0 i=0 f=f5] we drink, the food we eat, the climate surrounding our bodies and the space available
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=383 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for our movements. In addition, we exist in a social and cultural environment, which
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=396 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is of great importance for our mental and physical health.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=409 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Most diseases are either caused or influenced by environmental factors. We need
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=422 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to understand the ways in which specific environmental factors can interfere with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=435 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health to design effective prevention programmes. Environmental epidemiology pro-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=448 b=0 i=0 f=f5] vides a scientific basis for studying and interpreting the relationships between the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=461 b=0 i=0 f=f5] environment and population health. Occupational epidemiology deals specifically
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=474 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with environmental factors in the workplace. Physical injuries are strongly dependent
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=487 b=0 i=0 f=f5] on factors in the living or working environment but are also strongly determined by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=500 b=0 i=0 f=f5] behavioural factors. In common language the word “accident” is often applied to the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=513 b=0 i=0 f=f5] events that precede an injury, but it can be misleading as the word accident implies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=526 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a random event rather than a combination of predictable causal factors. In this chapter
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=539 b=0 i=0 f=f5] we shall use the term “environment” as a broad term for all factors external to the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=552 b=0 i=0 f=f5] body that may cause disease or injury. The different environmental factors that in-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=565 b=0 i=0 f=f5] fluence health are shown in Table 9.1.1
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=72 y=578 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The environmental and occupational health field includes a large number of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=591 b=0 i=0 f=f5] specific and proximate causal factors using the concepts described for hierarchies of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=604 b=0 i=0 f=f5] causes described in Chapter 5. The more distal risk factors can be analysed using the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=617 b=0 i=0 f=f5] DPSEEA framework as in Figure 5.5 for transport and health. The hierarchy of causes
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=630 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in environmental and occupational health are shown in Box 9.1.

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=301 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the highest death toll, with more than 4 million environmentally caused deaths yearly,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=314 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mostly in developing countries. The infant death rate from environmental causes is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=327 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 12 times higher in low-income than in high-income countries, reflecting the human
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=340 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health gain that could be achieved by supporting healthy environments.3

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=277 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Evaluation of preventive measures
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=305 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The main emphasis in environmental and occupational epidemiology has been on
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=318 b=0 i=0 f=f5] studies of the causes of disease. Specific preventive measures to reduce exposures
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=331 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and the impact of occupational health services also need evaluating. Exposure to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=344 b=0 i=0 f=f5] hazardous environmental factors is often the result of some industrial or agricultural
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] activity that brings economic benefit to the community, and the costs of elimi-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=370 b=0 i=0 f=f5] nating these exposures can be considerable. However, environmental pollution is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=383 b=0 i=0 f=f5] often costly in itself and may damage agricultural land or industrial property as well
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=396 b=0 i=0 f=f5] as people’s health. Epidemiological analyses, health impact assessments and cost-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=409 b=0 i=0 f=f5] effectiveness analyses help public health authorities to find an acceptable balance
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=422 b=0 i=0 f=f5] between health risks and the economic costs of prevention.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=445 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Value of prevention
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=456 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Examples of combined epidemiological and economic analysis demonstrate the po-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=469 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tential value of prevention.7 In three “pollution diseases” that occurred in the 1960s
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=482 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in Japan, it was calculated (Table 9.3) that prevention would have been cheaper than
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=495 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the cure for each of the three diseases.8 Costs included compensation of the victims
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=508 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and repairing environmental damage, compared with the estimated cost of pollution
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=521 b=0 i=0 f=f5] control to prevent the diseases. The benefit-cost ratio was 100 for mercury pollution
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=534 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and the resulting Minamata disease (Table 9.3).
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=557 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Future challenges
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=568 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Environmental epidemiology will face new challenges in the coming decades with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=581 b=0 i=0 f=f5] changes in the global environment. Studies are needed of the health impacts of global
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=594 b=0 i=0 f=f5] climate change, depletion of the ozone layer, ultraviolet radiation, acid precipitation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=607 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and aspects of population dynamics.9 Some of the different potential health effects
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=620 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of climate change have not yet been documented in epidemiological studies. How-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=633 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ever, as the evidence for climate change is accumulating around the world, epidemi-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=646 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ological studies are contributing new knowledge to this field.10

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=180 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dose are therefore particularly important in environmental Emerging large-scale risks to population health are:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=192 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and occupational epidemiology. •global climate change
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=204 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Exposure has two dimensions: level and duration. For •degradation of arable land

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=301 y=238 b=0 i=0 f=f5] •losses of species and ecosystems.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=247 b=0 i=0 f=f5] level determines whether effects occur (for instance, the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=260 b=0 i=0 f=f5] “London smog epidemic” of deaths from lung and heart
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=273 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease, as shown in Figure 9.2, is one of the world’s first
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=286 b=0 i=0 f=f5] major environmental disease outbreaks that was documented in detail).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=299 b=0 i=0 f=f5] However, many environmental factors produce effects only after a long peri-

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cadmium) and hazards that have a cumulative effect (for instance, radiation or noise).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] For these hazards, the past exposure levels and the exposure duration are more im-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] portant than the current exposure level. The total exposure (or external dose) needs
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to be estimated. It is often approximated as the product of exposure duration and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] exposure level.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In epidemiological studies, all kinds of estimates of exposure and dose have been
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] used to quantify the relationship between an environmental factor and the health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=175 b=0 i=0 f=f5] status of a population. For example, in Figure 1.1, the exposure is expressed in terms
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=188 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of exposure level only (number of cigarettes smoked per day). Table 5.2 shows the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] combined effect of duration and exposure level on noise-induced hearing loss. The
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f5] external dose can also be expressed as one combined measure, as with pack-years
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=227 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for cigarette smoking and fibre-years (or particle-years) for asbestos exposure in the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=240 b=0 i=0 f=f5] workplace (Figure 9.3). Sometimes a proxy measure of exposure is used, such as the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=253 b=0 i=0 f=f5] traffic flow per hour in a particular place or the petrol consumption per year
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=266 b=0 i=0 f=f5] as indicators of air pollution exposure. These variables could also be considered as
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=279 b=0 i=0 f=f5] “pressure” indicators in the causal hierarchy (Chapter 5). Other examples would be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=292 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the use of pesticides in an area or the number of children living in houses painted
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=305 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with lead-containing paint.13

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=597 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Biological monitoring
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=625 b=0 i=0 f=f5] If the environmental factor under study is a chemical, the exposure level and dose
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=638 b=0 i=0 f=f5] can sometimes be estimated by measuring the concentration in body fluids or tissues.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=651 b=0 i=0 f=f5] This approach is called biological monitoring. Blood and urine are most commonly
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=664 b=0 i=0 f=f5] used for biological monitoring, but for certain chemicals other body tissues and fluids
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=677 b=0 i=0 f=f5] may be of particular interest: hair is useful for studies of exposure to methylmercury

[PAGE 164]
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] from fish; nail clippings have been used to study arsenic exposure; analysis of faeces
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] can give an estimate of recent exposure to metals via food (particularly lead and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cadmium); breast milk is a good material for examining exposure to organochlorine
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pesticides and other chlorinated hydrocarbons such as polychlorinated biphenyls and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dioxins; and biopsies of fat, bone, lung, liver and kidney have been used in studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of patients with suspected poisoning.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=173 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Interpreting biological data
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The interpretation of biological monitoring data requires detailed knowledge of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f5] kinetics and metabolism of chemicals, which includes data on absorption, transport,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=226 b=0 i=0 f=f5] accumulation and excretion. Because of the rapid excretion of certain chemicals, only
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=240 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the most recent exposure to them can be measured. Sometimes one body tissue or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=253 b=0 i=0 f=f5] fluid gives an indication of recent exposure and another indicates the total dose. As
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=265 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the chemical would have to be absorbed to reach the biological indicator material,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=278 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the dose measured in this way is called the absorbed dose or internal dose, as opposed
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=292 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to the external dose estimated from environmental measurements.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=304 b=0 i=0 f=f5] As an example, Figure 9.4 shows the rapid increase of blood cadmium in the first
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=317 b=0 i=0 f=f5] months after exposure started for an industrial worker, whereas there is no apparent
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=331 b=0 i=0 f=f5] change in the urine cadmium level.15 On the other hand, after long-term exposure
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=344 b=0 i=0 f=f5] urine cadmium is a very good indicator of the accumulated dose. One of the study
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] questions in this chapter invites the reader to seek further specific examples.

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=615 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Individual versus group measurements
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=643 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Variation in time
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=654 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Individual measurements of exposure vary with time. The frequency of measurements
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=667 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and the method used to estimate the exposure or dose in an epidemiological study
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=680 b=0 i=0 f=f5] therefore require careful consideration. The estimate used needs to be valid (Chapter 3)

[PAGE 165]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 152 Chapter 9
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and the measurements need to be accompanied by quality assurance procedures
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that confirm measurement accuracy.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=120 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Variation in exposure
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=131 b=0 i=0 f=f5] There is also variation in exposure or dose between individuals. Even people working
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=144 b=0 i=0 f=f5] side-by-side in a factory have different exposure levels because of different work habits
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=157 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or differences in the local distribution of a pollutant. For instance, one machine may
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=170 b=0 i=0 f=f5] leak fumes while another may not. If the exposure or dose is measured by biological
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=183 b=0 i=0 f=f5] monitoring, an additional source of variation is the difference of individual absorption
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=196 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and excretion rates for the chemical. Even people with the same external dose may
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=209 b=0 i=0 f=f5] end up with different internal doses.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=232 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Distribution issues
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=243 b=0 i=0 f=f5] One way of presenting individual variations is through distribution curves (Chapter 4).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=256 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The distributions of individual doses of environmental chemicals are often skewed
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=269 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and conform to a log-normal frequency distribution more closely than to a normal
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=282 b=0 i=0 f=f5] distribution. Ideally, the shape of the dose distribution should be tested in every
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=295 b=0 i=0 f=f5] epidemiological study where quantitative dose measurements are carried out. If the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=308 b=0 i=0 f=f5] distributions are found to be log-normal, group comparisons should be carried out
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=321 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with geometric rather than arithmetic means and standard deviations.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=334 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Another way is to use quantiles or percentiles (see Chapter 4). For instance, in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=347 b=0 i=0 f=f5] assessing whether the dose of lead in a group of children is of concern, the average
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=360 b=0 i=0 f=f5] may be of less interest than the proportion with individual doses above a certain
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=373 b=0 i=0 f=f5] threshold. If a blood lead level of 100μg/l is the threshold of concern for effects of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=386 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lead on the brain, then information about the mean level in a group (e.g. 70 μg/l)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=399 b=0 i=0 f=f5] gives no indication of how many children could be affected. It may be more infor-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=412 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mative that 25% of the children had blood lead levels above 100ìg/l.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=435 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Measures of effect
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=446 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The same considerations regarding presentation of means or percentiles are impor-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=459 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tant for measurements of effect. There is increasing concern about the effects of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=472 b=0 i=0 f=f5] environmental chemicals on children’s intellectual development and behaviour. In
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=485 b=0 i=0 f=f5] some studies, the intelligence quotient (IQ) has been measured. Differences in the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=498 b=0 i=0 f=f5] average IQ between groups are often very small and the subgroups of special concern
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=511 b=0 i=0 f=f5] consist of children with particularly low IQs. However, a small drop in mean full-scale
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=524 b=0 i=0 f=f5] IQ from 107 to 102 in the classic study by Needleman et al.16 as shown in Table 9.4,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=537 b=0 i=0 f=f5] can produce a large increase in the proportion of children with an IQ below 70 (from
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=550 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 0.6% to 2%), the threshold for mental retardation in children.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=574 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Population dose
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=602 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In epidemiological studies of cancer caused by environmental or occupational factors,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=615 b=0 i=0 f=f5] another way of presenting group dose is sometimes used. This is the dose commit-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=139 y=628 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ment or population dose, calculated as the sum of individual doses. For radiation, a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=641 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dose commitment of 50 sievert (Sv) is expected to cause one fatal cancer. Whether
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=654 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the dose commitment refers to 100 people each with a dose of 0.5Sv or 10 000 people
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=667 b=0 i=0 f=f5] each with a dose of 5mSv, the result is one case of fatal cancer. This calculation is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=680 b=0 i=0 f=f5] based on the fundamental assumptions that there is no threshold individual dose

[PAGE 166]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=294 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Environmental and occupational epidemiology 153
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] below which the cancer risk is zero and that the cancer risk increases linearly with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dose. However, the dose variation within the group may be large and the people with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the highest dose obviously have a higher individual cancer risk due to this environ-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mental exposure.
