[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=415 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Dose-effect relationships
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=442 b=0 i=0 f=f5] For many environmental factors, effects range from subtle physiological or biochem-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=456 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ical changes to severe illness or death, as explained in Chapter 2. Usually, the higher
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=469 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the dose, the more severe or intense the effect. This relationship between dose and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=54 y=481 b=0 i=0 f=f5] severity of effect in the individual is called the dose-effect relationship (Figure 9.5),
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=495 b=0 i=0 f=f5] which can be established for an individual or a group (the average dose at which each
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=508 b=0 i=0 f=f5] effect occurs). At a low carbon monoxide (CO) dose (measured as carboxy-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=520 b=0 i=0 f=f5] haemoglobin in blood) a slight headache would be the only effect, but as the dose
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=533 b=0 i=0 f=f5] increases, the effects of CO become more severe as this figure shows. Not all indi-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=547 b=0 i=0 f=f5] viduals react in the same way to a given environmental exposure, so the dose-effect
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=559 b=0 i=0 f=f5] relationship for an individual differs from the group value.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=572 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The dose-effect relationship provides valuable information for the planning of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=586 b=0 i=0 f=f5] epidemiological studies. Some effects may be easier to measure than others, and some
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=598 b=0 i=0 f=f5] may be of particular significance for public health. Measurements of changes in the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=611 b=0 i=0 f=f5] blood or urine, so-called biomarkers, may be used to study some early subtle effects
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=625 b=0 i=0 f=f5] as well as the exposure. In the case of cadmium, for instance, the level of low molec-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=637 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ular weight proteins in the urine is a good biomarker of the earliest effects on the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=651 b=0 i=0 f=f5] kidney.15 The dose-effect relationship helps the investigator choose the appropriate
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=664 b=0 i=0 f=f5] effect to study.

[PAGE 167]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 154 Chapter 9
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=87 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Figure 9.5. Dose-effect relationship
[L s=7.2 r=0.72 x=191 y=100 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Degree of
[L s=7.2 r=0.72 x=197 y=108 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Effect
[L s=7.2 r=0.72 x=187 y=140 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Death
[L s=6.8 r=0.67 x=161 y=167 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Unconsciousness
[L s=7.2 r=0.72 x=181 y=190 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Nausea,
[L s=7.2 r=0.72 x=177 y=198 b=0 i=0 f=f13] blackouts
[L s=7.2 r=0.72 x=175 y=217 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Headache,
[L s=7.2 r=0.72 x=179 y=225 b=0 i=0 f=f13] dizziness
[L s=7.2 r=0.72 x=188 y=244 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Slight
[L s=7.2 r=0.72 x=177 y=252 b=0 i=0 f=f13] headache
[L s=7.2 r=0.72 x=229 y=281 b=0 i=0 f=f13] 10 20 30 40 50 60 70 80
[L s=7.2 r=0.72 x=265 y=293 b=1 i=0 f=f12] Carboxyhaemoglobin in blood (%)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=318 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In the process of establishing safety standards, the dose-effect relationship also
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=331 b=0 i=0 f=f5] gives useful information on effects that must be prevented and on those that may be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=344 b=0 i=0 f=f5] used for screening purposes. If a safety standard is set at a level where the less severe
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] effects are prevented, the more severe effects are also likely to be prevented, because
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=370 b=0 i=0 f=f5] they occur at higher doses.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=394 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Dose–response relationships
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=422 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Response is defined in epidemiology as the proportion of an exposed group that
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=435 b=0 i=0 f=f5] develops a specific effect. Theoretically the shape of the dose–response relationship
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=448 b=0 i=0 f=f5] should look like an S or like a cumulative normal distribution. Many examples of dose–
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=461 b=0 i=0 f=f5] response relationships with this shape have been found in environmental and occu-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=474 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pational epidemiology studies. At low doses almost nobody suffers the effect, and at
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=487 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a high level almost everybody does. This reflects the variation in individual sensitivity
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=500 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to the factor studied.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=513 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The dose–response relationship can, in some cases, be approximated to a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=526 b=0 i=0 f=f5] straight-line relationship, particularly when only a narrow range of low responses is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=539 b=0 i=0 f=f5] involved. This approach has been used, for instance, in the study of lung cancer risk
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=552 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and asbestos dose (Figure 9.3) or tobacco smoking dose (Figure 1.1). The dose–
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=565 b=0 i=0 f=f5] response relationship can be modified by factors such as age. This has been found,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=578 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for example, for hearing loss caused by loud noise,17 one of the most common health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=591 b=0 i=0 f=f5] effects in the workplace, where a strong dose–response relationship can be demon-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=604 b=0 i=0 f=f5] strated (Table 5.2). Dose–response relationships can be produced for any environ-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=617 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mental factor where the exposure can be quantified. Examples from epidemiological
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=630 b=0 i=0 f=f5] studies of injuries are given in a following section.

[PAGE 168]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=294 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Environmental and occupational epidemiology 155
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Assessing risk
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=115 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Risk assessment
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=143 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Risk assessment is a term with a variety of definitions, but the intuitive interpretation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=156 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is that it is some form of assessment of the health risk of a defined policy, action or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=169 b=0 i=0 f=f5] intervention. WHO has produced numerous guidelines and methods for doing risk
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=182 b=0 i=0 f=f5] assessments, particularly in relation to chemical safety.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=206 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Health impact assessment
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=234 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Health impact assessment can be considered as a risk assessment focused on a spe-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=247 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cific population or exposure situation, while risk assessment has a more general
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=260 b=0 i=0 f=f5] application, answering such questions as: “What type of health risk can this chemical
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=273 b=0 i=0 f=f5] potentially cause in certain exposure situations?” Health impact assessment is now
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=286 b=0 i=0 f=f5] widely recommended as a method to assess the potential value of different preventive
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=299 b=0 i=0 f=f5] policies and actions.18
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=323 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Risk management
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=351 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The term risk management is applied to the planning and implementation of actions
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=364 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to reduce or eliminate health risks.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=388 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Environmental health impact assessment
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=415 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In recent years, increased attention has been given to environmental impact assess-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=428 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ment (predictive analysis) and environmental audit (analysis of the existing situation)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=441 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of industrial or agricultural development projects. These procedures have become
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=454 b=0 i=0 f=f5] legal requirements in many countries. The health component of these environmental
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=467 b=0 i=0 f=f5] assessments has been labelled environmental health impact assessment and is an
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=480 b=0 i=0 f=f5] important application of epidemiological analysis in environmental health. Such as-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=493 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sessment is also used to predict potential health problems in the use of new chemicals
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=506 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or technologies. There are several steps to assist in an overall environmental risk
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=519 b=0 i=0 f=f5] assessment:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=540 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Identify which environmental health hazard may be created by the technology
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=82 y=553 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or project under study. Are there chemical hazards? If so, which specific
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=82 y=564 b=0 i=0 f=f5] chemicals are involved? Are there biological hazards? (see Table 9.1).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=575 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Analyse the type of health effect that each hazard may cause (hazard assess-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=82 y=587 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ment). The information can be collected in systematic reviews of the scientific
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=82 y=599 b=0 i=0 f=f5] literature (in the same manner as a Cochrane review of treatments for specific
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=82 y=610 b=0 i=0 f=f5] diseases, as outlined in (Chapter 3) for each hazard or by referring to inter-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=82 y=622 b=0 i=0 f=f5] national hazard assessments, such as the Environmental Health Criteria Series
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=82 y=633 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or the Concise International Chemical Assessment Documents published by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=82 y=645 b=0 i=0 f=f5] WHO, the Monograph Series published by the International Agency for Re-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=82 y=656 b=0 i=0 f=f5] search on Cancer (IARC) and, if necessary, complementing this information
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=82 y=668 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with epidemiological studies of people exposed to the hazards in question.

[PAGE 169]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 156 Chapter 9
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=82 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Measure or estimate the actual exposure levels for the people potentially af-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=95 b=0 i=0 f=f5] fected, including the general population and the workforce. The human
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=106 b=0 i=0 f=f5] exposure assessment should take into account environmental monitoring,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=118 b=0 i=0 f=f5] biological monitoring and relevant information about history of exposure and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=129 b=0 i=0 f=f5] changes over time.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=140 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Combine the exposure data for subgroups of the exposed population with the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=152 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dose–effect and dose–response relationships for each hazard to calculate the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=164 b=0 i=0 f=f5] likely health risk in this population.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=186 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemiological studies can also be used to measure health risk directly. The risk could
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=199 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be presented as potential increase in relative risk of certain health effects or the cal-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=212 b=0 i=0 f=f5] culated increase in the number of cases of certain diseases or symptoms (Box 9.3).
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=147 y=247 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Box 9.3. Example: health impact assessment
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=263 b=0 i=0 f=f5] One example of a health impact assessment that has had major impact on environmental
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=274 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health policy is the assessment of the impact of traffic-related air pollution in Europe.19
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Based on air monitoring data, estimates of the number of people exposed and dose–
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=296 b=0 i=0 f=f5] response relationships from epidemiological studies, investigators calculated the likely
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=307 b=0 i=0 f=f5] number of deaths due to this type of air pollution (Table 9.5). It was striking that the
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=317 b=0 i=0 f=f5] number of deaths from pollution far exceeded the number of deaths from traffic crashes.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=147 y=327 b=0 i=0 f=f5] This study inspired a series of policies to control traffic-related air pollution in Europe.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=160 y=340 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A similar analysis was done for New Zealand20 with a lower ratio for air pollution deaths
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=149 y=350 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to traffic crash deaths (Table 9.5). This lower ratio is expected, as the air pollution levels
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=149 y=361 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in general are lower than in Europe and the traffic crash risks are higher.

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=27 y=407 b=0 i=0 f=f5] years) and road death tolls (1996) use burden of disease estimates in the assessments. Tools

[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=27 y=519 b=0 i=0 f=f14] New 3.7 502 399 0.8
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=27 y=526 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Zealand process. The fundamental question is: is it necessary to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=249 y=538 b=0 i=0 f=f5] take preventive action because the estimated health risk
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=249 y=549 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is too high?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=560 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • If it is decided that preventive action is needed, the next step in risk manage-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=572 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ment is to reduce exposure. This may involve changing the technology to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=584 b=0 i=0 f=f5] eliminate hazards, installing equipment to control pollution or moving pro-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=595 b=0 i=0 f=f5] posed hazardous projects.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=606 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • Finally, risk management also involves monitoring of exposure and health risks
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=618 b=0 i=0 f=f5] after the selected controls have been put into place. It is important to ensure
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=630 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that the intended protection is achieved and that any additional protective
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=641 b=0 i=0 f=f5] measures are taken without delay. In this phase of risk management, human
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=168 y=653 b=0 i=0 f=f5] exposure assessments and epidemiological surveys play an important role.

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=434 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Traffic crash injuries

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=475 b=0 i=0 f=f5] strated dose–response relationship between driving speed (dose) and frequency of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=488 b=0 i=0 f=f5] injury (response) for drivers with and without seat belts (Figure 9.7). This has served
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=501 b=0 i=0 f=f5] as valuable information for decisions regarding two different preventive approaches:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=514 b=0 i=0 f=f5] speed reduction and the use of seat-belts.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=538 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Workplace injury
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=566 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Similarly, injuries are among the most important types of ill health caused by factors
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=579 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in the workplace. The environmental factors associated with these injuries are often
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=592 b=0 i=0 f=f5] more difficult to identify and quantify than those causing, for instance, chemical
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=605 b=0 i=0 f=f5] poisoning. However, technological and management improvements over the years
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=618 b=0 i=0 f=f5] have resulted in great reductions in occupational injury rates in most high-income
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=631 b=0 i=0 f=f5] countries (see the LABORSTA database of the International Labour Organization in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=644 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Geneva).

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=408 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Violence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=436 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Violence is another public health problem that has been highlighted through epide-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=449 b=0 i=0 f=f5] miological analysis during recent years.24 In certain high-income countries, homicides
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=462 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are a major cause of death among young males, and the situation is even worse in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=475 b=0 i=0 f=f5] some low- and middle-income countries. For example, the WHO mortality database
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=488 b=0 i=0 f=f5] shows that in Brazil, homicide accounts for 40% of all deaths among 15–24 year old
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=501 b=0 i=0 f=f5] males. Firearms are frequently used to commit homicide, and this is an increasing
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=514 b=0 i=0 f=f5] trend in several countries.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=538 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Suicides
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=566 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Another important cause of death is suicide. The environmental factors causing sui-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=579 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cidal intent are primarily social or economic,24 but completed suicides are also
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=592 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dependent on access to a suicidal method, which can be seen as an environmental
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=605 b=0 i=0 f=f5] factor. Figure 9.8 shows the dramatic increase of suicides in Western Samoa after the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=618 b=0 i=0 f=f5] introduction of the extremely toxic pesticide paraquat. It was easily available in the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=631 b=0 i=0 f=f5] community because it was used on banana plantations in every village. When control
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=644 b=0 i=0 f=f5] measures were introduced, the incidence of suicide decreased. This is an example
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=657 b=0 i=0 f=f5] where simple counting of the number of incident cases can clearly show the effect
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=670 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of preventive interventions.

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=294 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Environmental and occupational epidemiology 159
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=239 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Special features of environmental and
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=261 b=0 i=0 f=f9] occupational epidemiology
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=293 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemiology is used in environmental and occupational fields to establish:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=314 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • etiology
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=326 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • natural history
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=338 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • the health status of a population
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=350 b=0 i=0 f=f5] • the value of interventions and health services.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=374 b=0 i=0 f=f5] One special feature of environmental epidemiology is its geographic base. Air, water
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=387 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and soil pollution are generally related to sources with defined geographic locations.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=400 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Mapping of environmental levels or exposures can therefore be useful tools in epi-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=413 b=0 i=0 f=f5] demiological studies.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=426 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Environmental epidemiology studies often require approximations and modeling
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=439 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for quantification of exposures, because individual exposure measurements are very
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=452 b=0 i=0 f=f5] difficult to assemble. Air quality modeling combined with geographical information
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=465 b=0 i=0 f=f5] system (GIS) analysis has been used in several air pollution health effect studies. One
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=478 b=0 i=0 f=f5] example of exposure assessment is the number of days when nitrogen dioxide con-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=491 b=0 i=0 f=f5] centrations exceed different cut-off points, and the number of people exposed in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=504 b=0 i=0 f=f5] different parts of a city based on census data.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=528 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Setting safety standards
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=556 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Dose–effect and dose–response relationships are of particular importance in envi-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=569 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ronmental and occupational epidemiology because they provide the foundation for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=582 b=0 i=0 f=f5] setting safety standards. The dose–effect relationship can be used to decide which
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=595 b=0 i=0 f=f5] effect is most important to prevent. Once a decision is made concerning an acceptable
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=608 b=0 i=0 f=f5] response level, the dose–response relationship gives the maximum dose that would
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=621 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be acceptable. WHO has developed a series of water quality guidelines,25, 26 air quality
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=634 b=0 i=0 f=f5] guidelines27 and health-based maximum occupational exposure limits28 using this
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=647 b=0 i=0 f=f5] approach. In response to the accident at the Chernobyl nuclear power station, guide-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=660 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lines were also developed for judging the radioactive contamination of food.29 For
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=673 b=0 i=0 f=f5] many environmental factors, the available data are insufficient to permit a standard

[PAGE 173]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 160 Chapter 9
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to be set with any accuracy, and informed guesses or practical experience become
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the basis of the safety standard. Further epidemiological studies are needed to provide
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] more information on dose–response relationships.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=134 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Measuring past exposure
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] One special feature of many etiological studies in occupational epidemiology is the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=174 b=0 i=0 f=f5] use of company or trade union records to identify individuals with past exposure to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=187 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a specific hazard or type of work (see Chapter 3). With the help of such records,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] retrospective cohort studies can be done. Several associations between occupational
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f5] hazards and health effects have been identified in this way.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=238 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Healthy worker effect in occupational studies
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=265 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Occupational epidemiology studies often include only men who are physically fit.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=278 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The exposed group of workers thus has a lower overall mortality rate than the corre-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=291 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sponding age group in the general population. The lower mortality has been called
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=304 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the healthy worker effect,30 which needs to be taken into account whenever the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=317 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mortality rate in a group of workers is compared with the rate in the general popula-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=330 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tion. Often the rates among healthy workers are 70%–90% of those in the general
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=343 b=0 i=0 f=f5] population. The difference arises because of the presence of unhealthy and disabled
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=356 b=0 i=0 f=f5] people in the non-working population, who usually have higher mortality rates.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=380 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Continuing challenges for epidemiologists
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=408 b=0 i=0 f=f5] This chapter has highlighted the significant contribution to the global burden of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=421 b=0 i=0 f=f5] disease from a variety of environmental and occupational hazards. Epidemiological
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=434 b=0 i=0 f=f5] studies in this field have contributed essential information to health policy and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=447 b=0 i=0 f=f5] prevention strategies currently applied in high-income countries. Epidemiologists
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=460 b=0 i=0 f=f5] now face the challenge of generating evidence of the need for similar strategies in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=473 b=0 i=0 f=f5] low- and middle-income countries.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=486 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The priorities for health policy are sometimes driven by the “body count
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=499 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mentality”, meaning that dead bodies caused by a particular health hazard have to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=512 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be shown before the hazard is taken seriously. Because many environmental and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=525 b=0 i=0 f=f5] occupational hazard situations are related to economic activities where cost-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=538 b=0 i=0 f=f5] consciousness is high, preventive action in this area is often controversial. Epidemi-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=551 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ology can provide a foundation for evidence-based health and environment policy.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=564 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Controversy surrounds environmental issues such as climate change – where
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=577 b=0 i=0 f=f5] only limited epidemiological evidence has accumulated – but action to prevent future
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=590 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health damage needs to be taken now. There are many opportunities for important
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=603 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and interesting research in occupational and environmental health, and the field is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=616 b=0 i=0 f=f5] open to inventive and original approaches.

[L s=32.0 r=3.20 x=55 y=101 b=0 i=0 f=f7] Epidemiology, health policy
[L s=32.0 r=3.20 x=55 y=135 b=0 i=0 f=f7] and planning
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=168 y=183 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Key messages
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=216 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Epidemiology informs the development, implementation, and evaluation of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=228 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health policy and planning.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=239 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Epidemiologists can be usefully involved in health policy issues.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=251 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Techniques for assessing health policy interventions need refining.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=262 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Health planning is a cycle that ideally incorporates continual assessment of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=274 b=0 i=0 f=f5] effectiveness.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=302 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Introduction
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=334 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The full value of epidemiological research is only realized when it is translated into
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=347 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health policy and the subsequent planning and implementation of disease or injury
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=360 b=0 i=0 f=f5] prevention and control programs. As we have seen, there can be a delay between the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=373 b=0 i=0 f=f5] acquisition of knowledge and its uptake by health policy-makers and planners. In this
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=386 b=0 i=0 f=f5] chapter we describe how epidemiological knowledge informs health policy and plan-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=399 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ning. The principles remain the same through a range of activities from implementing
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=412 b=0 i=0 f=f5] programs to evaluating health services. But first, some definitions:
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=436 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Health policy
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=464 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Health policy provides a framework for health-promoting actions covering the social,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=477 b=0 i=0 f=f5] economic, and environmental determinants of health. Health policy can be viewed
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=490 b=0 i=0 f=f5] as a set of decisions about strategic goals for the health sector and the means for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=503 b=0 i=0 f=f5] achieving these goals. Policy is expressed in norms, practices, regulations and laws
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=516 b=0 i=0 f=f5] affecting the health of the population which together provide shape, direction and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=529 b=0 i=0 f=f5] consistency to decisions made over time.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=553 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Health planning
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=580 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Health service planning is a process of identifying key objectives and choosing among
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=593 b=0 i=0 f=f5] alternative means of achieving them. While the process implies a rational set of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=606 b=0 i=0 f=f5] actions, the reality of planning is often quite unpredictable (see Box 10.5).
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=630 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Evaluation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=658 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Evaluation is the process of determining – as systematically and objectively as
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=671 b=0 i=0 f=f5] possible – the relevance, effectiveness, efficiency and impact of activities with

[PAGE 179]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 166 Chapter 10
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] respect to the agreed goals. The evaluation of specific interventions is well
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] advanced; it is much more difficult, and controversial, to determine and compare the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] overall performance of health systems.1
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemiologists work with other specialists to inform communities and their
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] decision-makers so that policy choices can be made in full knowledge of the likely
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] outcomes and costs.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=140 y=171 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Health policy
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=204 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Public policy is the sum of the decisions that shape society. It provides a framework
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=217 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for the development of, for example, industrial and agricultural production, corporate
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=230 b=0 i=0 f=f5] management, and health services. It determines the range of options from which
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=243 b=0 i=0 f=f5] organizations and individuals make their choices, and thus directly influences the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=256 b=0 i=0 f=f5] environment and patterns of living. Public policy is a major determinant of the health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=269 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the population.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=257 y=282 b=0 i=0 f=f5] H ealth policy is often considered in a narrow sense,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=240 y=295 b=0 i=0 f=f5] referring specifically to medical care issues and the orga-

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=52 y=495 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cess;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=240 y=502 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ognized (see Box 10.1).
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=46 y=506 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120)clarity of vision on a small set of outcome-oriented
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=52 y=515 b=0 i=0 f=f5] objectives. T he influence of epidemiology is often mediated by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=240 y=528 b=0 i=0 f=f5] public opinion. Policy-makers in many countries respond
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=240 y=541 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to public opinion rather than leading it. The growth in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=554 b=0 i=0 f=f5] media attention given to epidemiological research has increased public awareness of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=567 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the subject. Epidemiology is often an important factor influencing public policy but
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=580 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is rarely the only influence.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=593 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A major difficulty in applying epidemiology to public policy is the necessity for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=606 b=0 i=0 f=f5] making judgements about the cause of a disease and decisions on what to do when
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=619 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the evidence is incomplete. Some epidemiologists believe their role should be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=632 b=0 i=0 f=f5] limited to epidemiological research, while others consider they should be directly
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=645 b=0 i=0 f=f5] involved in the application of the results to public policy. This difference reflects
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=658 b=0 i=0 f=f5] personal, social and cultural preferences. If a health issue is controversial – and most
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=671 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are – epidemiologists who are involved in the public policy arena may be accused of

[PAGE 180]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=311 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Epidemiology, health policy and planning 167
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] one-sidedness. However, the alternative is potentially neglecting the public health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] implications of epidemiological research.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] When applying epidemiology to public policy in a given country, difficult deci-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sions have to be made about the relevance of research done elsewhere. It is usually
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] impossible – and probably unnecessary – for major studies to be repeated. However,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] local evidence is often required before local decision-makers accept the arguments
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for policy change or costly interventions. The local evidence produces a “body count,”
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=175 b=0 i=0 f=f5] which can create the impetus for preventive actions.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=199 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Framing health policy
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=226 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In framing health policy, using comparative data on mortality and disability helps to:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=249 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) weigh the effects of non-fatal health outcomes on overall population health;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=261 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) inform debates on priorities for health service delivery and planning; and,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=273 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) research and development of the health sector.3
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=295 b=0 i=0 f=f5] It is easier to both plan and evaluate programmes with a summary measure such as
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=309 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the disability-adjusted life-year (DALY) because it accounts for both mortality and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=321 b=0 i=0 f=f5] incidence. Changes in either parameter are reflected in a standard way which can be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=334 b=0 i=0 f=f5] used to track changes over time (Chapter 2).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=347 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Almost all policies affect health. Policy decisions by a wide range of agencies –
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=361 b=0 i=0 f=f5] both governmental and nongovernmental – have a significant impact on health.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=373 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Concern for health and equity is needed in all areas of public policy, as:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=396 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) agricultural policies influence the availability, price and quality of meat and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=83 y=407 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dairy products;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=419 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) advertising and fiscal policies influence the price and availability of cigarettes
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=83 y=430 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or healthy foods such as fruit; and,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=442 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) transport policies influence the extent of urban air pollution and the risk of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=83 y=453 b=0 i=0 f=f5] traffic crashes.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=475 b=0 i=0 f=f5] This broad social policy approach contrasts with much health policy, which has been
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=488 b=0 i=0 f=f5] directed predominantly towards individuals or groups and has paid little attention to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=501 b=0 i=0 f=f5] action at the population level.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=514 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The Ottawa Charter for Health Promotion (1985) – claims that health is influ-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=528 b=0 i=0 f=f5] enced by a wide range of policy decisions.4 The Charter made it clear that health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=541 b=0 i=0 f=f5] policy is not simply the responsibility of health departments. The Bangkok Charter
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=554 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for Health Promotion in a Globalized World (2005) states that health promotion
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=567 b=0 i=0 f=f5] depends on empowering all sectors and addressing the global influences on
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=580 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health.5 (Box 10.2)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=593 b=0 i=0 f=f5] One goal of a healthy public policy is health promotion (enabling people to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=606 b=0 i=0 f=f5] increase control over, and to improve, their own health). Each individual plays a role
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=619 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in achieving the goals of healthy public policy.

[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=107 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The Bangkok Charter calls for all sectors and settings to:
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=160 y=122 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120)advocate for health based on human rights and solidarity;
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=160 y=133 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120)invest in sustainable policies, actions and infrastructure to address the determinants
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=145 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of health;
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=160 y=156 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120)build capacity for policy development, leadership, health promotion practice, knowl-
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=168 b=0 i=0 f=f5] edge transfer and research, and health literacy;
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=160 y=179 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120)regulate and legislate to ensure a high level of protection from harm and enable
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=191 b=0 i=0 f=f5] equal opportunity for health and well being for all people;

[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=214 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tions and civil society to create sustainable actions.
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=147 y=228 b=0 i=0 f=f5] To do this, four key commitments are identified to make the promotion of health:
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=160 y=242 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120)central to the global development agenda

[L s=14.0 r=1.56 x=140 y=312 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Health policy in practice
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=140 y=340 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The time-scale for the application of epidemiological research to policy varies; espe-
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=140 y=353 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cially with chronic diseases, it can be measured in decades rather than years. Box 10.3
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=140 y=366 b=0 i=0 f=f5] outlines the findings of research on coronary heart disease and the resulting policy
[L s=8.0 r=0.89 x=34 y=401 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Box 10.3. Evolution of national policy:coronary heart disease
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=417 b=0 i=0 f=f5] By the early 1950s, the public health significance of coronary heart disease was recognized, although little was known about
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=428 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the risk factors. However, the link between serum cholesterol and coronary heart disease was suspected on the basis of
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=439 b=0 i=0 f=f5] animal experiments, and pathologists had shown that cholesterol was a major component of atherosclerotic lesions in
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=450 b=0 i=0 f=f5] humans. International studies began to explore the role of dietary fat in the 1950s, and major cohort studies began. By the
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=461 b=0 i=0 f=f5] end of the 1950s observational evidence was accumulating on the importance of elevated serum cholesterol, hypertension
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=472 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and smoking as the major risk factors for coronary heart disease.
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=46 y=483 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The observational studies were complemented in the 1960s by the first trials that tested the effect of attempts to alter
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=494 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dietary fat intake on rates of coronary heart disease. Many of these trials were flawed and none produced a convincing effect
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=505 b=0 i=0 f=f5] individually, although the trends were consistent. It was soon recognized that definitive trials of dietary factors and coronary
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=516 b=0 i=0 f=f5] heart disease were impracticable and attention turned to the effect of blood pressure- and cholesterol-lowering drugs.
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=46 y=526 b=0 i=0 f=f5] From a policy perspective, many official pronouncements were made, beginning in 1960 with the first statement of the
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=538 b=0 i=0 f=f5] American Heart Association. In 1985 the National Consensus Development Conference in the USA signalled an increased
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=549 b=0 i=0 f=f5] emphasis on the prevention of coronary heart disease, in particular through attempts to lower cholesterol levels in both high-
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=560 b=0 i=0 f=f5] risk people and the population at large. This programme included a national education campaign on high cholesterol levels,
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=571 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a laboratory standardization programme, and continued efforts to lower cholesterol levels through strategies aimed at both
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=582 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the population and high-risk groups.
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=46 y=593 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In 2003, the Centres for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) developed a comprehensive national public health action
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=604 b=0 i=0 f=f5] plan for heart health promotion. The aim of this plan was to chart a course by which collaborating public health agencies,
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=615 b=0 i=0 f=f5] all interested partners and the public at large, would promote national goals for preventing heart disease and stroke over the
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=626 b=0 i=0 f=f5] next two decades.
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=46 y=637 b=0 i=0 f=f5] It has taken over 50 years for comprehensive prevention and control policies for coronary heart disease and stroke to be
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=648 b=0 i=0 f=f5] introduced in the United States. However, the emphasis of public policy on coronary heart disease still lies, to a large extent,
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=34 y=659 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in attempts to influence individual behaviour, both for members of the health professions and for the public.

[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=457 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Health planning
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=490 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In this section, we illustrate the process of planning for, and evaluating, a health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=503 b=0 i=0 f=f5] intervention directed towards a specific disease. The same process should be adopted
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=516 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in broader interventions, such as the development of a national care programme for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=529 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the elderly, or a new approach to the delivery of primary health care in rural areas.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=542 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The systematic use of epidemiological principles and methods for the planning
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=555 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and evaluation of health services is an important aspect of modern epidemiology.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=568 b=0 i=0 f=f5] From assessing the value of specific treatments it is a short step to assessing the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=581 b=0 i=0 f=f5] more general performance of health services. The ultimate – though perhaps
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=594 b=0 i=0 f=f5] unrealistic – goal is to develop a transparent process for setting priorities and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=607 b=0 i=0 f=f5] allocating scarce health care resources.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=620 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Because of the limited resources available for health care in all countries, choices
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=633 b=0 i=0 f=f5] have to be made between alternative strategies for improving health (see Chapter 6).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=646 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In the poorest countries, only a few dollars per person are available for public
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=659 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health services. Consequently, a large proportion of health service costs are met by

[PAGE 183]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 170 Chapter 10
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] individuals or their families, so-called “out-of-pocket” expenses. At the other extreme,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in the United States of America, approximately 5 600 USD is spent per person on
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health services each year.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=140 y=132 b=0 i=0 f=f9] The planning cycle
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=165 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Fig. 10.1 outlines the stages involved in the health planning process and provides a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=178 b=0 i=0 f=f5] useful framework for ensuring that the information required by policy-makers is iden-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=191 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tified. The process is a cycle of the following steps:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) assessing the burden
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=225 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) identifying the causes
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=237 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) measuring the effectiveness of existing interventions
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=249 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) determining efficiency
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=261 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) implementing interventions
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=273 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) monitoring activities and measuring progress.
[L s=8.0 r=0.80 x=140 y=303 b=1 i=0 f=f11] Figure 10.1. The health planning cycle
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=277 y=316 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Assess
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=276 y=328 b=0 i=0 f=f13] burden
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=187 y=352 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Measure Identify
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=183 y=364 b=0 i=0 f=f13] and report cause
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=187 y=378 b=0 i=0 f=f13] progress
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=370 y=398 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Measure
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=363 y=410 b=0 i=0 f=f13] effectiveness
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=190 y=438 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Monitor
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=198 y=450 b=0 i=0 f=f13] and Determine
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=189 y=463 b=0 i=0 f=f13] evaluate efficiency
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=274 y=486 b=0 i=0 f=f13] Implement
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=271 y=498 b=0 i=0 f=f13] intervention
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=527 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Usually, only part of the information needed for making decisions is available
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=540 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and it always has to be critically assessed. If the information is insufficient, new data
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=553 b=0 i=0 f=f5] have to be collected for appropriate policy choices. To achieve transparency in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=566 b=0 i=0 f=f5] decision-making, all assumptions should be clearly stated. This can be applied to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=579 b=0 i=0 f=f5] other health policy issues. Even so, caution is required (Box 10.5).7
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=592 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Epidemiology is involved at all stages of planning. The cyclical nature of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=605 b=0 i=0 f=f5] process indicates the importance of monitoring and evaluation to determine whether
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=618 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the interventions have had the desired effects. The process is repetitive because each
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=631 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cycle of intervention usually has only a small impact on the burden of illness, and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=644 b=0 i=0 f=f5] repeated intervention is required.

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=397 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Understanding causes
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=425 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Once the burden of illness in the community has been measured, the second step is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=438 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to identify the major preventable causes of disease so that intervention strategies can
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=451 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be developed. It is reassuring that the major causes of death appear to have the same
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=464 b=0 i=0 f=f5] risk factors in most societies.11 Thus specific studies on causation in each society may
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=477 b=0 i=0 f=f5] not be necessary. Interventions should have the prevention of disease as their primary
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=490 b=0 i=0 f=f5] focus, but this is not always possible. The role of epidemiology in identifying causal
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=503 b=0 i=0 f=f5] factors is discussed in Chapter 5.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=527 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Measuring effectiveness of interventions
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=554 b=0 i=0 f=f5] For the third step, we need information to guide decision-making on resource allo-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=567 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cation, and the relationships between health intervention programmes and changes
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=580 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in health status. We can characterize such relationships in both qualitative and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=593 b=0 i=0 f=f5] quantitative terms. We can also describe the structure of a health service organization
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=606 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and the process of health care, in terms such as the activities of health personnel.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=619 b=0 i=0 f=f5] However – although they are important – such qualitative approaches provide us with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=632 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a limited picture how the health service is performing. We need quantitative data to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=645 b=0 i=0 f=f5] complete the picture. We can measure effectiveness by how much a specific inter-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=658 b=0 i=0 f=f5] vention reduces morbidity or mortality (Box 10.7).

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=83 y=397 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ings and the estimated social cost of a death.

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=544 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Implementing interventions
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=572 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The fifth step in the planning process involves setting targets, and making sure that
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=585 b=0 i=0 f=f5] they can be reached. We need to anticipate, and address, the problems that are likely
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=598 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to arise as a result of the decisions that have been made. For example, if we plan to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=611 b=0 i=0 f=f5] use mammography to screen for breast cancer, we need to ensure that the necessary
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=624 b=0 i=0 f=f5] equipment and personnel are available. We also need to set specific quantified targets,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=637 b=0 i=0 f=f5] such as “to reduce the incidence of advanced breast cancer from 30% to 20% over a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=650 b=0 i=0 f=f5] five-year period.” This type of target-setting is essential if we want to formally assess
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=663 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the success of an intervention. Of course, in practice, it is hard to isolate the impact
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=676 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the specific intervention from other changes in society.

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Monitoring activities and measuring progress
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=111 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The final step in the planning process is monitoring and measuring progress.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=124 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Monitoring is the continuous follow-up of activities to ensure that they are proceeding
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=137 b=0 i=0 f=f5] according to plan. We need to tailor monitoring to specific programs, the success
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=150 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of which we can measure with short-term, intermediate, and long-term criteria.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=163 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Table 10.2 provides a specific example of all the planning steps.

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=228 b=0 i=0 f=f5] about diseases and risk factors.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=239 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Knowing how to select reading material – and how to appraise its relevance
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=251 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and validity – is an important part of keeping informed about new
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=262 b=0 i=0 f=f5] developments.

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] questions, writing clear protocols, obtaining ethical approval, and publishing
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=297 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and applying the results.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=66 y=308 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) This work is made easier by the many online resources that are freely available,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=74 y=320 b=0 i=0 f=f5] including databases, analytical tools, references, and teaching guidelines.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=348 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Introduction
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=380 b=0 i=0 f=f5] If this book has been successful, you should be keen to apply what you have learnt
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=393 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to practical work in epidemiology. To do this, you should keep an open mind, and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=406 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be always on the lookout for good research questions. You need to think about how
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=419 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to apply the right study design to answer your question (Chapter 3), how to get
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=432 b=0 i=0 f=f5] approval and funding, how to make sure that it hasn’t already been done, how to do
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=445 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the research well, and how to write up, present, and publish your findings.
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=467 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Specific diseases
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=500 b=0 i=0 f=f5] One place to start is continually learning more about specific diseases or public health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=513 b=0 i=0 f=f5] problems. A basic understanding of disease epidemiology requires knowledge of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=526 b=0 i=0 f=f5] items listed in Table 11.1. Rare, emerging, or rapidly evolving diseases are often the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=539 b=0 i=0 f=f5] subject of ongoing research to establish these very characteristics.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=552 b=0 i=0 f=f5] You should seek to complement your epidemiological knowledge with what is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=565 b=0 i=0 f=f5] known about the pathology, clinical treatment, pharmacology, rehabilitation and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=578 b=0 i=0 f=f5] economic impact of a disease. More detailed knowledge of engineering or sanitation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=591 b=0 i=0 f=f5] aspects of prevention, economic impact or changing patterns may be needed for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=604 b=0 i=0 f=f5] particular areas of public health practice.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=617 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Rather than focus on a specific disease, you may choose to focus on a specific
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=630 b=0 i=0 f=f5] risk factor such as tobacco smoking or pesticide exposure. This would also involve
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=643 b=0 i=0 f=f5] studying the literature and doing research on the particular risk its route of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=656 b=0 i=0 f=f5] exposure to humans, and the mechanisms by which it affects health (Table 11.2).

[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=140 y=410 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Critical reading
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=442 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Keeping informed, even in a narrow field, is difficult because of the huge quantity of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=455 b=0 i=0 f=f5] material that is published. Finding, sorting through, and understanding relevant and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=468 b=0 i=0 f=f5] valid information is a crucial skill that can only be acquired through a lot of practice.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=481 b=0 i=0 f=f5] However, the effort spent learning to appraise papers is repaid when it comes to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=494 b=0 i=0 f=f5] designing research, as the same questions apply.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=507 b=0 i=0 f=f5] One approach is to first categorize papers into five broad types – most
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=520 b=0 i=0 f=f5] epidemiological research papers are either on the natural history of disease; its geo-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=533 b=0 i=0 f=f5] graphic distribution; its causes; treatment; or diagnostic tests. The level of evidence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=546 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that any particular study will be able to provide is linked to its design. In general,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=559 b=0 i=0 f=f5] levels of evidence are considered to progress from expert opinion, through case-series,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=572 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to cohort studies, randomized controlled trials and systematic reviews, but it is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=585 b=0 i=0 f=f5] important to consider the quality and validity of any one example in addition to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=598 b=0 i=0 f=f5] its relative position on the ladder.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=611 b=0 i=0 f=f5] When reading a paper, you may wish to consider the following questions, in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=624 b=0 i=0 f=f5] this order.

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=322 b=1 i=1 f=f18] What is the research question?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=342 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) The first step is to determine the objectives of the study – the question that
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=353 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the authors wish to address or the hypothesis that they wish to test.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=376 b=1 i=1 f=f18] If valid, are the results relevant to my work?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=396 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) If yes, keep reading.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=408 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) If no, start again with another paper.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=431 b=1 i=1 f=f18] What kind of study is this?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=451 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Cross-sectional studies address questions about the prevalence of a disease or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=462 b=0 i=0 f=f5] risk factor.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=474 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Cohort studies address questions about natural history or prognosis and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=485 b=0 i=0 f=f5] causation.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=497 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Case-control or cohort studies identify possible causal factors.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=509 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Randomized controlled trials are usually the most appropriate design for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=520 b=0 i=0 f=f5] answering questions about the efficacy of treatment or other interventions.
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=55 y=543 b=1 i=1 f=f18] What is the study population?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=563 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Who is included and who is excluded?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=575 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Are the subjects a sample of the target population?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=587 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) If not, why not?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=599 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) How have the samples been selected?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=611 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Is there evidence of random selection, as opposed to systematic, or self-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=81 y=622 b=0 i=0 f=f5] selection?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=634 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) What possible sources of bias are there in the selection strategy?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=646 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Is the sample large enough to answer the question being addressed?

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 180 Chapter 11
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=84 b=1 i=1 f=f18] For experimental studies, are the methods well described?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=104 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) How were the subjects assigned to treatment or intervention: randomly or by
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=116 b=0 i=0 f=f5] some other method?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=127 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) What control groups were included (placebo, untreated controls, both or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=139 b=0 i=0 f=f5] neither?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=150 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) How were the treatments compared?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Were measurements supported by quality assurance procedures?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=174 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Is the hypothesis clearly stated in statistical terms?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=186 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Is the statistical analysis appropriate, and is it presented in sufficient detail?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=198 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) If this is a randomized controlled trial, was the study done with an “intention-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=210 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to-treat” analysis - e.g. are all the people who entered the study accounted for?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=221 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Were the outcome or effects measured objectively?
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=244 b=1 i=1 f=f18] For observational studies, are the methods well described?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=264 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Was the data collection process adequate (including questionnaire design
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=276 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and pre-testing)?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=287 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) What techniques were used to handle non-response and/or incomplete data?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=299 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) If a cohort study, was the follow-up rate sufficiently high?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=311 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) If a case-control study, are the controls appropriate, and adequately matched?
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=334 b=1 i=1 f=f18] How are the data presented?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=354 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Are there sufficient graphs and/or tables?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=366 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Are the numbers consistent? Is the entire sample accounted for?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=378 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Are standard deviations presented with means, confidence intervals, or other
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=390 b=0 i=0 f=f5] statistics, as well as the raw data?
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=140 y=412 b=1 i=1 f=f18] Evaluating and interpreting the results
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=433 b=0 i=0 f=f5] If you have been persuaded so far that the study is valid and relevant, it is worth
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=446 b=0 i=0 f=f5] proceeding.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=459 b=0 i=0 f=f5] If it is an experimental study,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=481 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Do the authors find a difference between the treatment and control groups?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=493 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) If there is no difference, and you can rule out the possibility of a Type II error
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=505 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (see Chapter 4), then this is a negative study – which does not mean that the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=516 b=0 i=0 f=f5] results are of no consequence.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=528 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) If the authors have found a difference, are you confident that it is not due to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=539 b=0 i=0 f=f5] chance (a Type I error, see Chapter 4), or bias?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=551 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) If there is a statistically significant difference, is it enough of a difference to be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=562 b=0 i=0 f=f5] clinically significant?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=584 b=0 i=0 f=f5] If it is an observational study
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=607 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Were the findings in the control group consistent with what you would expect
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=618 b=0 i=0 f=f5] – are the averages similar to the general population?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=630 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Do the authors find a difference between exposed and control groups or cases
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=641 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and controls?
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=653 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Can Type I or Type II errors be ruled out?

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Is there a statistically significant difference between groups?

[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=304 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Planning a research project
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=337 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Students in many basic epidemiology courses are given the task of designing a study.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=350 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In some situations students are expected to do the study and analyse the data. There
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=363 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is a natural progression from critical reading to the design of studies. The same
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=376 b=0 i=0 f=f5] questions apply, and the same approach (as outlined above) can be used. Designing
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=389 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a study with help from an experienced tutor is a good way of learning the principles
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=402 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and methods of epidemiology.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=415 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The steps involved in planning a research project include:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=437 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) choosing the project
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=449 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) writing the protocol
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=461 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) getting approval
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=473 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) doing the research
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=485 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) analysing the data
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=497 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) disseminating the results.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=521 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Choosing a project
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=548 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Your supervisors should take an active role in selecting the topic and contacting any
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=561 b=0 i=0 f=f5] participants in the community. Students’ projects should not be too ambitious
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=574 b=0 i=0 f=f5] because of the inevitable shortage of time and resources. Ideally, they should be of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=587 b=0 i=0 f=f5] local significance and of relevance to some health service agency, a member of which

[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=140 y=119 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Writing the protocol
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=147 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Once you have established – through an extensive literature search – that your
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=159 b=0 i=0 f=f5] proposed research has not already been done, or is worth repeating, you need to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=172 b=0 i=0 f=f5] write the research protocol. You should consult the relevant consensus guidelines for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=186 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the type of study that you want to do, to make sure that you cover all the points
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=140 y=199 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (Table 11.3). In general, a protocol should explain:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=221 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) What you intend to do: a clear description of the problem and your approach
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=232 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to solving it.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=244 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) A justification of the importance of the research question, and how it will
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=255 b=0 i=0 f=f5] contribute to knowledge.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=267 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) A description of the population, setting, intervention or observation.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=279 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Details of the study design which should include:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=301 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) the sampling strategy,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=313 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) numbers of participants,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=325 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) variables of interest, including potential confounding variables,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=337 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) data collection methods, including pre-testing,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=349 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) quality assurance,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=361 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) data recording and data management
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=166 y=373 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) data processing and analysis.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=395 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Budget and timetable (include funding sources and all resources needed).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=407 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Roles and responsibilities of all involved.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=158 y=419 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) The ethical review committee to whom the proposal will be submitted for

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=327 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] First steps in practical epidemiology 183
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Research protocols are subject to intense scrutiny, and are the basis on which you
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are going to seek funding and ethics approval for your study. Some journals solicit
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] protocols for peer review, in the same way as research papers. Practices vary, but if
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] your protocol survives peer review and is published by the journal, the editors will
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] often undertake to review the paper that contains the main results of the study.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=160 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Doing the research
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=187 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Once you have written the protocol, it needs to be circulated for comments and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] revised as necessary. With major epidemiological studies there is often a long delay
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f5] between preparing the protocol and starting the project, caused by the processing of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=226 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a grant application. Students’ projects should be designed so that they can be done
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=239 b=0 i=0 f=f5] quickly, as the time available is often very limited.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=253 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Students’ projects should not require major resources, and the supervisor should
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=265 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be responsible for acquiring any that are necessary. The supervisor should also be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=278 b=0 i=0 f=f5] charged with submitting the project for ethical approval in good time.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=292 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Group projects require a reasonable division of labour and it is often helpful if
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=304 b=0 i=0 f=f5] one member of the group communicates with the supervisor. You need to review
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=317 b=0 i=0 f=f5] your progress together regularly, and allow time to pretest questionnaires and do a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=331 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pilot study of the sampling and data collection process.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=343 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Your project should end with a verbal presentation to the whole class (preceded,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=356 b=0 i=0 f=f5] if possible, by a rehearsal) followed by a written report, which could be circulated to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=369 b=0 i=0 f=f5] interested people. The report could be used for teaching purposes or as a basis for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=382 b=0 i=0 f=f5] further studies.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=407 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Analysing the data
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=434 b=0 i=0 f=f5] There is a wide choice of software for statistics and epidemiology, ranging from
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=447 b=0 i=0 f=f5] spreadsheets which can do limited analyses, through software made for specific
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=460 b=0 i=0 f=f5] analyses, to “all-purpose” software which can do almost all the statistical analyses
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=473 b=0 i=0 f=f5] required for epidemiological research. A catalogue of epidemiological resources which
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=486 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are available free or at minimal cost has been produced by Epidemiology Monitor
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=499 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (http://www.epimonitor.net). Rothman’s Episheet program can be downloaded
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=512 b=0 i=0 f=f5] from http://77www.oup-usa.org/epi/rothman. Public domain programs, such as
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=524 b=0 i=0 f=f5] “OpenEpi” or the CDC’s ‘Epi Info’TM are also distributed free; commercial programs
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=537 b=0 i=0 f=f5] can cost as much as several thousand US dollars.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=550 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In choosing software, you may wish to evaluate how the program handles data
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=563 b=0 i=0 f=f5] entry and missing variables, what the program’s capacity is for updating and merging
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=576 b=0 i=0 f=f5] data sets, the types of analysis that it can do, and the presence of any report-writing
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=589 b=0 i=0 f=f5] features or graphics and mapping options.
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=613 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Getting published
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=641 b=0 i=0 f=f5] You should think about where you are going to submit your work for publication
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=654 b=0 i=0 f=f5] throughout the planning stages. The best way to resolve disputes over authorship is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=667 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to avoid them in the first place, which means deciding early who in your research
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=680 b=0 i=0 f=f5] group is going to be an author – and how much of the writing each of you will do.

[PAGE 197]
[L s=9.0 r=1.00 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 184 Chapter 11
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=158 y=84 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Guidelines for journal submission often contain very useful information about
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=140 y=97 b=0 i=0 f=f5] design and reporting specifications, and many of these are impossible to correct in
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=140 y=110 b=0 i=0 f=f5] retrospect. You should consult the relevant consensus guidelines (see Table 11.3)
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=140 y=123 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for the type of study you are doing, and make sure that your protocol covers all the
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=140 y=136 b=0 i=0 f=f5] points. Your funding source may stipulate that you have to publish in an open access
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=140 y=149 b=0 i=0 f=f5] journal, and you will need to register an experimental study with an approved registry
[L s=10.0 r=1.11 x=140 y=162 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to fulfill minimum requirements for publication with major journals.
[L s=20.0 r=2.23 x=240 y=184 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Further reading

[L s=20.0 r=2.50 x=55 y=566 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Further training

[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=150 y=558 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 11.1 The following is based on the preliminary report of a study designed to assess

[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=170 y=618 b=0 i=0 f=f5] controlled trial testing whether 325mg of aspirin taken every other day
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=170 y=630 b=0 i=0 f=f5] reduces mortality from cardiovascular disease. The potentially eligible partic-
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=170 y=642 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ipants in the study were all male physicians 40 to 84 years of age residing in
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=170 y=654 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the United States at the beginning of the study in 1982. Letters of invitation,
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=170 y=666 b=0 i=0 f=f5] informed-consent forms, and baseline questionnaires were mailed to 261248
[L s=10.0 r=1.25 x=170 y=678 b=0 i=0 f=f5] such physicians identified from information on a computer tape obtained

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=327 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] First steps in practical epidemiology 187
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f5] from the American Medical Association. By 31 December 1983, 112528 had
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=95 b=0 i=0 f=f5] responded, of whom 59285 were willing to participate in the trial. A large

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=191 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 11.2 The following extract is taken from a paper on asthma mortality in New
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=203 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Zealand, published in the Lancet (Wilson JD, Sutherland DC, Thomas AC.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=215 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Has the change to beta-agonists combined with oral theophylline increased
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=227 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cases of fatal asthma? Lancet 1981;1:1235-37.)
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=248 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Abstract
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=279 b=0 i=0 f=f5] An apparent increase in young people dying suddenly from acute asthma has been
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=292 b=0 i=0 f=f5] noted in the past 2 years in Auckland. 22 fatal cases were reviewed. Prescribing habits
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=304 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for asthma therapy have been changing in New Zealand, with a considerable increase
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=318 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in the use of oral theophylline drugs, particularly sustained-release preparations,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=331 b=0 i=0 f=f5] which in many patients have replaced inhaled steroids and cromoglycate. It is sug-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=343 b=0 i=0 f=f5] gested that there may be an additive toxicity between theophylline and inhaled
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=356 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ß -agonists at high doses which produces cardiac arrest.
[L s=7.0 r=0.70 x=60 y=361 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 2
[L s=14.0 r=1.40 x=55 y=379 b=0 i=0 f=f10] Methods
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=410 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Details of deaths from asthma were obtained from the coroner’s pathologist, the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=423 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Auckland Asthma Society, general practitioners, and from the intensive and critical
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=436 b=0 i=0 f=f5] care wards of Auckland Hospital. The doctors and relatives of the patients were con-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=449 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tacted and descriptions of mode of death and the pattern of drug administration were
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=462 b=0 i=0 f=f5] obtained. Statistical information on fatal asthma cases in New Zealand in the years
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=475 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1974–78 was obtained from the New Zealand Department of Health. Necropsies had
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=488 b=0 i=0 f=f5] been performed on the 8 patients referred to the coroner.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=73 y=501 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Taking into consideration the methods used, would you agree with the sugges-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=55 y=514 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tion that a toxic drug interaction was leading to an increased risk of death?
