[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=354 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Answers to study questions 189
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Chapter 1
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=117 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1.1 The fact that there were over 40 times more cholera cases in one district than
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=129 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in the other does not reflect the risk of catching cholera in the two districts.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=141 b=0 i=0 f=f5] It is not appropriate to compare the number of deaths in the two groups since
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=153 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the population supplied by the Southwark Company was over eight times
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=165 b=0 i=0 f=f5] larger than the population supplied by the Lambeth Company. Death rates
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=177 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (number of deaths divided by the population supplied) must be compared.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=189 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In fact the death rate in the population supplied by the Southwark Company
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] was over five times greater than that in the Lambeth district.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=212 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1.2 The best evidence would come from intervention studies. The 1854 epidemic
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=224 b=0 i=0 f=f5] was controlled in a most dramatic manner when the handle of a water pump
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=236 b=0 i=0 f=f5] was removed. The epidemic died away rapidly, although the evidence sug-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=248 b=0 i=0 f=f5] gests (and Snow knew) that the epidemic was already waning before this act.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=260 b=0 i=0 f=f5] More convincing was the reduction in cholera rates in the population supplied
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=272 b=0 i=0 f=f5] by the Lambeth Company in the period 1849–54 (before the epidemic) after
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=284 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the Company had begun extracting water from a less contaminated part of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=296 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the River Thames.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=308 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1.3 Doctors make a good study group because they comprise a well-defined oc-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=320 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cupational group with similar socioeconomic status, and are relatively easy
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=331 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to follow up. They are also likely to be interested in health matters and co-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=343 b=0 i=0 f=f5] operative in this type of study.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=355 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1.4 It can be concluded that lung cancer death rates increase dramatically with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=367 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the number of cigarettes smoked. From the data alone it is not possible to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=379 b=0 i=0 f=f5] conclude that smoking causes lung cancer; some other factor associated with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=391 b=0 i=0 f=f5] smoking might be causing the disease. However, in 1964, on the basis of this
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=403 b=0 i=0 f=f5] study and many others, the United States Surgeon General concluded that
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=415 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lung cancer was caused by cigarette smoking.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=427 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1.5 The distribution of the population is the first factor to consider. The con-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=439 b=0 i=0 f=f5] centration of cases in one area is interesting only if the population is spread
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=450 b=0 i=0 f=f5] throughout that area. Secondly, it needs to be known whether the search for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=462 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cases has been as intensive in the areas without cases as in the area with
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=474 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cases. During the Minamata disease outbreak, an intensive search was made
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=486 b=0 i=0 f=f5] throughout the whole region and it was found that several large population
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=498 b=0 i=0 f=f5] centres had no cases.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=510 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1.6 The reported occurrence of rheumatic fever has declined dramatically in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=522 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Denmark since the early 1900s. It could be a real decline although it would
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=534 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be important to try to rule out the influence of changes in diagnostic fashion
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=546 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and reporting practices. Since effective medical treatment for rheumatic fever
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=557 b=0 i=0 f=f5] became available only in the 1940s, most of the decline has been due to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=569 b=0 i=0 f=f5] socioeconomic improvements, e.g. in housing and nutrition. It is also pos-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=581 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sible that the responsible organism has become less virulent.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=593 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1.7 Men who do not smoke and are not exposed to asbestos dust have the lowest
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=605 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lung cancer rates, followed in increasing order by men exposed to asbestos
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=617 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dust alone, men who smoke but are not exposed to asbestos dust, and finally
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=629 b=0 i=0 f=f5] men who both smoke and are exposed to asbestos dust. This is an example
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=641 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of interaction in which two factors work together to produce a very high rate
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=653 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of disease. From a public health perspective it is important to ensure that
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=665 b=0 i=0 f=f5] people exposed to asbestos dust do not smoke, and, of course, to reduce
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=676 b=0 i=0 f=f5] exposure to the dust.

[PAGE 203]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 190 Annex
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=140 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Chapter 2
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=117 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 2.1 The three measures are prevalence, incidence and cumulative incidence.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=129 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Prevalence is the proportion of the population affected by a disease or con-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=141 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dition at a given point in time and is approximately equal to the incidence
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=153 b=0 i=0 f=f5] multiplied by the duration of disease. Incidence measures the rate at which
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=165 b=0 i=0 f=f5] new events occur in a population; it can take into account variable time
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=177 b=0 i=0 f=f5] periods during which individuals are disease-free. Cumulative incidence mea-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=189 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sures the denominator (i.e. the population at risk) at only one point in time
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (usually at the beginning of a study) and thus measures the risk of individuals
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f5] contracting a disease during a specified period.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=225 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 2.2 Prevalence is a useful measure of the frequency of non-insulin-dependent
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=237 b=0 i=0 f=f5] diabetes because diabetes has a relatively low incidence and because a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=249 b=0 i=0 f=f5] very large population and a long study period would be required in order to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=261 b=0 i=0 f=f5] find sufficient new cases to measure incidence. The variation shown in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=273 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Table 2.3 could reflect differences in measurement. The adequacy of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] methods used in the various surveys would need to be assessed; survey re-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=297 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sponse rates and laboratory methods would have to be looked at, among
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=309 b=0 i=0 f=f5] other things. It should be noted, however, that standard criteria are being
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=321 b=0 i=0 f=f5] applied on the basis of blood glucose levels after a standard glucose load. It
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=333 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is likely that much of the variation in diabetes prevalence is real and due, at
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=345 b=0 i=0 f=f5] least in part, to variations in diet, exercise and other elements of lifestyle.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 2.3 The population attributable risk or attributable fraction (population) is cal-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=369 b=0 i=0 f=f5] culated as:
[L s=9.6 r=0.96 x=293 y=390 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 30.2–17.7
[L s=9.6 r=0.96 x=335 y=397 b=0 i=0 f=f5] = 0.414
[L s=9.6 r=0.96 x=301 y=404 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 30.2
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=426 b=0 i=0 f=f5] corresponding to 41.4%.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=438 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 2.4 Risk difference and risk ratio.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=450 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 2.5 Although the relative risk is only about 1.5, the population attributable risk
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=462 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is about 20% (i.e. about 20% of the cases of lung cancer in a typical popu-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=474 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lation of a developed country can be attributed to passive smoking). This is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=486 b=0 i=0 f=f5] because up to half the population is exposed to passive smoking.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=498 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 2.6 Age standardization ensures that differences in death rates are not due simply
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=510 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to differences in age distribution in the populations. Standardizing crude rates
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=522 b=0 i=0 f=f5] takes the age distribution out of the picture and therefore allows a comparison
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=534 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of populations with different age structures by using a population with a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=546 b=0 i=0 f=f5] standard age distribution
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=558 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 2.7 Either rate can be used and even the number of cancer deaths. It all depends
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=570 b=0 i=0 f=f5] on how the information will be interpreted. The number of cases tells you
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=582 b=0 i=0 f=f5] which part of the country would have the largest number of cancer cases
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=594 b=0 i=0 f=f5] needing treatment. The crude rate tells you where the number of case per
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=606 b=0 i=0 f=f5] capita is the highest, but a high crude rate may just indicate that there are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=618 b=0 i=0 f=f5] many elderly people in that area. However, the age standardized rates tells
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=630 b=0 i=0 f=f5] us where the risk of cancer is highest, which would be the first step in de-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=642 b=0 i=0 f=f5] signing epidemiological studies to identify preventable risk factors.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=654 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 2.8 They reflect the fact that the average life expectancy in Cote d’Ivoire is low
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=666 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and there are not many people in the older age groups (and cancer risk in-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=678 b=0 i=0 f=f5] creases with age).

[PAGE 204]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=354 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Answers to study questions 191
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 2.9 Without having the age standardized rates for both countries, comparisons
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=95 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are not possible. The higher crude rates in Japan could have occurred because
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=107 b=0 i=0 f=f5] it has the highest life expectancy in the world and many more older people
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=119 b=0 i=0 f=f5] than in Cote d’Ivoire - ie they have populations with radically different age
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=131 b=0 i=0 f=f5] distributions.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=143 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In fact, Japan has a an age standardized cancer rate of 119.2 per 100,000
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=155 b=0 i=0 f=f5] compared with Cote d’Ivoire (which has a rate of 160.2 per 100,000 - see
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=167 b=0 i=0 f=f5] above). With age standardization, Cote d’Ivoire’s rate increases and Japanese
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=179 b=0 i=0 f=f5] rates decrease.

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 192 Annex
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=140 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Chapter 3
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=117 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 3.1 The main epidemiological study designs are the cross-sectional survey, the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=129 b=0 i=0 f=f5] case-control study, the cohort study and the randomized controlled trial.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=141 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Their relative strengths and weaknesses are summarized in the text and in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=153 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Tables 3.3 and 3.4.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=165 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 3.2 The case-control study would start with cases of bowel cancer, preferably
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=177 b=0 i=0 f=f5] newly diagnosed ones, and a group of controls (without the disease) from
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=189 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the same source population (to avoid selection bias). The cases and controls
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] would be asked about their usual diet in the past. Measurement bias could
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be a problem. It is difficult to remember past diet with great accuracy, and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=225 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the development of the disease might influence recall. The analysis would
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=237 b=0 i=0 f=f5] compare the content of the diet in the cases and controls, controlling for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=249 b=0 i=0 f=f5] possible confounding variables.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=261 b=0 i=0 f=f5] In a cohort study, detailed data on diet are collected in a large group of people
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=273 b=0 i=0 f=f5] free of bowel disease; the cohort is followed up for several years and all new
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cases of bowel cancer are identified. The risk of disease is then related to the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=297 b=0 i=0 f=f5] fat content of the diet at the beginning and during the study. This study
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=309 b=0 i=0 f=f5] design presents many logistic problems but systematic bias is less of a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=321 b=0 i=0 f=f5] problem.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=333 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 3.3 Random error is the variation of an observed value from the true population
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=345 b=0 i=0 f=f5] value due to chance alone. It can be reduced by increasing the size of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] study sample and improving the reliability of the measurement method.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=369 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 3.4 Systematic error occurs when there is a tendency to produce results that differ
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=381 b=0 i=0 f=f5] systematically from the true values. The main sources of systematic error are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=393 b=0 i=0 f=f5] selection bias and measurement bias.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=405 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Selection bias occurs when the people who take part in a study are system-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=417 b=0 i=0 f=f5] atically different from those who do not. The possibility of selection bias can
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=429 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be reduced by a clear and explicit definition of the criteria for entry into the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=441 b=0 i=0 f=f5] study, a knowledge of the natural history and management of the disease,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=453 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and a high response rate.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=465 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Measurement bias occurs when there is a systematic error in measurement
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=477 b=0 i=0 f=f5] or classification of the participants in a study. It can be reduced by good study
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=489 b=0 i=0 f=f5] design, involving, for example, standard criteria for the disease, detailed at-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=501 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tention to the quality control of measurement methods, and the collection
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=513 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of data without knowledge of the disease status of the participant.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=525 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 3.5 Relative risk (RR) is used in prospective studies (e.g. cohort) while Odds
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=537 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Ratio (OR) is calculated in a case-control (retrospective) study. In a case-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=549 b=0 i=0 f=f5] control study, there are those who have the disease and those who do not
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=561 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (including those who are exposed and those who are not). The ratio of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=573 b=0 i=0 f=f5] probability of occurrence of an event to that of non occurrence is the odds
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=585 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of that event, and by computing the ratio of the odds of the event in exposed
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=597 b=0 i=0 f=f5] versus non exposed (the odds ratio, which is the ratio of two odds), it is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=609 b=0 i=0 f=f5] estimated how much more likely is the event in exposed compared with non
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=621 b=0 i=0 f=f5] exposed. This odds ratio is indeed an approximation to the relative risk, and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=633 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is particularly useful if the event is rare. Further details on how the odds ratio
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=645 b=0 i=0 f=f5] approximates and differs from the relative risk are beyond a course in basic
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=657 b=0 i=0 f=f5] epidemiology.

[PAGE 206]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=354 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Answers to study questions 193
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A relative risk is almost never calculated in case-control studies. In calculating
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=95 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a RR, one is comparing the incidence of those exposed and incidence of those
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=107 b=0 i=0 f=f5] not exposed (probability of occurrence of a disease in the exposed and in the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=119 b=0 i=0 f=f5] non-exposed).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=131 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 3.6 See answer to question 3.5. In the case of a rare disease (for example, most
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=143 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cancers), RR and OR are very similar. This is because the odds ratio formula
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=155 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is: (sick exposed x non-sick not exposed) / (sick not exposed x non-sick
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=167 b=0 i=0 f=f5] exposed).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=179 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 3.7 Maternal age is a confounder: it is correlated with birth order and is a risk
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=191 b=0 i=0 f=f5] factor even if birth order is low. In another sample where all mothers are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=203 b=0 i=0 f=f5] below 30 years, no association with birth order would be found.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=144 y=233 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Birth Order Down’s Syndrome
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=256 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Maternal age
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=286 b=0 i=0 f=f5] One way to avoid confounding would be to stratify by maternal age.

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=117 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 4.1 The sum of the n = 10 observations is 679.1 kg; the mean is 67.91; the median
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=129 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is 67.3—note that there are two observations with values of 67.3 that are in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=141 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the middle of the group after they have been put into order; the variance
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=153 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is 104.03 kg2; the standard deviation is 10.20 kg; and the standard error is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=165 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 3.23 kg.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=177 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 4.2 The median is often used to report personal income for a group since it is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=189 b=0 i=0 f=f5] much less affected by a few very high income levels, which can sometimes
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] make the average income for the group much higher than the levels for most
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the members.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=225 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 4.3 There are two major differences among these models. First, while the inde-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=237 b=0 i=0 f=f5] pendent variables can be the same for all three, the dependent variables are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=249 b=0 i=0 f=f5] quite different, with the dependent variable for linear regression being a con-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=261 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tinuous variable, for logistic regression being a representation for a dichoto-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=273 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mous variable such as the presence or absence of some characteristic, and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for survival models being the measure of a time interval from some specified
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=297 b=0 i=0 f=f5] point until a pre-specified event occurs. Coefficients for linear regression rep-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=309 b=0 i=0 f=f5] resent either differences between means or slopes, for logistic regression
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=321 b=0 i=0 f=f5] represent odds ratios and for survival models represent hazard rate ratios.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=333 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 4.4 The narrower the better. This is true since the confidence interval concept is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=345 b=0 i=0 f=f5] such that, for example, the sample mean, which is an estimate of the mean
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] for the population from which the sample was taken, is in the middle of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=369 b=0 i=0 f=f5] confidence interval. Further, one would expect 95% of such intervals to con-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=381 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tain the true value of the population mean and the shorter the interval the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=393 b=0 i=0 f=f5] closer the sample mean is likely to be to the population mean.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=405 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 4.5 In general, tables presenting data or results should “stand alone” in a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=417 b=0 i=0 f=f5] manuscript or report. This means that the reader should be able to interpret
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=429 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the data presented without reference to the text or to other documents. The
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=441 b=0 i=0 f=f5] table title is essential for this purpose. Data tables typically are made up of a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=453 b=0 i=0 f=f5] set of cells and the table title should state “what, how classified, where and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=465 b=0 i=0 f=f5] when” in reference to the information in the cells. An example is “Number
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=477 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and percent of participants, classified by age, race and gender, CARDIA Study,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=489 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 2006.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=501 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 4.6 For this situation, b = mean - mean = 5.0 kg, adjusted for the other
[L s=7.0 r=0.70 x=242 y=506 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1 males females
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=515 b=0 i=0 f=f5] independent variables in the model.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=527 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 4.7 For this situation, b = 0.5 represents the slope for the relationship between
[L s=7.0 r=0.70 x=243 y=531 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 1
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=540 b=0 i=0 f=f5] age and body weight. It is interpreted as the increment in body weight per
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=552 b=0 i=0 f=f5] one year increment in age, which, in this case, means that body weight in-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=564 b=0 i=0 f=f5] creases 0.5 kg per year of increase in age.

[PAGE 208]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=354 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Answers to study questions 195
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Chapter 5
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=117 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 5.1 The process of determining whether an observed association is likely to be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=129 b=0 i=0 f=f5] causal.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=141 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 5.2 This means that some causal factors lead to exposures to other factors that
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=153 b=0 i=0 f=f5] are the direct cause of the disease. For instance, low income is associated
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=165 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with a low intake of fruit and vegetables in the United Kingdom (Figure 5.9).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=177 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Low intake of fruit and vegetables is in turn associated with a higher diastolic
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=189 b=0 i=0 f=f5] blood pressure. Income determines diet, which determines a health outcome;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a hierarchy of causes.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 5.3 Attributable fractions are as follows: Smoking among asbestos workers =
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=225 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (602–58)/602 = 0.904, or 90%; smoking among non-asbestos workers =
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=237 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (123-11)/123 = 0.910 or 91%; asbestos exposure among smokers = (602–123)/
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=249 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 602 = 0.796, or 80%; asbestos exposure among non-smokers = (58-11)/56
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=261 b=0 i=0 f=f5] = 0.810 or 81%. These fractions add up to more than 100% because they are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=273 b=0 i=0 f=f5] each dependant on the other. Thus, eliminating smoking among asbestos
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] workers reduces lung cancer rate by 90%, and then eliminating asbestos ex-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=297 b=0 i=0 f=f5] posure reduces the remaining risk by 80%. The total reduction of risk is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=309 b=0 i=0 f=f5] therefore 90% plus 80% x 0.1, which, when added up, gives a 98% reduction.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=321 b=0 i=0 f=f5] When deciding on prevention programs, a judgement needs to be made of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=333 b=0 i=0 f=f5] how large a proportion of each hazard exposure can be eliminated. Techno-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=345 b=0 i=0 f=f5] logical change can eliminate all asbestos exposure, while smoking cessation
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] programs may only eliminate a fraction of the tobacco exposure. In order to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=369 b=0 i=0 f=f5] calculate population attributable risks it is necessary also to know what pro-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=381 b=0 i=0 f=f5] portion of the population is smoking and what proportion is exposed to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=393 b=0 i=0 f=f5] asbestos at work.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=405 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 5.4 The criteria include: the temporal nature of the relation, plausibility, consis-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=417 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tency, the strength of the association, the dose-response relationship, re-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=429 b=0 i=0 f=f5] versibility, and the study design. Of these criteria, only temporality is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=441 b=0 i=0 f=f5] essential; ultimately, judgment is required.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=453 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 5.5 On the basis of this evidence alone one could not be certain that the asso-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=465 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ciation was causal; a policy of withdrawing the drug could not, therefore, be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=477 b=0 i=0 f=f5] recommended. The effects of bias (measurement, selection) and confound-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=489 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ing in the study and the role of chance would need to be assessed. If bias
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=501 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and chance are unlikely to be the explanation, then the causal criteria can be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=513 b=0 i=0 f=f5] applied. In fact, when all the evidence was considered in such a study in New
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=525 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Zealand, the investigators concluded that the association was likely to be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=537 b=0 i=0 f=f5] causal.28
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=549 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 5.6 A temporal relationship is most important. Did the patients consume the oil
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=561 b=0 i=0 f=f5] before or after they fell ill? Prior evidence of the potential toxic effects of this
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=573 b=0 i=0 f=f5] chemical is also crucial. If there is no information on the chemical in the oil
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=585 b=0 i=0 f=f5] that is associated with the disease, it is impossible to assess plausibility or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=597 b=0 i=0 f=f5] consistency. Therefore strength and dose-response relationship based on in-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=609 b=0 i=0 f=f5] formation on oil consumption could be the next matters for study. As it is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=621 b=0 i=0 f=f5] urgent priority to find the likely cause, the most suitable approach would be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=633 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to conduct a case-control study, together with chemical analysis of the oil
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=645 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and of biological monitoring samples. It would be prudent to intervene as
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=657 b=0 i=0 f=f5] soon as a temporal relationship has been clearly established and the strength
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=669 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of association appears great, particularly if there is no other likely cause.

[PAGE 209]
[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 196 Annex
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 5.7 It is acceptable for acute effects that occur within hours or days of the ex-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=95 b=0 i=0 f=f5] posure. It is using the exposed group as its own control. On hot days the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=107 b=0 i=0 f=f5] population is exposed and on cooler days the same population serves as a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=119 b=0 i=0 f=f5] control. If daily data are used, it is considered that the population size or
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=131 b=0 i=0 f=f5] character does not change during the study period and confounding should
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=143 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be limited.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=155 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 5.8 Meta-analysis combines data from more than one study in order to achieve
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=167 b=0 i=0 f=f5] more stable and precise conclusions concerning causal associations. To use
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=179 b=0 i=0 f=f5] this method each study needs to have used the same exposure and health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=191 b=0 i=0 f=f5] outcome variables and the basic population characteristics (age, sex, etc.)
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=203 b=0 i=0 f=f5] should also be the same in each study.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=215 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 5.9 The risk of ischaemic heart disease is about twice as high in the lower fruit
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=227 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and vegetable consumption quintile as in the upper quintile (Figure 5.8). The
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=239 b=0 i=0 f=f5] fruit and vegetable intake levels in Figure 5.9 for the highest and lowest quin-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=251 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tiles are approximately 300 and 150 grams per day, respectively. Combining
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=263 b=0 i=0 f=f5] these estimates indicates that lower income groups may have four times
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=275 b=0 i=0 f=f5] higher ischaemic heart disease risk in relation to fruit and vegetable con-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=287 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sumption than the higher income groups. Clearly, public health actions and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=299 b=0 i=0 f=f5] policies need to find ways to make fruit and vegetable intake more common
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=311 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in low income households. Figure 5.9 indicates that at least in the United
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=323 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Kingdom food prices may be a key factor. Just like taxes are applied to un-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=335 b=0 i=0 f=f5] healthy products, such as tobacco, it may be worth providing subsidies for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=347 b=0 i=0 f=f5] fruit and vegetable production and distribution. School lunches can also be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=359 b=0 i=0 f=f5] a target for improved diets in this respect.

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=354 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Answers to study questions 197
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=55 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Chapter 6
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=117 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 6.1 The four levels of prevention are: primordial, primary, secondary and tertiary.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=129 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A comprehensive programme for the prevention of stroke would include
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=141 b=0 i=0 f=f5] activities at each of these levels.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=153 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Primordial prevention would involve stopping the rise of population levels of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=165 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the major risk factors for common chronic diseases including stroke.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=177 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Primary prevention includes both population prevention through public
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=189 b=0 i=0 f=f5] health legislation and environmental changes addressed at the whole popu-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lation, as well as a “high risk” strategy targeting treatment at individuals at
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f5] high overall risk of an acute stroke event.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=225 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Secondary prevention programmes would involve the early treatment and
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=237 b=0 i=0 f=f5] rehabilitation. If people who have already had a heart attack or stroke are
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=249 b=0 i=0 f=f5] included in the high risk prevention strategy, in effect, this merges the high
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=261 b=0 i=0 f=f5] risk strategy with secondary prevention.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=273 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Tertiary prevention involves rehabilitation of patients suffering from the long-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] term effects or sequelae of stroke.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=297 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 6.2 This cannot be answered in general terms. Each potential prevention pro-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=309 b=0 i=0 f=f5] gramme needs to be assessed in context. Each programme must be balanced
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=321 b=0 i=0 f=f5] by an appropriate mix of population and high-risk activities based on a num-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=333 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ber of factors including the levels of diabetes and obesity, the major risk
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=345 b=0 i=0 f=f5] factors, the affordability of clinical care, and issues of equity. The challenge
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] is not to choose between one or the other approach, but to move investment
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=369 b=0 i=0 f=f5] towards populations approaches while improving the quality of the high-risk
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=381 b=0 i=0 f=f5] approaches that are in operation.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=393 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 6.3 For a disease to be suitable for screening it must be serious, the natural history
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=405 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the disease must be understood, there should be a long period between
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=417 b=0 i=0 f=f5] development of the first signs and appearance of overt disease, an effective
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=429 b=0 i=0 f=f5] treatment must be available and, usually, the prevalence of the disease must
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=441 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be high.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=453 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 6.4 All study designs have been used to evaluate screening programmes. Ran-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=465 b=0 i=0 f=f5] domized controlled trials are ideal, but cross-sectional, cohort and case-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=477 b=0 i=0 f=f5] control studies are also used.

[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=140 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Chapter 7
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=117 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 7.1 The proportion of deaths due to infectious diseases has declined in Brazil
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=129 b=0 i=0 f=f5] since 1950 and chronic diseases have become more important. Demographic
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=141 b=0 i=0 f=f5] change, with an increased proportion of elderly people, is one explanation.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=153 b=0 i=0 f=f5] It would be helpful to have age-specific mortality data for individual diseases
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=165 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to allow further examination of the trends. Two general explanations for a
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=177 b=0 i=0 f=f5] decrease in age-specific infectious disease mortality have been advanced.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=189 b=0 i=0 f=f5] First, there has been a general reduction in host susceptibility through im-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] proved nutrition and sanitation. This is likely to be the most important factor,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f5] particularly in respect of the early improvement. Secondly, specific medical
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=225 b=0 i=0 f=f5] interventions may have played a part, particularly since the 1950s.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=237 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 7.2 A record of weekly (or daily) cases of measles found by clinics and health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=249 b=0 i=0 f=f5] practitioners in the district should be kept. The “normal” background level
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=261 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (perhaps two cases or fewer per week) and a threshold level for an incipient
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=273 b=0 i=0 f=f5] epidemic (perhaps twice or three times the background level) should be es-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tablished. When the threshold is exceeded, preventive action should be
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=297 b=0 i=0 f=f5] taken.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=309 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 7.3 The chain of infection for foodborne salmonella goes from faecal material
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=321 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (either from humans or animals, particularly chickens) to water or food which,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=333 b=0 i=0 f=f5] when consumed, leads to infection. Alternatively, it goes from faecal material
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=345 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to hands and then to food (during food preparation), which again leads to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] infection.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=369 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 7.4 The revised International Health Regulations (2005) establishes a single code
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=381 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of procedures and practices for routine public health measures.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=393 b=0 i=0 f=f5] The IHR (2005) does not include an enforcement mechanism for the coun-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=405 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tries which fail to comply with its provisions.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=417 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Countries will need to meet the human and financial requirements for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=439 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) developing, strengthening and maintaining the necessary public health
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=182 y=450 b=0 i=0 f=f5] capacities, and mobilizing the resources necessary for that purpose;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=462 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) adopting the necessary legal and administrative provisions
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=474 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) designating a national IHR Focal Point
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=486 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) assessment and notification of events occurring within their territory that
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=182 y=498 b=0 i=0 f=f5] may constitute a public health emergency of international concern, and;
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=509 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) implementing measures at certain international airports, ports and ground
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=182 y=520 b=0 i=0 f=f5] crossings, including routine inspection and control activities.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=544 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 7.5 The four levels of prevention are: primordial, primary, secondary and tertiary.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=556 b=0 i=0 f=f5] A comprehensive programme for the prevention of tuberculosis would in-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=568 b=0 i=0 f=f5] clude activities at each of these levels.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=580 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Primordial prevention can involve preventing the entry of infectious cases
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=592 b=0 i=0 f=f5] into a healthy population. People from endemic areas can be required to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=604 b=0 i=0 f=f5] provide evidence that they are not infected before entering non-endemic
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=616 b=0 i=0 f=f5] areas. In addition, the factors that increase the risk of tuberculosis, such as
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=628 b=0 i=0 f=f5] overcrowding, poverty and poor nutrition can be addressed.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=640 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Primary prevention includes immunization and case-finding, to avoid spread
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=652 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the disease.

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=354 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] Answers to study questions 199
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Secondary prevention programmes involve the early and effective treatment
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=95 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of infected people.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=107 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Tertiary prevention involves rehabilitation of patients suffering from the long-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=119 b=0 i=0 f=f5] term effects or sequelae of tuberculosis and its treatment.

[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=140 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Chapter 8
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=117 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 8.1 The term is strictly a contradiction in that epidemiology deals with popula-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=129 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tions whereas clinical medicine deals with individual patients. However, it is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=141 b=0 i=0 f=f5] appropriate because clinical epidemiology studies populations of patients.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=153 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 8.2 The limitation of this definition is that there are no biological grounds for
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=165 b=0 i=0 f=f5] using an arbitrary cut-off point as the basis for distinguishing normal from
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=177 b=0 i=0 f=f5] abnormal. For many diseases the risk increases with increasing levels of risk
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=189 b=0 i=0 f=f5] factors and much of the burden of disease falls on people in the normal range.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 8.3 The sensitivity of the new test=8/10×100=80%; its specificity=
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 9000/10000×100=90%. The new test appears good; a decision on whether
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=225 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to use it in the general population requires information on its positive pre-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=237 b=0 i=0 f=f5] dictive value, which in this case is 8/1008=0.008. This very low value is related
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=249 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to the low prevalence of the disease. For this reason, it would not be appro-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=261 b=0 i=0 f=f5] priate to recommend general use of the test.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=273 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 8.4 The positive predictive value of a screening test is the proportion of the people
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] with positive results who actually have the disease. The major determinant
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=297 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the positive predictive value is the prevalence of the pre-clinical disease in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=309 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the screened population. If the population is at low risk for the disease, most
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=321 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of the positive results will be false. Predictive value also depends on the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=333 b=0 i=0 f=f5] sensitivity and specificity of the test.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=345 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 8.5 Some of the potential problems with this metaanalysis include the following:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=367 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Aspirin dose, duration of treatment, and lengths of follow up were unlikely
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=182 y=378 b=0 i=0 f=f5] to be uniform in the 6 selected studies.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=390 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Even with pooling six large trials, individual outcome events were infre-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=182 y=402 b=0 i=0 f=f5] quent because of the low risk of the populations studied, thus reducing
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=182 y=413 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the power of the study to detect differences.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=425 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Only analysis of data from participants from all the available trials would
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=182 y=436 b=0 i=0 f=f5] have allowed an examination of aspirin benefit in particular subgroups
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=182 y=448 b=0 i=0 f=f5] who may have benefited.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=459 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Meta-analysis is retrospective research, subject to the methodological de-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=182 y=471 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ficiencies of each included study.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=494 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 8.6 On the basis of this study, it could be concluded that low-dose aspirin is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=506 b=0 i=0 f=f5] associated with a reduction on cardiovascular events in both men and women
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=518 b=0 i=0 f=f5] but also associated with a significant risk of major bleeding. Recommenda-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=530 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tions would include the need to explain to an individual both the beneficial
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=542 b=0 i=0 f=f5] and harmful effects of aspirin before considering aspirin for the primary pre-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=554 b=0 i=0 f=f5] vention of cardiovascular disease in low risk patients. This information should
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=566 b=0 i=0 f=f5] be conveyed in a clinically significant way - in terms of number-needed-to-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=578 b=0 i=0 f=f5] treat (and number-needed-to-harm) or absolute risk reduction rather than
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=590 b=0 i=0 f=f5] relative risk reduction.

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=165 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (b) Because it is known that the total amount (dose) of asbestos particles
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=100 y=177 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (fibres) inhaled (concentration×duration of exposure) is what determines
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=100 y=189 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the risk of asbestos-induced disease.

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f5] logical materials to consider are: blood, urine, hair, saliva, nail clippings,
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=225 b=0 i=0 f=f5] faeces, and possibly biopsy materials.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=237 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 9.4 You should start by collecting case histories, holding discussions with local

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 9.5 Information on deaths in previous years (without smog) and on the age-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=297 b=0 i=0 f=f5] specific causes of death would be helpful. Evidence from animal experiments
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=309 b=0 i=0 f=f5] might serve to document the effects of the smog (in fact, the live animals on
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=321 b=0 i=0 f=f5] display at London’s Smithfield Meat Market also suffered). The close time
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=333 b=0 i=0 f=f5] association of the smog and its pollutants with an increase in deaths is strong
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=345 b=0 i=0 f=f5] evidence for a causal relationship.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=357 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 9.6 The healthy worker effect refers to the low background morbidity and mor-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=369 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tality rates that are found in both exposed and unexposed groups in the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=381 b=0 i=0 f=f5] workplace. The reason is that, in order to be active in an occupation, people
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=393 b=0 i=0 f=f5] need to be reasonably healthy. Ill and disabled people are selectively excluded
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=405 b=0 i=0 f=f5] from the study groups. If a control group is chosen from the general popu-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=417 b=0 i=0 f=f5] lation, bias may be introduced because the group is inherently less healthy.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=429 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 9.7 Situations where: a. well defined geographic sub-area and census or other
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=441 b=0 i=0 f=f5] population data exist; b. the exposure of interest can be measured or modeled
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=453 b=0 i=0 f=f5] in the same geographic sub-areas; c. data on exposures and effects for each
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=465 b=0 i=0 f=f5] geographic sub-area can be assembled for appropriate time periods.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=65 y=477 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 9.8 Driving cars or motor cycles: seat belts, speed limits, alcohol limits, crash

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=501 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ucts, e.g. electrical products, child safety lids on jars and medicine containers.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=85 y=513 b=0 i=0 f=f5] Life jackets in boats, etc.

[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=117 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 10.1 Using the guiding principles of the Bangkok Charter towards the development
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=129 b=0 i=0 f=f5] of healthy public policy would include such actions as:
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=151 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Advocate: advocacy is required to ensure governments fulfil all the obli-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=182 y=163 b=0 i=0 f=f5] gations of the Framework Convention on Tobacco Control to prevent
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=182 y=174 b=0 i=0 f=f5] tobacco use in children.
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=186 b=0 i=0 f=f5] (cid:120) Invest: the resources to address the underlying determinants of tobacco
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=182 y=197 b=0 i=0 f=f5] use in children e.g., deprivation, poverty and alienation.

[L s=9.0 r=0.90 x=27 y=53 b=0 i=0 f=f6] 204 Annex
[L s=20.0 r=2.00 x=140 y=83 b=0 i=0 f=f9] Chapter 11
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=117 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 11.1 This was a well-designed and well-conducted randomized controlled trial on
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=129 b=0 i=0 f=f5] the use of aspirin in the primary prevention of cardiovascular mortality. The
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=141 b=0 i=0 f=f5] study was conducted on male American physicians who, it turned out, were
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=153 b=0 i=0 f=f5] very healthy. Out of a total of 261 000 physicians, 22 000 took part. The
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=165 b=0 i=0 f=f5] healthy state of the physicians meant that the study had less statistical power
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=177 b=0 i=0 f=f5] than originally planned. Extrapolating the results to other populations is dif-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=189 b=0 i=0 f=f5] ficult because of the exclusions that limited the study population to physi-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=201 b=0 i=0 f=f5] cians likely to comply and not to have adverse side-effects. These design
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=213 b=0 i=0 f=f5] features increased the likelihood of a high success rate. Confirmation of the
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=225 b=0 i=0 f=f5] benefits of aspirin is required from other studies. It is always necessary to
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=237 b=0 i=0 f=f5] balance benefits against risks (gastrointestinal side-effects, increased risk of
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=249 b=0 i=0 f=f5] bleeding, etc.).
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=150 y=261 b=0 i=0 f=f5] 11.2 Ecological evidence on asthma therapy is related to a suggested increase in
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=273 b=0 i=0 f=f5] asthma mortality. It would be difficult to agree with the conclusion. Infor-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=285 b=0 i=0 f=f5] mation is presented only on people dying with asthma; no information is
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=297 b=0 i=0 f=f5] provided on asthmatics not dying. This study is a case series; there are no
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=309 b=0 i=0 f=f5] controls. Such a study, however, points to the desirability of further investi-
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=321 b=0 i=0 f=f5] gation. In this case a more formal examination of asthma mortality trends
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=333 b=0 i=0 f=f5] has identified a new epidemic of asthma deaths; a particular drug contributed
[L s=10.0 r=1.00 x=170 y=345 b=0 i=0 f=f5] substantially to the epidemic.
